Purse-Cutting: The Four Scenarios of Sleeve and Tie
Commentary on four scenarios of purse-cutting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the four scenarios of purse-cutting (tareer) and determine when the hadd cutting applies based on the location of the hirs.

Core Idea
Cutting applies only when the purse is taken from inside the sleeve (hirs), not when the tie is outside exposing the money.

The commentary turns to a practical case: a purse-cutter (tareer) who tears a purse (surah) from a person's sleeve. The ruling depends on the configuration of the dirhams relative to the sleeve and the tie. We will break down the four scenarios and the principle that the hirs (sleeve) must be breached completely for cutting to apply.

Guided Path
1
Understand the hirs: the sleeve as a secure place
Recognize that the sleeve of a garment can serve as a hirs when it contains money. The hirs is breached only if the money is taken from inside the sleeve.
The sleeve is a form of hirs because it is a container that protects the money.
2
Identify the four scenarios based on placement of money and tie
The commentary describes two variables: (1) whether the dirhams are inside or outside the sleeve, and (2) whether the tie (ribat) is inside or outside. This yields four combinations.
The scenarios: (a) inside sleeve + tie outside, (b) inside sleeve + tie inside, (c) outside sleeve + tie outside, (d) outside sleeve + tie inside.
3
Apply the rule: cutting requires taking from within the hirs
For each scenario, determine if the thief's act (tearing or untying) results in taking the money from inside the sleeve (the hirs). If the money is taken from outside, no cutting.
The key is: when the money is inside the sleeve and the tie is inside, tearing the money takes it from inside the sleeve. If the tie is outside, tearing takes it from outside the sleeve.
4
Examine the ruling for each scenario
The commentary explicitly states that if the tie is outside, tearing the purse does not lead to cutting because the money is taken from outside the hirs. If the tie is inside, tearing leads to cutting. For untying (hall), similar logic: untying from outside tie does not require entry; untying from inside tie may still require entry.
The detailed ruling is given after laying out the four scenarios.
5
Appreciate the nuance: the usul scholars' generalization
The commentary notes that the usul scholars' statement that 'the purse-cutter's hand is cut like a thief' is not absolute but limited to cases where the hirs is breached. This highlights the need for precision.
This is a correction of a common oversimplification.

اليد.

(۳) قوله : فإن ؛ شروع في حكم كل من الصور الأربع بعد تمهيدها.
[٤] قوله : فلا قطع ؛ لأن الرباط في هذه الصورة من خارج ، فبالطر يؤخذ أخذ الدراهم من الخارج الظاهر ، ولا يوجد هتك الحرز بخلاف الصورة التي تليها ، فإن الرباط هناك من داخل فبالطر يتحقق الأخذ من الحرز وهتك ، وهو الكم.
The Complete Care upon the Explanation of al-Wiqayah. Or he breaks a house then inserts his hand into it and takes something, or he tears a purse protruding from another's sleeve. (Or he breaks a house then inserts his hand into it and takes something): This is according to us, and according to Abu Yusuf he is cut as in the case of the safe. We said: It is not a complete breach of the hirs, unlike the safe, because the only possible action is this. (Or) a purse protruding from another's sleeve. [1] His statement: 'Or he breaks... etc.' means: if he breaks a house and inserts his hand through that breach while he is outside the house and takes property, there is no cutting according to us. Similarly, if he enters through the breach or otherwise and takes the property and places it in the breach, then exits and takes it, he is not cut according to the correct view as in al-Fath and others. Because he has not actually removed the property secretly before his exit, and after his exit when he takes from the breach he did not take it from a hirs, so it is like when he inserts his hand from the breach and takes. This is unlike the case where he breaks and enters, takes something, and throws it onto the road in a way that he can see it, then exits and takes it—he is cut, because the throwing is a trick used by thieves, either due to difficulty of exiting with the goods or to enable defense and escape, so the entire act is considered one act. This is from Sharh al-Kanz by al-Zayla'i. [2] His statement: 'as in the safe': if he inserts his hand into the safe of a money-changer and takes something, he is cut by agreement. [3] His statement: 'We said... etc.': its summary is that cutting is only obligatory when there is complete breaching of the hirs, to avoid the doubt of non-existence, and that is by entering. And it is possible to consider that in our case, indeed it is customary, unlike taking from a safe, because the possible and customary action there is taking by inserting the hand without entry, so they differ. [4] His statement: 'Or he tears a purse... etc.': al-tarr (with fat-h on the ta' and tashdid on the ra') means cutting and splitting. (1) That is, complete breaching of the hirs is a condition because by it the crime is complete, and breaching is not complete in cases where entry is possible unless entry occurs, unlike taking from a safe because breaching it by entry is impossible, so taking by inserting the hand is considered complete breaching and he is cut. See al-Bada'i' (7:66). (2) al-tarr: cutting and splitting, from the verb dakhala: i.e., he splits or cuts a garment and takes money from it. See Talbat al-Talabah (p. 78). (3) Fath al-Qadir (5:388). (4) Tabyin al-Haqa'iq (3:223). Book of Theft, Chapter on What Requires Cutting and What Does Not. 305. This includes whether the purse is other than the sleeve, or the sleeve itself such that he places the dirhams in the sleeve and ties them from the outside, so the place of the dirhams—a part of the sleeve—remains outside what is in the sleeve; if he tears, cutting is not obligatory. And know that if the purse is the sleeve itself, there are four scenarios; the sleeve comes in four scenarios: because either he places the dirhams inside the sleeve with the tie outside, or he places them on the outside of the sleeve with the tie inside. In both cases, either he tears or unties the tie. If he tears and the tie is outside, no cutting, as mentioned before the division. And if he tears and the tie is inside, and that is by inserting his hand into the sleeve, cutting the place of the dirhams, so the dirhams come out with the end, then he takes the dirhams from the sleeve, he is cut for taking from the hirs. And al-surah (with damm on the sad and tashdid on the ra') is the cloth in which dirhams are tied. This issue indicates that what the usul scholars mentioned in the discussion of al-khafi—that the purse-cutter's hand is cut like a thief, unlike the grave robber—is not absolute, but is specific to some pictures of tearing. [1] His statement: 'from inside': meaning from the inner side of the sleeve that adheres to the skin of the hand. [2] His statement: 'or unties': al-hall (with fat-h on the ha' and tashdid on the lam) in Persian: kushadan. The upshot: in both cases—i.e., the case of the dirhams inside the sleeve and the tie outside, and the case of the dirhams outside the sleeve and the tie inside—it is either that the tareer tears that purse and takes the dirhams, or he unties the tie and takes them without tearing. [3] His statement: 'then': beginning to rule on each of the four scenarios after laying them out. [4] His statement: 'no cutting': because the tie in this scenario is outside, so by tearing the dirhams are taken from the external apparent location, and there is no breaching of the hirs, unlike the scenario that follows it, because the tie there is inside, so by tearing the taking from the hirs and its breaching is realized, which is the sleeve.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the matn of al-Wiqayah, dealing with the punishment for theft (qat' al-yad). It clarifies a key principle: for the hand to be cut, the thief must have completely breached the security (hirs) of the property. The first ruling discusses a person who makes a hole in a house but only inserts his hand without entering fully. According to the Hanafi school (our view), this does not result in cutting because he did not enter the hirs completely. Abu Yusuf disagrees and equates it to taking from a safe, where insertion of the hand alone suffices. The Hanafi response is that in a safe, entry is impossible, so insertion is considered complete breaching; but in a house, entry is possible and customary, so without entry the breach is incomplete and the penalty is waived to avoid doubt. The second ruling discusses a purse (surah) protruding from a sleeve. The commentary breaks down four scenarios based on the position of the dirhams relative to the sleeve (inside/outside) and the method of removal (tearing/untying). The principle remains: cutting is only required if the taking occurs from within the hirs. If the tie is outside and the purse is torn, the dirhams are taken from outside the hirs (the sleeve did not secure them) so no cutting. If the tie is inside, tearing the purse effectively takes the dirhams from within the sleeve (the hirs), so cutting applies. The commentary also notes that the usul scholars' general statement about the tareer (purse-cutter) being like a thief is not absolute but limited to cases where the hirs is breached. This nuanced approach reflects the Hanafi commitment to precise conditions for hadd punishments, avoiding application in cases of doubt.

Key Terms
طر (tarra): To tear or cut; specifically, the action of a purse-cutter (tareer).
صرة (surah): A cloth bag for dirhams, often kept in a sleeve.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A purse-cutter tears a purse that is tied to the outside of a person's sleeve. The money is inside the sleeve. Does the thief's hand get cut according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, because he took money from a sleeve.
B) No, because the tie is outside, so the money is taken from outside the hirs.
C) Yes, because he used a sharp instrument.
D) No, because the value is below nisab.

Key Takeaway
In purse-cutting, the hadd applies only when the purse is taken from inside the hirs (sleeve), which occurs when the tie is inside and the thief tears the purse.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties

Pickpocketing from a Sleeve: The Four Scenarios and Abu Yusuf's Disagreement
Elaboration of four scenarios of cutting/untying a purse in a sleeve and Abu Yusuf's dissent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each of the four pickpocketing scenarios from a sleeve and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute with Abu Yusuf regarding the definition of the hirz.

Core Idea
The hand is cut only when the thief breaches the hirz (sleeve interior); the tie's location and method of theft determine the ruling.

This lesson examines a nuanced issue in Hanafi hudud law: the pickpocket who steals from a sleeve. The ruling depends on two factors—the location of the purse's tie (inside or outside the sleeve) and the method of theft (cutting or untying). The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah lays out four scenarios and then records an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf argues that the sleeve is always a hirz, either by itself or by the owner's presence, so any removal warrants cutting. The majority holds that the hirz is the sleeve's interior, and only breaching that triggers the hadd. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the punishment of hand-cutting in theft cases.

Guided Path
1
Define the key terms
Understand the terms: ṭarrār (pickpocket), ṣurrah (purse), kumm (sleeve), ribāṭ (tie), ḥirz (safe place). These are the building blocks of the legal analysis.
The definitions are given in the key terms section below.
2
Diagram the four scenarios
Create a 2x2 table based on two variables: (1) tie location (inside sleeve vs. outside sleeve) and (2) action of the thief (cutting vs. untying). This yields four distinct cases.
The tie location determines whether the thief must enter the sleeve interior (breach the hirz) to access the purse.
3
Apply the ruling to each scenario
For each scenario, determine whether cutting the hand is wajib. The key principle: cutting is wajib only if the thief breaches the interior of the sleeve (the hirz).
Refer to the passage: cutting with tie outside — no breach; cutting with tie inside — breach; untying reverses the breach logic.
4
Identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Abu Yusuf argues that the sleeve is a hirz either by itself or because the owner is present, so any removal from the sleeve (in all four scenarios) should trigger cutting. Contrast this with the majority position.
The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) reject this, saying the owner's presence is incidental (walking or resting), not for guarding.
5
Evaluate the qiyas analogy
Analyze the analogy between the sleeve and a sack (jawaliq) on a camel. The owner relies on the sack as hirz, not on his own presence. This supports the majority view that the hirz is the container, not the person.
This qiyas illustrates the Hanafi method of using 'illa extraction (tanqih al-manat) to determine the effective cause of the ruling.

كتاب السرقة باب ما يقطع به وما لا يقطع هذا يشمل ما إذا كانت الصرة غير الكم، أو نفس الكم بأن جعل الدراهم في الكم وربطها من خارج، فبقي موضع الدراهم - وهو شيء من الكم – خارج ما في الكم، فإذا طر لا يجب القطع. واعلم أنه إذا كانت الصرة نفس الكم يأتي بأربع صور؛ لأنه إما أن جعل الدراهم في داخل الكم والرباط من خارج، أو جعلها على خارج الكم والرباط من داخل. وعلى التقديرين إما أن طر أو حل الرباط: فإن طر والرباط من خارج فلا قطع، وهو ما مر قبل التقسيم. وإن طر والرباط من داخل، وذلك بأن يدخل يده في الكم، فيقطع موضع الدراهم، فيخرج الدراهم مع الطرف، فأخذ الدراهم من الكم، فيقطع للأخذ من الحرز. والصرة: بضم الصاد المهملة، وتشديد الراء المهملة - : الخرقة التي تشد فيها الدراهم، وهذه المسألة ترشد إلى أن ما ذكره الأصوليون في بحث الخفي: إن الطرار تقطع يده كالسارق دون النباش ليس على إطلاقه، بل هو خاص ببعض صور الطر. [١]قوله: من داخل؛ أي من الجانب الداخل من الكم الذي يكون ملاصقاً ببشرة اليد. (۳) قوله: فإن؛ شروع في حكم كل من الصور الأربع بعد تمهيدها. [٤] قوله: فلا قطع؛ لأن الرباط في هذه الصورة من خارج، فبالطر يؤخذ أخذ الدراهم من الخارج الظاهر، ولا يوجد هتك الحرز بخلاف الصورة التي تليها، فإن الرباط هناك من داخل فبالطر يتحقق الأخذ من الحرز وهتك، وهو الكم. وإن حل الرباط، وهو خارج قطع؛ لأنه إذا حل الرباط يبقي الدراهم في الكم، فلا بد أن يدخل يده في الكم، فيأخذ الدراهم. وإن حل الرباط وهو داخل لا يقطع؛ لأنه أدخل يده في الكم فحل الرباط، فيبقى الدراهم خارج الكم، فأخذها من خارج. وعند أبي يوسف " الله يقطع في الوجوه كلها؛ لأن الكم حرز [١] قوله: وإن حل... الخ؛ قال في «الهداية»: «ولو كان مكان الطر حل الرباط، ثم الأخذ في الوجهين ينعكس الجواب لانعكاس العلة». (انتهى) قال في «النهاية»: يعني فيما إذا حل من خارج يقطع؛ لأنه لما حل الرباط الذي كان من خارج، وقعت الدراهم في الكم، فاحتاج في أخذ الدراهم إلى إدخال اليد في الكم، فيجب القطع، وأما إذا كان حل الرباط من داخل، فإنه لا يقطع؛ لأنه لما حل الرباط من خارج بقيت الدراهم خارج الكم، فلم يهتك الحرز، وهو نظير من نقب وأدخل يده فأخرج شيئا. [٢] قوله: وعند أبي يوسف... الخ؛ قال في «الهداية»: «عن أبي يوسف له: إنه يقطع على كل حال؛ لأنه محرز إما بالكم أو بصاحبه. قلنا: الحرز هو الكم؛ لأنه يعتمده، وإنما قصده قطع المسافة، أو الاستراحة فأشبه الجوالق». انتهى قال في «النهاية»: أي لأن صاحب المال يعتمد على الكم في حفظ المال لا قيام نفسه عند المال: كالبيت إذا أحرز به المال، فإنه محرز بالبيت دون الصاحب، وقصد صاحب الكم من وجوده عند المال ليس حفظ المال، بل لا يخلو من أحد أمرين: إما أن يكون هو في حالة المشي، أو في غيرها. فإن كان في حالة المشي، فمقصوده قطع المسافة لا حفظ المال. وإن كان في غير حالة المشي فمقصوده الاستراحة فقط. والمقصود هو المعتبر في هذا الباب، ألا ترى أن من شق الجوالق الذي على إبل، فأخذ الدراهم منها يقطع؛ لأن صاحب الجوالق اعتمد عليها حرزاً، ومن سرق الجوالق من الهداية»(٥: ٣٩١).
Book of Theft, Chapter on what necessitates cutting and what does not. This includes when the purse is not the sleeve, or the sleeve itself—when dirhams are placed in the sleeve and tied from outside, so the place of the dirhams—which is part of the sleeve—remains outside what is in the sleeve, then if he cuts, cutting is not obligatory. Know that when the purse is the sleeve itself, it comes in four scenarios because either he placed the dirhams inside the sleeve and the tie from outside, or he placed them on the outside of the sleeve and the tie from inside. In both scenarios, either he cuts or unties the tie: if he cuts and the tie is from outside, then no cutting, which is what passed before the division. If he cuts and the tie is from inside—that is by inserting his hand into the sleeve, cutting the place of the dirhams, extracting the dirhams with the edge—so he took the dirhams from the sleeve, he is cut for taking from the safe place. The purse: with damma on the sad and tashdid on the ra—is the cloth in which dirhams are tied. This issue indicates that what the usuliyyun mentioned in the discussion of the pickpocket—that his hand is cut like a thief, not a grave robber—is not unrestricted, but is specific to some forms of cutting. [1] His saying: from inside means from the inner side of the sleeve that adheres to the skin of the hand. [2] His saying: or untie; untying—with fatha on the ha and tashdid on the lam—in Persian: kushadan. The result: in both scenarios—that is, the scenario of the dirhams being inside the sleeve and the tie from outside, and the scenario of the dirhams being outside the sleeve and the tie from inside—it is not free: either the pickpocket cuts—i.e., slits and cuts that purse—and takes those dirhams, or he unties the tie and takes them without slitting. (3) His saying: for then is the beginning of the ruling for each of the four scenarios after their introduction. [4] His saying: so no cutting because the tie in this scenario is from outside, so by cutting the taking of the dirhams is from the apparent outside, and there is no violation of the safe place, unlike the scenario that follows—there the tie is from inside, so by cutting the taking from the safe place and violation is realized, which is the sleeve. And if he unties the tie while it is outside, he is cut because when he undoes the tie the dirhams remain in the sleeve, so he must insert his hand into the sleeve to take the dirhams. And if he unties the tie while it is inside, he is not cut because he inserted his hand into the sleeve and undid the tie, so the dirhams remain outside the sleeve, so he took them from outside. And according to Abu Yusuf, ' He is cut in all cases because the sleeve is a safe place.' [1] His saying: And if he unties... etc. He said in al-Hidayah: 'If instead of cutting there was untying of the tie, then taking in both scenarios, the answer is reversed due to the reversal of the cause.' (End) He said in al-Nihayah: meaning when he unties from outside, he is cut because when he undid the tie that was from outside, the dirhams fell into the sleeve, so he needed to insert his hand into the sleeve to take them, so cutting is obligatory. As for when the untying of the tie is from inside, he is not cut because when he undid the tie from outside, the dirhams remained outside the sleeve, so he did not violate the safe place, and it is analogous to one who breaches and inserts his hand and takes something out. [2] His saying: And according to Abu Yusuf... etc. He said in al-Hidayah: 'From Abu Yusuf: he is cut in every case because it is safeguarded either by the sleeve or by its owner. We said: the safe place is the sleeve because he relies on it, and his intention is only to cover distance or rest, so it resembles a sack.' End. He said in al-Nihayah: because the owner of the money relies on the sleeve for preserving the money, not on his own presence with the money—like a house if money is safeguarded therein, then it is safeguarded by the house, not by the owner. And the intention of the owner of the sleeve from his presence with the money is not preservation of the money; rather, it is not free from one of two things: either he is in the state of walking or otherwise. If he is in the state of walking, his intention is to cover distance, not to preserve the money. If he is in other than the state of walking, his intention is only rest. And the intention is what is considered in this chapter. Do you not see that the one who slits the sack that is on camels and takes the dirhams from it is cut because the owner of the sack relied on it as a safe place, and the one who steals the sack from al-Hidayah (5:391).
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on Al-Wiqayah addressing the hadd punishment for pickpocketing from a sleeve. It opens by stating the chapter on what necessitates cutting (of the hand) and what does not. The passage first clarifies that if the purse is not the sleeve itself (i.e., it is separate), and the thief cuts from outside, there is no cutting. Then it introduces four scenarios when the purse is the sleeve itself, based on two variables: (1) whether the dirhams are inside the sleeve with the tie outside, or outside the sleeve with the tie inside, and (2) whether the thief cuts the purse or unties it. 

For cutting: If the tie is outside, cutting is from outside the hirz (safe place), so no cutting. If the tie is inside, cutting breaches the sleeve interior, so cutting is required. 

For untying: The ruling reverses relative to cutting. If the tie is outside, untying causes the dirhams to fall inside the sleeve, so the thief must insert his hand (breaching the hirz), thus cutting is required. If the tie is inside, untying leaves the dirhams outside the sleeve, so no hirz breach, no cutting. 

The commentary then defines 'surah' (purse) and notes that the legal discussion of pickpockets is not absolute but specific to these scenarios. 

Then comes the intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that in all four scenarios the hand is cut because the sleeve is a hirz (either by itself or by the owner's presence). The Hanafi response, citing Al-Hidayah, refutes this: the hirz is the sleeve itself, not the owner. The owner's presence is incidental (for walking or rest), not for guarding. The analogy to a sack (jawaliq) on a camel is used—the sack is the hirz, not the camel or its owner. 

The passage thus establishes the nuanced Hanafi position that the hand is cut only when the thief actually breaches the interior of the sleeve (the hirz), not for external removal.

Key Terms
الطرّار (al-ṭarrār): The pickpocket who cuts or slits a purse to steal.
الصرّة (al-ṣurrah): A purse or piece of cloth in which dirhams are tied.
الكمّ (al-kumm): The sleeve of a garment.
الرباط (al-ribāṭ): The tie or string used to close the purse.
الحرز (al-ḥirz): A safe place or repository where valuables are kept.
الطرّ (al-ṭarr): Cutting or slitting the purse.
الحلّ (al-ḥall): Untying the knot of the purse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which scenario does the Hanafi school require cutting the hand of a pickpocket who steals from a sleeve?
A) When the thief cuts the purse from outside and the tie is outside
B) When the thief cuts the purse and the tie is inside the sleeve
C) When the thief unties the purse from inside and the tie is inside
D) When the thief unties the purse from outside and the tie is outside

Key Takeaway
The hand of a pickpocket is cut only when the theft breaches the sleeve's interior (the hirz); external cutting or untying from a certain side does not trigger the hadd.

Untying the Knot: Amputation and the Hiraz
Intra-Hanafi dispute on cutting hand when thief unties knot

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on amputation when a thief unties a knot, explain Abu Yusuf's dissent, and apply the rule to analogous scenarios.

Core Idea
Amputation depends on entering the hiraz, not merely untying the knot.

In theft cases, the hand is cut only if the thief breaches the hiraz (secure place). When a thief unties a knot in a sleeve, the location of his hand matters. This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, sharpening our understanding of what constitutes a hiraz.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: if the thief unties from outside, he is cut; if from inside (hand already in sleeve), he is not cut. The key is whether the hand enters the sleeve to take the dirhams.
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's dissent
Abu Yusuf holds that amputation applies in all cases because the sleeve is a hiraz, and additionally the owner's presence secures the money.
3
Follow Abu Yusuf's proof
Abu Yusuf argues that the money is secured either by the sleeve or by the owner's presence, so any breach triggers amputation.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: the hiraz is exclusively the sleeve; the owner's presence is incidental (walking or resting) and not for preservation. The analogy of the jualaq (sack) on a camel illustrates that a container is the hiraz, not the driver's presence.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the thief unties from inside, Abu Hanifa does not cut; Abu Yusuf does. The relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's.

(انتهى) قال في «النهاية»: يعني فيما إذا حَلّ من خارج يقطع ؛ لأنه لَمَّا حَلَّ الرباط الذي كان من خارج ، وقعت الدارهم في الكم، فاحتاج في أخذ الدراهم إلى إدخال اليد في الكم، فيجب القطع، وأما إذا كان حَلُ الرباط من داخل ، فإنّه لا يقطع ؛ لأنه لَمَّا حلَّ الرباط من خارج بقيت الدراهم خارج الكم، فلم يهتك الحرز، وهو نظير من نقب وأدخل يده فأخرج شيئا.

If he unties the knot while [his hand is] outside, he is cut; because when he untied the knot, the dirhams remained in the sleeve, so he necessarily inserted his hand into the sleeve and took the dirhams. If he unties the knot while inside, he is not cut; because he inserted his hand into the sleeve and then untied the knot, so the dirhams remained outside the sleeve, and he took them from outside. According to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, he is cut in all cases because the sleeve is a hiraz (secure place). [1] His statement: "And if he unties the knot..." etc. It is said in al-Hidayah: "If instead of cutting the pocket (tarr), he untied the knot, then taking it in the two cases – the answer is reversed due to the reversal of the causal factor." (end) He said in al-Nihayah: Meaning, when he unties from outside, he is cut; because when he untied the knot that was from outside, the dirhams fell into the sleeve, so he needed to insert his hand into the sleeve to take the dirhams, so amputation is obligatory. But if he untied the knot from inside, then he is not cut; because when he untied the knot from outside, the dirhams remained outside the sleeve, so he did not breach the hiraz. This is analogous to someone who breaches [a wall] and inserts his hand, then takes something. [2] His statement: "And according to Abu Yusuf..." etc. He said in al-Hidayah: "From Abu Yusuf: He is cut in all cases because it is secured (muhraz) either by the sleeve or by its owner. We say: The hiraz is the sleeve, because he (the owner) relies on it, and he only intended to cover distance or rest; so it resembles the jualaq (sack)." End. He said in al-Nihayah: That is because the owner of the sleeve relies on the sleeve for preserving the money, not on his own presence with the money – like a house, if money is secured in it, it is secured by the house, not by the owner. And the intention of the owner of the sleeve from his presence with the money is not preservation of the money; rather, it is nothing but one of two matters: either he is in the state of walking, or in other than that. If he is in the state of walking, his intention is to cover distance, not preserving the money. If he is in other than the state of walking, his intention is only rest. And intention is what is considered in this chapter. Do you not see that if someone rips the jualaq that is on a camel and takes the dirhams, he is cut, because the owner of the jualaq relied on it as a hiraz. But if someone steals the jualaq with what is in it, while the jualaq is on a camel, he is not cut, because the driver or leader only intends by his action to cover distance and drive, not preservation, so the jualaq did not become intended as a hiraz.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah begins by stating the ruling on a thief who unties the knot (halla al-ribat) instead of cutting the pocket or sleeve. The base ruling: if the thief unties from outside (i.e., his hand is outside the sleeve when he unties), the dirhams fall inside the sleeve, requiring him to insert his hand to take them; thus the hiraz (secure place) is breached and amputation is required. If he unties from inside (hand already inside the sleeve), the dirhams fall outside, so he takes them from outside without entering the hiraz, and he is not cut. Then Abu Yusuf's dissenting view is presented: he amputates in all cases because the sleeve is a hiraz, or alternatively because the owner's presence also secures it. The author rebuts, siding with Abu Hanifa: the hiraz is exclusively the sleeve, because the owner relies on the sleeve, not on his own presence. His presence is incidental (for walking or rest), not for preservation. The analogy to a sack (jualaq) on a camel is given: if someone cuts the sack and takes coins, amputation applies because the sack was the hiraz; but stealing the entire sack from the camel does not incur amputation because the driver's intention is not preservation. Thus, the sleeve alone is the hiraz, and Abu Yusuf's view is not upheld.

Key Terms
الكم (al-kumm): The sleeve of a garment, considered a hiraz (secure place) for money placed in it.
حرز (hirz): A secure place or container that by custom protects property from theft.
جوالق (juwalīq): A large sack used for carrying goods on camels.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He is cut because he breached the hiraz.
B) He is not cut because he did not enter the hiraz to take the dirhams.
C) He is cut according to Abu Hanifa but not according to Abu Yusuf.
D) He is cut because the sleeve is always a hiraz.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the hand is cut only if the thief enters the hiraz to take the property; untying a knot from inside the hiraz does not trigger amputation.

Theft from a Caravan: Conditions for Amputation
Ruling on amputation for theft from a caravan with conditions on owner's role

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which amputation applies for theft from a caravan and apply the Hanafi distinction between a guardian and a mere rider/driver.

Core Idea
A rider, leader, or driver does not make a caravan a hiraz; only a designated guardian or a sleeping person counts.

In this passage, the author of al-Hidayah clarifies when stealing a camel or load from a caravan triggers amputation. The key is whether the property was in a hiraz (protected place) – but what counts as protection? The text explains that the mere presence of a rider, driver, or leader is not enough, because they intend travel, not guarding. However, if the owner sleeps on the load or designates a specific guardian (hafiz), the thief is liable. A subtle dispute among Hanafi scholars about the shepherd is also resolved in the later view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is stealing a camel or load from a caravan (qitar). The ruling: amputation is wajib if the property was guarded (hiraz).
The baseline ruling is that theft from a hiraz requires amputation.
2
Locate the condition for hiraz
Hiraz exists if the owner (or his agent) guarded the item or slept on it. The rider, leader, and driver do not count because their intent is travel, not protection.
The term 'hafiz' means a person whose intention is to guard.
3
Trace the dalil
The proof is the hadith of Safwan ibn Umayyah who slept in the mosque with his cloak under his head; the Prophet ﷺ ordered amputation. This shows sleeping is a form of guarding.
The hadith is recorded by Malik in al-Muwatta.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Regarding a shepherd (ra'i) who is with the animals: some early scholars said he is a guardian, but many later muftis (mashayikh) follow al-Baqalli’s view that he is not, because he does not intend to guard against thieves.
The mufta bihi position is no amputation if the only 'guardian' is a shepherd.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The commentary states that the majority of later Hanafi scholars gave fatwa according to al-Baqalli, so that is the relied-upon view.
This reflects the fatawa mutakhkhirin authority level.

Or if he steals a camel from a caravan, or a camel, and the hand is cut if its owner guarded it, or slept on it (or he stole a camel from a caravan, or a load, and the hand is cut if its owner guarded it). Indeed, the leader, the driver, and the rider do not intend except covering distance, not preservation. Even if there is a guardian, the thief of the camel or the load is cut. (Or if he slept on it) – for sleeping on the load or near it is preservation for it.
[1] His statement: 'from a qitar' - with a kasra on the qaf - and the author of al-Durr al-Mukhtar erred in pronouncing it with a fatha; it refers to camels in a single line.
[2] His statement: 'or a load' (himl) – meaning the load on the camel or on other animals. It is said in Ghiyath al-Lughat: himl (with a fatha on the first letter, sukun on the second) means 'to lift' and also 'a burden carried on the neck', while with a kasra it means 'a load one weaves or places on the head'.
[3] His statement: 'Indeed, the leader... etc.' – this is the reason for not cutting in stealing a camel from the caravan or a load, and it refutes what might be imagined: why not cut even though there is a guardian? The rider on the camel, or the leader who leads the animal by taking the reins etc. in front of it, and the driver who drives from behind – they do not intend preservation.
[4] His statement: 'guardian' – meaning other than those mentioned. Even if the guardian is the shepherd, there is a difference of opinion: some said he is cut, and many mashayikh gave fatwa according to al-Baqalli's statement that he is not cut because the shepherd does not intend to guard the animals from thieves, unlike others. So in al-Nahr and others.
[0] His statement: 'or slept on it' – he indicated that sleeping on the load is sufficient for preservation. This is one of the cases where they said the sleeping person has the same ruling as the awake one. The author of al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir has enumerated them. Its origin is the hadith: Safwan ibn Umayyah, may Allah be pleased with him, slept in the mosque and placed his cloak under his head; a thief stole it and was brought to the Messenger of Allah, and he ordered that his hand be cut off. Recorded by Malik in al-Muwatta.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on theft (kitab al-sariqa) discusses the conditions for amputation when a thief steals a camel or a load from a caravan (qitar). The base ruling: if the owner guarded the camel or load, or slept on it, then the thief's hand is cut. The reason is that the leader, driver, or rider do not intend preservation but merely travel; thus, their mere presence does not constitute a hiraz. However, if there is a designated guardian (hafiz) distinct from the traveler, then theft incurs amputation. The commentary clarifies that the leader (qa'id) leads the animal from the front, the driver (sa'iq) drives from behind, and the rider (rakib) is on top; none of these are guards. A dispute is noted regarding the shepherd (ra'i): many later scholars (mutakhkhirin) follow al-Baqalli's view that the shepherd is not a guardian because he does not intend to guard against thieves, so amputation is not required. The passage then discusses the case of sleeping: sleeping on the load itself or near it suffices as preservation, because the sleeping person's ruling is that of an awake guardian. The proof is the hadith of Safwan ibn Umayyah, who slept in the mosque with his cloak under his head; a thief stole it, and the Prophet ﷺ ordered the thief's hand to be cut. This hadith demonstrates that sleeping does not negate the hiraz.

Key Terms
قطار (qiṭār): A line of camels tied together in a caravan.
حافظ (ḥāfiẓ): A guardian specifically assigned to protect property, as opposed to a mere rider/driver.
راعي (rā‘ī): A shepherd who tends to animals; in this context, there is ikhtilaf on whether he qualifies as a hafiz.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Whenever the owner is present, whether riding or driving.
B) Only if the owner is a designated guardian (hafiz) or sleeping on the load.
C) Only if the owner is the leader (qa'id) or driver (sa'iq).
D) Always, because the caravan itself is a hiraz.

Key Takeaway
Amputation for theft from a caravan depends on intentional guarding, not mere presence; sleeping on the load counts as guarding.

Theft from a Caravan: When Is Amputation Applied?
Ruling on theft of a camel or load from a caravan with conditions of preservation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for amputation when a camel or load is stolen from a caravan, and apply the concept of hizr to determine whether the thief's hand is cut.

Core Idea
A camel in a caravan is not a protected place unless the owner guards it or sleeps on the load.

In Hanafi fiqh, the hadd penalty for theft (qat' al-yad) requires that the stolen item be taken from a protected place (hizr). This ruling clarifies what constitutes a hizr for camels and loads in a caravan. The default is that animals in transit are not considered protected, but specific actions by the owner change that status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its hukm
The act is stealing a camel or load from a caravan. The hukm is that amputation is applied only if the item was in a hizr.
2
Understand the default ruling
The default is no amputation because the caravan's leader, driver, and rider are focused on travel, not guarding.
3
Recognize the exception
Amputation applies if the owner explicitly guards the camel/load or sleeps upon/near it.
4
Trace the dalil
The hadith of Safwan ibn Umayya proves that sleeping on an item makes it a hizr.
Hadith: Safwan slept in the mosque, his cloak was stolen, Prophet ordered amputation.
5
Contrast with other schools
Shafi'i considers the driver's presence sufficient; Hanafi distinguishes driver's intent is travel, not guarding.
Cross-madhhab difference.

أو سرق جملاً من قطار، أو حملاً، وقطع إن حفظه ربه، أو نام عليه (أو سرق جملاً من قطار، أو حِمْلاً ، وقطع إن حفظُهُ ربُّهُ) ؛ فإن القائد ، والسائق والراكب لا يقصدون إلا قطع المسافة دون الحفظ، حتى لو كان هناك حافظ قطع سارق الجمل والحمل ، (أو نام عليه ) ؛ فإن النوم على الحمل، أو بقرب منه حفظ له.

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling on theft of a camel or load from a caravan. The key condition for amputation is that the item must be in a protected place (hizr). A camel in a caravan is not considered protected because the leader, driver, and rider are only concerned with travel, not guarding. However, if the owner specifically guards the camel or load, or sleeps upon or near the load, then it becomes a hizr, and the thief's hand is cut off. Cutting the Load and Taking Something
Ruling on cutting open a load and stealing its contents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cutting open a load and stealing its contents and explain why the sack qualifies as a protected place (hizr).

Core Idea
Cutting a load to steal is like breaking a lock—amputation applies because the sack is a hizr.

In Hanafi law, the protection of property (hizr) is central to the application of the hadd penalty for theft. This lesson examines a specific case where a thief cuts open a loaded sack on an animal and takes its contents. The key insight is that the sack itself, by virtue of being tied and guarded, constitutes a hizr. Understanding this distinction helps clarify the boundaries of amputation in theft cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is cutting open a load (such as a sack on a camel) and taking something from inside. The ruling is that the thief's hand is cut off (amputation).
2
Recognize the legal basis: the sack is a hizr
The text states 'فإنّ الجوالق حرز' (the sack is a protected place). This means the sack itself serves as a physical barrier that secures its contents.
3
Distinguish from other scenarios
Compare with carrying off the whole sack or taking contents that have fallen out. In those cases, the hizr is not violated, so amputation does not apply.
The commentary clarifies that if the contents spill onto the ground and are then taken, no amputation applies.
4
Apply the condition to modern contexts
Identify which modern containers qualify as hizr: e.g., a locked suitcase, a sealed package, a store display case. The key is whether the container is customarily considered a safeguarding enclosure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو شق الحِمل وأخذ منه شيئاً (أو شق) الحمل وأخذ منه شيئاً ) ؛ فإنّ الجوالق " حرز.
Or if he cuts the load and takes something from it. So indeed the sack is a protected place.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This ruling clarifies that when a thief cuts open a load (e.g., a sack on an animal) and extracts its contents, his hand is cut off. The reason is that the sack itself serves as a hizr (protected container). By cutting it, the thief violates the protection. This is contrasted with stealing the entire sack, where the sack's protection is not violated in the same way. The commentary adds that if the contents fell out and then were taken from the ground, no amputation applies because they were no longer in the hizr.

Key Terms

جوالق (juwāliq): A large sack or bag used for storing goods on animals.

D) No amputation, because the sack belongs to a caravan.

Key Takeaway
Under Hanafi law, cutting open a load to steal its contents incurs the hadd amputation because the load itself is a protected enclosure (hizr).

Inserting Hand into a Hizr: Intent to Steal
Ruling on theft by inserting hand into container or clothing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft when a hand is inserted into a container or clothing and distinguish between taking and other actions.

Core Idea
Inserting hand into another’s hizr with intent to steal triggers amputation, even if nothing is taken.

The Hanafi school defines theft not only by the act of taking but also by the intrusion into a protected place (hizr). This passage clarifies that inserting the hand into a box, sleeve, or pocket with the intent to steal constitutes theft, provided other conditions are met. Understanding the role of intent is critical to applying hudud correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the physical act
The passage describes inserting the hand into another's box, sleeve, or pocket. These are all protected containers or clothing (hizr).
The act is the insertion, not necessarily removal of an item.
2
Determine the intent
The ruling hinges on the purpose: 'for taking' (stealing) versus other actions like untying a knot. The intent must be to steal.
Intent is inferred from context; a mere insertion without intent is not theft.
3
Recognize the hizr condition
The box, sleeve, or pocket is considered a hizr – a place where valuables are protected. Inserting into a hizr violates the protection.
This aligns with the general condition that the stolen item must be in a hizr.
4
Apply the ruling
If the insertion is for taking and other conditions (e.g., nisab, no doubt) are met, amputation is wajib.
Even if the hand is empty upon withdrawal, if intent was to steal, it may still be theft (subject to other conditions).

The Ruling on Theft from a Maqsurah
Ruling on theft from a maqsurah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that theft from a maqsurah (a room) incurs amputation because it is considered a hizr (secure place), and define what constitutes a maqsurah in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
A maqsurah is a secure enclosure; theft from it triggers the hadd of amputation.

In this lesson, we examine a brief but important ruling from the Hanafi text on hudud: theft from a maqsurah (a room) is treated like theft from any other secure enclosure (hizr). The term maqsurah, a Kufan dialect word, refers to a room or chamber within a house. The lesson clarifies why this qualifies as a hizr and thus leads to the application of amputation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that taking property from a maqsurah incurs amputation. This is the ruling (hukm) for theft of the minimum amount from such a secure place.
2
Define the key term: maqsurah
Understand that maqsurah is a Kufan term for a room, chamber, or enclosed space within a house. It is not an open area or a shared space.
Distinguish from the raised platform in a mosque (also called maqsurah) which is not relevant here.
3
Establish the legal link: maqsurah as a hizr
The commentary explains that a maqsurah is considered a hizr (secure place). In Hanafi hudud law, theft from a hizr is a condition for amputation. Therefore, a maqsurah qualifies.
Hizr can be a locked room or an enclosed space that is not accessible to the public without permission.
4
Conclusion and application
Therefore, if a thief meets all other conditions (e.g., nisab, sound mind), amputation (qat') becomes wajib for theft from a maqsurah.

Commentary

A maqsurah is a room or enclosed space within a house, typically used for storage or private use. The commentary in the original text (from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah) explains that this term is used in the Kufan language for rooms or apartments, and it is considered a hizr.

Conditions for Amputation in Theft: Breaching Various Safe Places
Explaining scenarios of theft that trigger amputation, including the distinction between taking and destroying.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the scenarios that constitute theft requiring amputation in Hanafi fiqh and distinguish between theft and destruction in the rule of hirz.

Core Idea
Amputation requires removal from a safe place AND taking possession; mere destruction triggers no hadd.

Theft (sariqa) leading to amputation (qat' al-yad) is a severe hadd punishment in Islamic law. The Hanafi school strictly defines theft as the secret taking of property from a secure place (hirz). This lesson explores various scenarios that satisfy or fail the condition of breaking the hirz and the crucial distinction between taking possession and merely destroying property. Understanding these nuances ensures the hadd is applied correctly and not extended to cases of mere damage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios in the passage
Read the passage and list the specific acts described: splitting a sack, inserting hand into box/sleeve/pocket, taking from private rooms in a shared building, and throwing property from a safe place.
Each scenario tests the concept of hirz in different contexts.
2
Define the Hanafi concept of hirz
From the scenarios, understand that hirz is context-dependent: a sack is hirz for its contents, a private room is hirz for its occupant if independent. The key is whether the property is in a place that by custom is considered secure.
Hirz is not a fixed physical enclosure; it varies with custom (urf).
3
Distinguish between taking and destroying
The passage highlights the scenario of throwing property into the road: Hanafi holds that if the thief does not take it afterward, no amputation; Shafi'i disagrees. Identify the core difference: the Hanafi requirement of actual possession (akhdh).
This is the key ikhtilaf in the passage.
4
Apply the rule to the maqasir scenario
For a building with independent private rooms, moving property between rooms or from a room to the courtyard breaks the hirz. Contrast this with a single-owner house where all rooms share the same hirz.
The test is whether the occupant has exclusive control and does not need the other areas for daily necessities.
5
Evaluate the practical consequence
Determine the ruling for each scenario: amputation wajib when the thief takes property from its hirz; if no taking, then the thief is a destroyer liable for compensation but no hadd.
This upholds the principle of dar' al-hudud (avert hadd punishments by doubts).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو شقّ الحِمل وأخذ منه شيئاً (أو شق) الحمل وأخذ منه شيئاً (١)؛ فإنّ الجوالق " حرز. (أو أدخل يده في صندوق غيره، أو كمه، أو جيبه)، المراد إدخال اليد في الكم؛ للأخذ لا لحل الرباط، كما مر. (أو أخرج من مَقْصُورة دار فيها مقاصير إلى صحنِها، أو سرق ربِّ مَقْصَورة من أخرى فيها، أو ألقى شيئاً من حرز في الطريق، ثم أخذه، أو حمله على حمار فساقه وأخرجه من الحرز) فيها مقاصير إلى صحنِها، أو سرق ربِّ مَقْصَورة من أخرى فيها. أراد موضعاً كمدرسة، أو نحوها فيها حجرات يسكن في كلِّ منها إنسان لا تعلق له بالحجرة التي يسكن فيها غيره، لا كالدار التي صاحبها واحد، وبيوتها مشغولة بمتاعه وخدامه، وبينهم انبساط. (أو ألقى شيئاً من حرز في الطريق، ثم أخذه، أو حمله على حمار فساقه وأخرجه من الحرز)، هذا عندنا. وأما عند الشَّافِعِي له يقطع سواء أخذه أو تركه في الطريق.
Or he split the load and took something from it - he split the load and took something from it, because the sack is a safe place. Or he inserted his hand into another's box, or his sleeve, or his pocket - the intended meaning is inserting the hand into the sleeve for taking, not for untying a knot, as has passed. Or he took out from a private room of a house that has private rooms leading to its courtyard, or the owner of one private room stole from another in it, or he threw something from a safe place into the road, then took it, or he loaded it on a donkey and drove it and removed it from the safe place - in it are private rooms leading to its courtyard, or the owner of one private room stole from another in it. He intended a place like a school or similar in which there are rooms, each of which is inhabited by a person who has no connection to the room in which another lives, unlike the house whose owner is one and its rooms are occupied by his belongings and servants, and among them there is familiarity. Or he threw something from a safe place into the road, then took it, or loaded it on a donkey and drove it and removed it from the safe place - this is according to us. And according to al-Shafi'i, amputation is required whether he took it or left it in the road.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, a commentary on al-Wiqaya, elaborates on various scenarios that constitute theft requiring amputation (qat' al-yad) in Hanafi fiqh. The key principle is that the thief must have taken the property from its safe place (hirz). The first scenario: splitting a load (himl) and taking part of its contents. Since the sack (juwalaq) itself is considered a hirz, splitting it breaches the safe place, making amputation wajib. The second scenario: inserting a hand into another's box, sleeve, or pocket. However, this only applies if the hand enters for the purpose of taking; if merely untying a knot, it does not count as theft. The third scenario involves private rooms (maqasir) within a larger building like a school. If each room is independent (with its own lock and the occupant does not need the courtyard for daily necessities), then taking an item from one room to the courtyard or to another room is considered removal from hirz, thus amputation is required. This contrasts with a single house owned by one person where rooms are shared; moving items within the house does not break hirz. The fourth scenario: throwing an item from a safe place into the road and then taking it. According to Abu Hanifa (and the Hanafi school), amputation is required only if the thief actually takes the item after throwing it. If he throws it and leaves it without taking, he is considered a destroyer (mudiya') rather than a thief, so no amputation. Al-Shafi'i, however, holds that amputation is required regardless of whether the thief takes the item or not, because the act of removing it from the safe place itself constitutes theft. The Hanafi position distinguishes between theft (taking) and mere destruction, aligning with the principle that hadd punishments are applied strictly. Throughout, the commentary emphasizes the importance of defining hirz according to custom and context.

Key Terms
حرز (hirz): A safe place where property is kept, such that breaking into it constitutes a breach of security for theft.
مقصورة (maqsura): A private room within a larger building, with its own lock and independent occupancy.
جوالق (juwalaq): A sack or container used for goods, considered a hirz.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on a thief who removes property from a safe place and then leaves it without taking possession (e.g., throws it into the road and does not take it)?
A) Amputation is required.
B) No amputation, he is considered a destroyer.
C) Amputation is discretionary.
D) Only tazir applies.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, amputation for theft requires both removal from a safe place (hirz) and actual taking (akhdh); without taking, it is destruction, not theft.

Theft from Independent Compartments: Separate Hirz
Explaining the concept of hirz for compartments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a compartment in a multi-unit building constitutes a separate safe place (hirz) and apply the Hanafi ruling on removal of property from such compartments.

Core Idea
Each independent dwelling is its own hirz; removal to a common area triggers hadd.

This lesson clarifies the Hanafi definition of a safe place (hirz) in multi-occupancy buildings. The key factor is whether the occupant of a compartment is independent of common areas for daily needs. If yes, moving property to the courtyard is removal from hirz, subjecting the thief to the hadd penalty. This distinction prevents unnecessary punishment when compartments are not independent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the building type
Determine whether the building is a single-family dwelling or a multi-unit residence (e.g., school, hostel).
2
Assess independence of compartments
Check if each compartment has its own door and lock, and if the occupant does not need the courtyard or other rooms for essential needs.
If the occupant uses the courtyard like a lane (for passage only), independence exists.
3
Apply the hirz classification
If compartments are independent, each is a separate hirz; if not, the entire building is one hirz.
For non-independent compartments, removal to the courtyard is not theft from hirz.
4
Determine the ruling on theft
If a thief removes property from one independent compartment to the courtyard or another compartment, the hadd applies because removal occurred from a hirz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

فيها مقاصير إلى صحنِها ، أو سرق ربِّ مَقْصَورة من أخرى فيها ، أو ألقى شيئاً من حرز في الطريق، ثم أخذه، أو حمله على حمار فساقه وأخرجه من الحرز فيها مقاصير إلى صحنِها (۱) ، أو سرق ربِّ مَقْصَورة من أخرى فيها)، أراد موضعاً كمدرسة ، أو نحوها فيها حجرات يسكن في كلِّ منها إنسان لا تعلق له بالحجرة التي يسكن فيها غيره، لا كالدار التي صاحبها واحد، وبيوتها مشغولة بمتاعه و خدامه، وبينهم انبساط.

(۲) ينظر: «المحلي» (٤) : (١٩٦) ، و«تحفة المحتاج» (٩) : (١٤٦) ، ونهاية المحتاج»(٩ : ٤٥٩)، وغيرها.

وعند زفـر له لا قطع في الإلقاء ولا في الحمل ، فإنَّ الإلقاء ليس بإخراج كمناولة من هو خارج ، وكما إذا ألقي ولم يأخذ.

Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on the hadd penalty for theft in the Hanafi school addresses two key issues: theft from a building with multiple compartments, and the act of throwing stolen goods out of a safe place (hirz) onto a public road or loading them onto an animal and driving it out.

For the first issue, the author explains that if a building has separate compartments (maqasir) that are independent residences—each with its own door, lock, and no need for the common courtyard—then removing property from one compartment to the courtyard or to another compartment constitutes removal from the safe place, thus requiring the cutting of the hand. This is because each compartment is a separate hirz. However, if the compartments are small and their inhabitants rely on the courtyard for daily needs, the entire building is considered one hirz, and removal within it (e.g., to the courtyard) does not trigger the hadd.

For the second issue, the Hanafi school holds that if a thief throws stolen goods from a safe place onto the road and then later goes out and retrieves them, the hadd penalty applies because the act of removal is complete when the thief takes possession after the goods leave the hirz. If he does not retrieve them, there is no cutting because he is merely a waster of property (mudi'), not a thief. Al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that the removal itself (throwing out) is sufficient for the hadd regardless of retrieval. The Hanafi response is that the thief's hand has not actually taken possession until after retrieval; throwing without retrieval is akin to destroying the property.

Zufar (Zufr) holds that there is no cutting in either the throwing or the loading onto an animal, because the act of removal is not directly attributed to the thief—throwing is not removal by his hand (like handing over to an outsider), and loading involves the animal's movement. The Hanafi response distinguishes: if no other hand intervenes, the thrown goods remain under the thief's control until he picks them up, completing the theft. For the animal, driving it is an act of the thief, so the movement is attributed to him; if the animal went out on its own, no cutting would apply.

The passage thus clarifies the Hanafi position through detailed casuistry and responses to opposing views.

Key Terms
حرز (hirz): A safe place where property is secured, such that unauthorized removal constitutes theft. In this lesson, each independent compartment is a separate hirz.
مقصورة (maqsura): A compartment or room within a larger building that serves as an independent dwelling, with its own lock and no need for common areas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The compartment has a door.
B) The occupant does not need the courtyard or other rooms for essential daily needs.
C) The building is a school.
D) The property inside is locked.

Key Takeaway
An independent compartment is a separate hirz; removal from it to a common area triggers the hadd penalty for theft.

Throwing Goods onto the Road and Loading onto an Animal
Explaining Hanafi rulings on throwing and loading, with disputes from Shafi'i and Zufar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when throwing stolen goods onto a road or loading them onto an animal constitutes theft triggering the hadd, and explain the disagreement with Shafi'i and Zufar.

Core Idea
Thief who throws goods out of hirz must retrieve them for hadd; loading onto animal requires driving it out.

This lesson examines two scenarios: a thief throws stolen property from a safe place onto the road, then later retrieves it; or loads it onto an animal and drives it out. The Hanafi school distinguishes between these and the act of mere throwing without retrieval. The Shafi'i and Zufarhold different views, leading to practical differences in implementation of the hadd penalty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position on throwing
Hanafi: cutting applies only if the thief retrieves the goods after throwing them out. If he leaves them, he is a waster, not a thief.
The act of theft is completed by taking possession after removal from hirz.
2
Compare with Shafi'i position
Shafi'i: cutting applies regardless of retrieval, because the goods have left the hirz.
The disagreement is on the necessity of subsequent taking.
3
Understand the Hanafi response to Shafi'i
Hanafi: if the thief does not take, his hand never possessed the goods after removal; the hadd is only for taking from hirz.
4
Analyze the loading case
Hanafi: if the thief loads goods onto an animal and drives it out, cutting applies because the movement is attributed to him.
If the animal goes out on its own, no cutting.
5
Examine Zufar's objection and Hanafi response
Zufar: no cutting in either case because throwing is not removal (like handing over) and loading involves animal's action. Hanafi: if no other hand intervenes, thrown goods remain under thief's control; driving animal is his act.
The principle: If no actual hand (other than the thief's) intervenes, the property is in his constructive possession until he retrieves it.

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on the hadd penalty for theft in the Hanafi school addresses two key issues: theft from a building with multiple compartments, and the act of throwing stolen goods out of a safe place (hirz) onto a public road or loading them onto an animal and driving it out.

For the first issue, the author explains that if a building has separate compartments (maqasir) that are independent residences—each with its own door, lock, and no need for the common courtyard—then removing property from one compartment to the courtyard or to another compartment constitutes removal from the safe place, thus requiring the cutting of the hand. This is because each compartment is a separate hirz. However, if the compartments are small and their inhabitants rely on the courtyard for daily needs, the entire building is considered one hirz, and removal within it (e.g., to the courtyard) does not trigger the hadd.

For the second issue, the Hanafi school holds that if a thief throws stolen goods from a safe place onto the road and then later goes out and retrieves them, the hadd penalty applies because the act of removal is complete when the thief takes possession after the goods leave the hirz. If he does not retrieve them, there is no cutting because he is merely a waster of property (mudi'), not a thief. Al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that the removal itself (throwing out) is sufficient for the hadd regardless of retrieval. The Hanafi response is that the thief's hand has not actually taken possession until after retrieval; throwing without retrieval is akin to destroying the property.

Zufar (Zufr) holds that there is no cutting in either the throwing or the loading onto an animal, because the act of removal is not directly attributed to the thief—throwing is not removal by his hand (like handing over to an outsider), and loading involves the animal's movement. The Hanafi response distinguishes: if no other hand intervenes, the thrown goods remain under the thief's control until he picks them up, completing the theft. For the animal, driving it is an act of the thief, so the movement is attributed to him; if the animal went out on its own, no cutting would apply.

The passage thus clarifies the Hanafi position through detailed casuistry and responses to opposing views.

Key Terms
حرز (hirz): A safe place where property is secured. In this lesson, the hirz is the location from which the goods are thrown or loaded.
مضيع (mudi'): A waster or destroyer of property, as opposed to a thief. One who throws goods and does not retrieve is a mudi', not a sariq.
إخراج (ikhraj): Removal from the safe place. In theft, ikhraj is necessary but not sufficient for hadd; taking must follow.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, in the case of theft by throwing goods onto the road, when does the hadd of cutting apply?
A) Immediately upon throwing, regardless of retrieval.
B) Only if the thief later retrieves the goods.
C) Never, because throwing is not considered removal.
D) Only if the thief throws the goods into a courtyard.

Key Takeaway
For hadd to apply, the thief must complete the theft by taking possession after removal (throwing) or by personally driving the animal (loading).

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate

Dispute on Cutting for Throwing and Carrying
Intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute on cutting for ilqa' and haml

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on cutting in cases of ilqa' and haml, and explain why the intervention of another hand negates the cutting penalty.

Core Idea
Ilqa' followed by taking completes removal; haml is attributed to the thief – cutting applies unless another hand intervenes.

In this passage, we encounter an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa (along with the Sahibayn) and Imam Zufar regarding the application of the hadd penalty for theft in two specific scenarios: when the thief throws goods out of storage and later retrieves them (ilqa'), and when he loads goods onto an animal which carries them out (haml). The passage also addresses the Shafi'i perspective. By carefully analyzing the reasoning, we see how the Hanafi school attributes removal to the thief through the concept of constructive possession and the attribution of an animal's movement to its driver.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage discusses two methods of removing stolen goods: ilqa' (throwing) and haml (loading onto an animal). Identify each and note that both are disputed.
Ilqa' involves throwing out and later taking; haml involves the animal carrying out.
2
State Zufar's objection
Zufar argues that cutting does not apply in either case because throwing is not removal, and carrying by an animal is not the thief's act.
Zufar likens ilqa' to handing (munawalah) where no cutting applies.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school distinguishes ilqa' from munawalah: in ilqa', no other hand intervenes, so the goods remain in the thief's constructive possession; taking after removal completes the theft. For haml, the movement of the animal is attributed to the thief because he drives it.
The response also addresses the Shafi'i view implicitly.
4
Distinguish the boundary cases
Note the two exceptions: if the thief throws and does not take, it is waste (no theft); if he hands to an accomplice (munawalah), another hand intervenes and cutting drops for the original thief.
Also; if the animal exits on its own, no cutting because the animal has choice.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under the Hanafi position, cutting applies in these cases; under Zufar's view, it does not. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) is the Hanafi position.
This affects actual legal judgments in hadd cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند زفر له لا قطع في الإلقاء ولا في الحمل ، فإنَّ الإلقاء ليس بإخراج كمناولة من هو خارج ، وكما إذا ألقي ولم يأخذ. قلنا : إذا لم يطرأ عليه يد حقيقة كان في حكم يده ، فَتَمَّ بالأخذ بعد الخروج ، بخلاف مسألتي المناولة وعدم الأخذ. وفي مسألة الحمل سير الدابة يضاف إليه . قوله : لا قطع في الإلقاء ولا في الحمل ; أما عدم القطع في الإلقاء ; فلان الإلقاء ليس بإخراج ; ولهذا لا يقطعُ إذا ألقاه في الطريق وذهب ولم يأخذه، ولا إذا ناول الخارج على ما مر تفصيله، وأما عدم القطعُ في الحمل ; فلأنه لم يوجد منه الإخراج، بل من الدابة. قوله : قلنا... الخ ; جواب عن قول زفر الله بحيث يتضمن الجواب عن قول الشافعي ، والحاصل أنه فرَّقَ بين هذه المسألة وبين مسألة المناولة، فإن طرأ ان اليد الأخرى هنا أبطل نسبة الأخذ إليه، فأسقط القطع بخلاف ما نحن فيه، فإنه وإن ألقاه في الطريق، لكنّه لم تطرأ يد أخرى عليه، فإذا أخذه تم منه فعل السرقة ، بخلاف ما إذا تركه، ولم يأخذه فإنه تضييع، وأما مسألة الحمل فسير الدابة يضاف إليه فيكون هو الآخذ.
And according to Zufar, there is no cutting in throwing nor in carrying, because throwing is not removal like handing to someone outside, and like when he throws and does not take. We said: If no other hand actually intervenes, it is considered his hand, so [the theft] is completed by taking after removal, contrary to the two cases of handing and not taking. And in the case of carrying, the movement of the animal is attributed to him. He said: 'No cutting in throwing nor in carrying' — as for no cutting in throwing, because throwing is not removal; hence he is not cut if he throws it in the road and leaves and does not take it, nor if he hands to an outsider as previously detailed. As for no cutting in carrying, because removal did not occur from him, but from the animal. He said: 'We said...' is a response to Zufar's statement, such that it includes a response to al-Shafi'i's statement. The upshot is that he distinguished between this case and the case of handing: here the intervention of another hand invalidates the attribution of taking to him, so cutting is dropped, contrary to our case. Here, even if he threw it in the road, no other hand intervened, so when he takes it, the act of theft is completed from him, contrary to if he leaves it and does not take it, then it is waste. As for the case of carrying, the movement of the animal is attributed to him, so he is the taker.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (with Zufar) and also addresses the Shafi'i position on the cutting of a thief who throws goods or carries them on an animal. The base text states Zufar's view: no cutting in throwing (ilqa') or carrying (haml) because throwing is not an act of removal (ikhraj) analogous to handing to someone outside the storage, and if the thief throws and does not pick up, removal is incomplete. Carrying by an animal is attributed to the animal, not the thief. The commentator (the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah) then presents the Hanafi response: 'We said...' The argument is that if no other hand actually intervenes (as in throwing and then picking up), the thrown goods remain under the thief's constructive possession (fi hukm yadihi), so when he picks them up after removal, the theft is complete. This is distinguished from the case of handing (munawalah) where another hand (the recipient) intervenes, breaking the attribution of taking to the original thief. Similarly, if he throws and does not take, it is waste (tadyi') and not theft. For carrying on an animal, the animal's movement is attributed to the thief because he drives it, so he is considered the agent of removal. The footnote explains that if the donkey exits on its own, the thief is not cut because the animal has choice. Thus, the relied-upon Hanafi position is that cutting applies in these cases, contrary to Zufar.

Key Terms
إلقاء (ilqa'): The act of the thief throwing stolen goods out of the storage place.
حمل (haml): The act of loading stolen goods onto an animal which then carries them out.
مناولة (munawalah): Handing the stolen goods to someone outside the storage, which involves another hand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a thief subject to cutting if he throws goods out of storage and then picks them up?
A) Because throwing alone constitutes removal
B) Because no other hand intervened, so the goods remained in his constructive possession
C) Because picking up is considered stealing even without removal
D) Because he intended to steal from the beginning

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ilqa' (throwing) followed by taking completes removal for theft, and haml (carrying on an animal) is attributed to the thief, so the hadd penalty applies in both cases.

Manner of Cutting the Thief's Hand: Proofs for Right Hand and Wrist
Ruling with proof on cutting the right hand from wrist and cauterizing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the manner of cutting the thief's hand and explain the Quranic and Sunnah evidence for specifying the right hand and the wrist.

Core Idea
The thief's right hand is cut from the wrist — punishment uses the minimal certain meaning of 'yad'.

The Quran commands cutting the hand of the thief, but how much of the hand? The Hanafi school specifies the right hand from the wrist. This precision is based on a combination of Quran, a renowned variant reading, and prophetic practice. Understanding how these proofs interact reveals the principles of Hanafi legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is executing the hadd punishment for theft. The ruling: the right hand is cut from the wrist and cauterized.
This is a physical punishment to be carried out by the ruler (fard kifayah).
2
Locate the primary dalil
The Quranic verse (5:38) provides the general obligation to cut the hand. The imperative 'faqta'u' indicates obligation (wujub).
The verse is qati' al-thubut but zanni al-dalalah regarding details.
3
Analyze the specification of the right hand
The general term 'aydiyahuma' (their hands) is specified by the well-known reading of Ibn Mas'ud ('aymanahuma' = their right hands) and by multiple hadith. This is a valid takhsis (restriction) of a general Quranic command by a mashhur qira'ah and sunnah.
Hanafi usul allows such takhsis; the objection that this abrogates the Quran by solitary reports is rejected because the qira'ah is mashhur, not solitary.
4
Determine the place of cutting
The term 'yad' (hand) is ambiguous in Arabic: it can mean fingers to elbow (wudu verse) or fingers to armpit, or fingers to wrist. In hudud punishments, the minimal certain meaning (wrist) is taken based on the principle that punishments require certainty.
This is supported by the Prophet's action of cutting from the joint (mifsal) and the practice of Abu Bakr and Umar.
5
Note the post-cut procedure
The cut hand is cauterized (hasm) with a heated iron to stop bleeding, preventing death. This is not part of the punishment but a medical necessity.
Cauterization is recommended to preserve life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل كيفية القطع، وإثباته يقطع يمين السارق من زنده وتحسم قوله : يقطع يمين السارق الخ ; أما القطعُ فثابت بنص قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَالسَّارِقُ وَالسَّارِقَةُ فَاقْطَعُوا أَيْدِيَهُمَا جَزَاءً بِمَا كَسَبَا نَكَلًا مِنَ اللَّهِ ﴾ . وأما تخصيص اليمين فلأحاديث وردت بذلك ولقراءة عبد الله بن مسعود رضي الله عنه : فاقطعوا أيمانهما»، وهي قراءة مشهورة، أخرجها البيهقي وغيره جازت بها الزيادة على الكتاب، وتقييد إطلاقه، فاندفع ما أورد أن هذا نسخ إطلاق الكتاب بأخبار الآحاد ، وهو لا يجوز عندنا. قوله : من زنده ; الزند - بالفتح - مفصل طرف الذراع في الكف واليد ، وإن كانت مشتملة من الكف إلى الإبط لكن لما كان قد تطلق على المجموع، وقد تطلق من الأصابع إلى المرافق كما في آية الوضوء، وقد تطلق من الأصابع إلى الزند، وكان هذا الأقل متيقناً أخذ به، فإنّ العقوبات إنّما يؤخذ فيها بالمتيقن. وقد تأيد ذلك بفعل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : إنه قطع يد السارق من المفصل، أخرجه ابنُ أبي شَيْبَةَ في «مصنِّفه»، وابنُ عَدي في «الكامل»، والدارقطني في «سننه». ولأبي الشيخ في كتاب الحدود عن ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما : «إن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وأبا بكر وعمر رضي الله عنهم كانوا يقطعون السارق من المفصل». قوله : وتحسم ; أي تلك اليد المقطوعة ، والحسم الكي بحديدة محماة ، وقيل : الكي.
Chapter on the manner of cutting and its proof. The right hand of the thief is cut from his wrist and cauterized. He said: 'The right hand of the thief is cut' etc. As for cutting, it is established by the text of His saying the Exalted: 'And the thief, male and female, cut off their hands as a recompense for what they earned, a punishment from Allah.' As for specifying the right hand, it is due to traditions that came with that and the reading of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him): 'So cut off their right hands' — a well-known reading, narrated by al-Bayhaqi and others, by which addition to the Book and restriction of its absolute [meaning] is permitted. Thus the objection that this is abrogation of the absolute [text] of the Book by solitary reports is repelled, for this is not permissible with us. He said: 'from his wrist' — al-zand (with fatha) is the joint at the end of the forearm in the palm and hand. While [the hand] may refer to the whole from palm to armpit, it may also refer to the fingers to the elbow as in the verse of wudu, and [also] from the fingers to the wrist. Since this lesser [extent] is certain, it is taken; for punishments are taken with certainty. This is supported by the act of the Prophet (peace be upon him): he cut the thief's hand from the joint, reported by Ibn Abi Shayba in his Musannaf, Ibn Adi in al-Kamil, and al-Daraqutni in his Sunan. And by Abu al-Shaykh in the Book of Hudud from Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with both): 'The Prophet (peace be upon him), Abu Bakr, and Umar (may Allah be pleased with them) used to cut the thief from the joint.' He said: 'and is cauterized' meaning that cut hand, and hasm is cauterization with a heated iron; it is said: cauterization.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter on the manner of cutting the thief's hand, providing both the ruling and its proofs. The base text states: 'The right hand of the thief is cut from his wrist and cauterized.' The commentator then expands with evidence from Quran and Sunnah. The cutting itself is proven by the verse from Surah al-Ma'idah (5:38). The specification of the right hand is not explicitly mentioned in the general Quranic text, but is established by the famous reading of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud ('so cut off their right hands') and by multiple hadith narrations. The commentator argues that such a reading and accompanying sunnah are permissible additions to and restrictions of the absolute Quranic command, contrary to the objection that solitary hadith (akhbar ahad) cannot abrogate or restrict the Quran. In Hanafi usul, a well-known reading (qira'ah mashhurah) can serve as evidence and can restrict the general (takhsis al-kitab bi al-khabar al-wahid) under certain conditions. Next, the place of cutting: 'from his wrist' (zand), which is the joint of the forearm at the palm. The term 'hand' (yad) is ambiguous in Arabic, ranging from fingers to elbow (as in wudu) or to armpit. But for punishments, the minimal certain interpretation (the wrist) is taken. This is supported by the Prophet's practice of cutting from the joint (mifsal). Literal evidence from Ibn Umar confirms the practice of the Prophet and the first two caliphs. Finally, 'cauterized' (hasm) means the cut hand is cauterized with a heated iron to stop bleeding, a practice to prevent death from blood loss.

Key Terms
زند (zand): The wrist joint at the end of the forearm, site of cutting for theft according to Hanafi school.
حسم (hasm): Cauterization of the cut hand with a heated iron to stop bleeding.
قراءة مشهورة (qira'ah mashhurah): A well-known variant reading of the Quran (such as Ibn Mas'ud's reading 'aymanahuma'), which can be used as evidence to restrict or clarify the general text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the Quran explicitly says 'from the wrist'
B) Because the term 'hand' (yad) can mean wrist, and punishments are decided by the minimal certain interpretation
C) Because all companions agreed on the wrist
D) Because the Prophet only cut from the wrist for minor thefts

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school cuts the right hand from the wrist based on Quran, qira'ah mashhurah, and prophetic practice, taking the minimum certain meaning of 'yad'.
