Amputation Sequence for Theft: Hanafi Rulings and Proofs
Ruling on the sequence of amputations and imprisonment for theft

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi sequence of punishments for theft and explain the proof for each stage.

Core Idea
A thief's right hand then left foot are cut; a third offence means imprisonment, not further amputation.

This passage from the chapter on hadd amputation lays out the Hanafi sequence of punishments for theft: from the first offense to the third. It grounds each stage in specific proofs from the Quran, hadith, and the practice of Imam Ali. Understanding this ruling reveals how the Hanafi school balances scriptural commands with mercy and practicality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling Structure
The passage presents a sequence: first cut the right hand, then the left foot if repeated, then imprison if repeated a third time. Identify each stage and its outcome.
This is the mufta bihi position of the Hanafi school.
2
Examine the Primary Proof for Amputation
Analyze how Quran 5:38 is used as evidence for the obligation. Note the imperative 'faqta'u' and how it establishes the hadd.
The text also uses a variant reading (Ibn Mas'ud) to specify the right hand.
3
Determine the Point and Method of Cutting
Look at why the wrist (zand) is chosen as the amputation point. The text argues it is the minimum certain understanding of 'hand' in punishments. Also note the order for cauterization (hasm) and its evidence.
The Prophet's practice and the practice of the caliphs support the wrist.
4
Analyze the Dispute on the Third Offence
Compare the Hanafi position (imprisonment only) with the Shafi'i position (further amputations). Examine the proofs cited: the practice of Ali vs. a general hadith. Understand the Hanafi response and the principle of mercy.
The dispute is a legitimate ikhtilaf; the Hanafi school follows Ali's precedent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل كيفية القطع، وإثباته
يقطع يمين السارق من زنده وتحسم
ثم رجله اليسرى إن عاد، فإن عاد ثالثاً لا ويسجن حتى يتوب
أما القطع فثابت بنص قوله تعالى: ﴿وَالسَّارِقُ وَالسَّارِقَةُ فَاقْطَعُوا أَيْدِيَهُمَا﴾. وأما تخصيص اليمين فلأحاديث وردت بذلك ولقراءة عبد الله بن مسعود: «فاقطعوا أيمانهما»، وهي قراءة مشهورة، أخرجها البيهقي وغيره جازت بها الزيادة على الكتاب، وتقييد إطلاقه.
وأما قوله: من زنده؛ الزند مفصل طرف الذراع في الكف واليد، وإن كانت اليد مشتملة من الكف إلى الإبط لكن لما كان قد تطلق على المجموع، وقد تطلق من الأصابع إلى المرافق كما في آية الوضوء، وقد تطلق من الأصابع إلى الزند، وكان هذا الأقل متيقناً أخذ به، فإن العقوبات إنما يؤخذ فيها بالمتيقن. وقد تأيد ذلك بفعل النبي ﷺ: إنه قطع يد السارق من المفصل، أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة وابن عدي والدارقطني. ولأبي الشيخ عن ابن عمر: «إن النبي ﷺ وأبا بكر وعمر كانوا يقطعون السارق من المفصل».
وقوله: وتحسم؛ أي تلك اليد المقطوعة، والحسم الكي بحديدة محماة، أو الكي بزيت مغلي ونحوه؛ وهذا لئلا يسيل دمه فيؤدي إلى التلف، وقد أمر به النبي ﷺ، أخرجه الحاكم وصححه، والدارقطني وأبو داود.
ثم رجله اليسرى إن عاد؛ الأصل في هذا الباب ما أخرجه محمد في «كتاب الآثار» بسند جيد عن علي قال: «إذا سرق السارق قطعت يده اليمنى، فإن عاد قطعت رجله اليسرى، فإن عاد ضمنه السجن حتى يحدث خيراً، إني استحيي من الله أن أدعه ليس له يد يأكل بها ويستنج بها ورجل يمشي عليها». وأخرج عبد الرزاق عن الشعبي: كان علي لا يقطع إلا اليد والرجل، وإن سرق بعد ذلك سجنه، ويقول: إنّي لاستحيي من الله أن لا أدع له يداً يأكل بها ويستنج. وفي رواية ابن أبي شيبة: كان علي لا يزيد على أن يقطع السارق يداً ورجلاً، فإذا أتي به بعد ذلك، قال: إنّي لاستحي من الله أن أدعه لا يتطهر لصلاته، ولكن احبسوه.
وعند الشافعي له يقطع يده اليسرى، ثم رجله اليمنى؛ لقوله: «من سرق فاقطعوه، فإن عاد فاقطعوه، فإن عاد فاقطعوه».
وأما السجن فروايتان: إما السجن فقط، وإما مع التعزير عند بعض مشايخنا.
Chapter: The manner of amputation and its proof. The right hand of the thief is cut from his wrist and cauterized. Then his left foot if he repeats. If he repeats a third time, he is not (cut) but imprisoned until he repents. As for amputation, it is established by the text of His statement: 'As for the thief, male and female, cut off their hands.' As for specifying the right hand, it is due to hadiths transmitted about that and the reading of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud: 'cut off their right hands,' which is a well-known reading, transmitted by al-Bayhaqi and others; by it an addition to the Book is permitted, and its absolute application is restricted. And as for His statement: 'from his wrist'; the wrist is the joint of the end of the forearm at the palm and hand. Although 'hand' may include from the palm to the armpit, since it is sometimes used for the whole, and sometimes for the fingers to the elbows as in the verse of wudu, and sometimes for the fingers to the wrist, and this least is certain, so it is adopted, for punishments are taken by that which is certain. This is supported by the action of the Prophet: he cut the thief's hand from the joint, transmitted by Ibn Abi Shayba, Ibn Adi, and al-Daraqutni. And from Abu al-Shaykh from Ibn Umar: 'The Prophet, Abu Bakr, and Umar used to cut the thief from the joint.' And his statement: 'and cauterize it'; that is, that cut hand, and cauterization is branding with a heated iron, or branding with boiling oil and the like; this is so that his blood does not flow and lead to destruction, and the Prophet ordered it, transmitted by al-Hakim who declared it authentic, and al-Daraqutni and Abu Dawud. Then his left foot if he repeats; the basis for this chapter is what Muhammad transmitted in 'Kitab al-Athar' with a good chain from Ali who said: 'If the thief steals, his right hand is cut; if he repeats, his left foot is cut; if he repeats, he is imprisoned until he does good; I am ashamed before God to leave him without a hand to eat with and clean himself with, and a foot to walk on.' And Abd al-Razzaq transmitted from al-Sha'bi: Ali used not to cut except the hand and foot, and if he stole after that, he imprisoned him and said: 'Indeed I am ashamed before God to leave him with a hand to eat and clean himself with.' And in a narration of Ibn Abi Shayba: Ali did not exceed cutting the thief's hand and foot; when he was brought to him after that, he said: 'I am ashamed before God to leave him unable to purify himself for his prayer, but imprison him.' And according to al-Shafi'i, his left hand is cut, then his right foot; due to his saying: 'Whoever steals, cut him; if he repeats, cut him; if he repeats, cut him.' As for imprisonment, there are two narrations: either imprisonment only, or with ta'zir according to some of our scholars.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter on the manner and proof of amputation for theft. It states the Hanafi position on the sequence of amputations: the first offense warrants cutting the right hand from the wrist and cauterizing it. The proof for amputation itself is from the Quranic verse 'cut off their hands'. The specification of the right hand is based on hadith and the reading of Ibn Mas'ud, which is considered an addition to the Quranic text, permissible because it restricts the absolute. The wrist (zand) is chosen as the point of cutting because it is the minimum certain understanding of 'hand'; in punishments, certainty is required. This is supported by the practice of the Prophet and the caliphs. Cauterization (hasm) is performed to prevent bleeding to death, following the Prophet's order. If the thief repeats, the left foot is cut, based on the practice of Ali, transmitted by Muhammad al-Shaybani in al-Athar. If he repeats a third time, he is not further amputated but imprisoned until he repents, again following Ali's practice and out of mercy to leave him limbs for basic needs. The Shafi'i school differs, cutting the left hand then right foot on the third offense, based on the general hadith 'if he repeats, cut him'. But the Hanafi school follows the more specific precedent of Ali. Regarding imprisonment, there are two views among Hanafi scholars: either just imprisonment or imprisonment with ta'zir (discretionary punishment).

Key Terms
الزند (al-zand): The wrist joint; the articulation between the forearm and hand, which is the point of amputation for theft.
الحسم (al-hasm): Cauterization of the amputated stump by applying a heated iron or boiling oil to stop bleeding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is done if a thief steals a third time after having his right hand and left foot cut?
A) His left hand is cut.
B) His right foot is cut.
C) He is imprisoned until he repents.
D) He is executed.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a thief's punishment is limited to two amputations (right hand, left foot); a third offense results in imprisonment, preserving the offender's ability to perform basic tasks.

Hanafi Stance on Amputation Sequence for Serial Theft
Refuting hadith opposing Hanafi amputation sequence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects the hadith that suggests cutting all four limbs for serial theft and state the relied-upon view.

Core Idea
Hanafi amputation sequence is based on Companion consensus and critical hadith analysis, not opposition to Sunnah.

This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute defending the Hanafi position on the manner of amputation for serial theft. The Hanafi school holds that a first-time thief loses only the right hand, and for a second theft, the left foot is cut. This view is attributed to Ali, Umar, and Ibn Abbas. The passage refutes a hadith that suggests cutting all four limbs over two thefts, arguing that the hadith is weak, abrogated, or a discretionary measure. By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the Hanafi proof and respond to the opposing evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Clearly state what the Hanafi school rules for a serial thief: right hand for first theft, left foot for second.
2
Identify the opposing view and its evidence
Describe the opposing view that calls for cutting all four limbs (right hand, left foot, then left hand, right foot over two thefts). Note the hadith they cite.
The opposing view is based on a hadith that the Prophet cut hand then foot etc., and a narration from Abu Bakr.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response: hadith criticism
Explain Al-Tahawi's critique that the hadith is not authentic. Mention that it is not found in sound collections.
Al-Tahawi said: 'We traced these reports and found no basis for them.'
4
Analyze the Hanafi response: alternative interpretations
Even if authentic, the Hanafi response offers two interpretations: either the hadith is abrogated (naskh) due to initial severity, or it was a discretionary political measure (siyasah), not a fixed hadd.
The case of the Urannis is cited as an example of early severity in hudud.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: under Hanafi law, a serial thief loses only one hand and one foot total; under the opposing view, he loses all four limbs over two thefts.
This directly affects the severity of punishment.

[٣] قوله : أو هو محمول.. الخ ؛ يعني لو سُلّم أن الحديث صحيح، فهو محمول على التعزير والسياسة، لا على أنه حد مقدَّر مقرّر ، وعلى هذا يحملُ ما رُوِيَ عن أبي بكر له من قطع اليدين والرجلين فيما أخرجه مالك وغيره، وقد أشبعنا الكلام في هذا
المقام في «التعليق الممجد على موطأ محمد».
Chapter of Theft / Section: Manner of Cutting and Its Proof. Our school (Hanafi) is reported from Ali. If the hadith were authentic, he would not have opposed it, and the Companions would not have taken his saying. Al-Tahawi has criticized the hadith, or it is interpreted as based on policy. In a narration by Ibn Abi Shaybah: Ibn Abbas wrote to Najda al-Haruri with a similar opinion to Ali's, and Umar consulted them about a thief and they all agreed upon an opinion like that of Ali. In another narration from Umar: 'Do not cut his other hand and leave him to eat and clean himself with it, but imprison him away from the Muslims.' [1] His saying: 'This is firmly established...' Al-Marghinani in Fath al-Qadir said: In al-Mabsut: The hadith is not authentic, and even if accepted, it is interpreted as abrogated because in the beginning there was severity in the hudud like cutting off the hands and feet of the Urannis and smearing their eyes. Then he said in al-Fath after mentioning what has passed from Umar, Ali, and Ibn Abbas: 'This is established beyond doubt, and it is far-fetched that the Prophet would cut off a thief's four limbs and then kill him without Ali, Umar, and Ibn Abbas knowing, who were his constant companions. Even if they were absent, it is customary that they would have known. So following Ali is either due to the weakness of what has passed or due to his knowledge that this is not a permanent hadd but rather from the imam's discretion.' [2] His saying: 'He criticized the hadith'; when he said: 'We traced these reports and found no basis for them, and it has passed that the reports of cutting four times are not proven by a chain that is considered authoritative.' [3] His saying: 'Or it is interpreted...'; meaning even if the hadith is accepted as authentic, it is interpreted as discretionary punishment (ta'zir) and policy, not as a fixed and determined hadd. And upon this is interpreted what is reported from Abu Bakr regarding cutting off hands and feet, as recorded by Malik and others, and we have thoroughly discussed this topic in our commentary al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad on Muwatta Muhammad.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute defending the Hanafi position on the manner of amputation for serial theft. The text begins by stating that the Hanafi view (only cutting the right hand for the first theft and the left foot for the second) is reported from Ali (RA). The author then addresses a potential objection: if a hadith opposing this view were authentic, Ali would not have contradicted it, nor would the Companions have followed his opinion. Al-Tahawi is cited as having criticized that hadith, and even if it were authentic, it is interpreted as a political measure rather than a fixed hadd. The passage provides evidence from narrations that Umar and Ibn Abbas agreed with Ali. The commentary from Fath al-Qadir explains that the hadith is not authentic, and even if accepted, it was abrogated because early on, hudud were applied more severely (like the case of the Urannis). The author emphasizes that it is highly unlikely the Prophet would cut off four limbs without the knowledge of close Companions like Ali, Umar, and Ibn Abbas. Therefore, the Hanafi position is either due to the weakness of the opposing evidence or because the reported incident was not a fixed hadd but a discretionary punishment. Finally, the author references his own work on Muwatta Muhammad for further discussion.

Key Terms
الحد (al-hadd): A fixed, divinely ordained punishment for specific crimes, such as theft.
السياسة (al-siyasah): Discretionary punishment based on public policy or the ruler's judgment, not a fixed hadd.
الانتساخ (al-intisakh): Abrogation; that the ruling of the hadith was later abrogated by another revelation or practice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position defended in this passage, what is the basis for rejecting the hadith that suggests cutting all four limbs?
A) The hadith is found in Bukhari and Muslim so it is authentic but abrogated.
B) The hadith is not authentic or is weak, and even if authentic it is interpreted as discretionary punishment or abrogated.
C) The hadith was contradicted by the Qur'an and thus cannot be applied.
D) The hadith was only applicable to non-Muslims.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi view on amputation sequence for serial theft is based on Companion consensus and rigorous hadith criticism, not opposition to the Sunnah.

Missing or Paralyzed Limbs: Effect on Hadd of Amputation
Listing excuses based on physical limb condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on amputation when the thief's left hand, thumb, two fingers, or right foot are missing or paralyzed, and explain why these conditions create doubt.

Core Idea
A missing or non-functional limb creates shubha (doubt) that prevents application of the hadd on that limb.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah details exceptions to the hadd of amputation. If the specific limb to be cut is already absent or non-functional, the punishment cannot be carried out because the intended physical retribution is impossible, and the state of the limb creates a legal doubt (shubha). Understanding these nuances is essential for correct application of criminal law in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the affected limb and condition
Determine which limb is at issue: left hand, its thumb, two fingers, or right foot. Check if it is already cut off (مقطوعة) or paralyzed (شلاء).
2
Apply the rule of doubt
If that limb is missing or non-functional, the hadd on that limb is dropped because of shubha (doubt). The principle is 'avoid hudud with doubts'.
This applies to the specific limb mentioned; other limbs are unaffected.
3
Distinguish between loss of one finger vs. two fingers
Loss of two fingers (other than thumb) impairs grasping significantly and is treated like loss of thumb – the hadd is dropped. Loss of one finger does not visibly impair grasping, so the hadd is still applied.
The distinction is based on functional impairment.
4
Understand the rationale
The hadd aims to remove a functioning limb as retribution. If the limb is already non-functional, the purpose is nullified, creating uncertainty (shubha) that prevents the punishment.
This reflects the Hanafi principle of avoiding hudud when doubt exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان يده اليسرى، أو إبهامها، أو إصبعاها ، أو رجله اليمنى مقطوعة، أو شلاء ، أو ردّه إلى مالكه قبل الخصومة، أو ملكه بهبة، أو بيع، أو نقصت قيمته من النصاب قبل القطع ، أو سرق فادعى ملكه، أو أحد السارقين وإن لم يُبرهن (فإن كان يده اليسرى، أو إبهامها ، أو إصبعاها، أو رجله اليمنى
[٢]

أو نقصت قيمته من النصاب قبل القطع، أو سرق فادعى ملكه، أو أحد السارقين وإن لم يُبرهن [١] قوله : أو إصبعاها ؛ أي سوى الإبهام، فإن كانت أصبع واحدة سوى الإبهام مقطوعة أو شلاء قطع ؛ لأن فوت الواحدة لا يوجب خللها ظاهراً في البطش بخلاف فوت الإصبعين ؛ لأنهما يتنَزَّلان منزلة الإبهام في نقصان البطش. كذا في «الهداية»). [٢] قوله : أو شلاء ؛ - بفتح الشين المعجمة ، وتشديد اللام - : أي يابسة بحيث
لا تتحرك. (۳) قوله : أو رده ؛ يعني سرق شيئاً، ثمّ ردّه إلى مالكه قبل أن يخاصم هو معه عند القاضي سواء كان الرد إلى مالكه حقيقة أو حكماً ، بأن يرد إلى أصوله، ولو في غير عياله : كوالده وجده ووالدته وجدته ؛ لأن لهؤلاء شبهة الملك ، فتثبت شبهة الردّ، ومن الرد الحكمي الردُّ إلى فرعه، وكلُّ ذي رحم محرم منه، وإلى مكاتبه، وعبده، وزوجته، وأجيره مشاهرة، أو مسانهة، كذا في «البحر» و«الفتح». [٤]قوله : أو ملكه ؛ أي سرق شيئاً، ثمّ ملكه السارق بهبة المالك، أو بيعه منه
قبل القطع، ولو بعد حكم القاضي به. [0]قوله : فادعى ملكه؛ یعنی سرق شيئاً وادّعى أنه ملكه، أو ادّعى ذلك أحد السارقين، وسكت الآخرون سواء ثبت ذلك بشهادة أو لم يثبت، فإن مجرد دعواه يورث الشبهة.
[٦ ) قوله : أو أحد ؛ يعني إذا أقرّ رجلان بسرقة أو ثبت ذلك بالبينة، ثم قال
If his left hand, or its thumb, or two fingers, or his right foot, is cut off or paralyzed, or he returns [the stolen property] to its owner before litigation, or he owns it by gift or sale, or its value decreases below the nisab before cutting, or he steals and claims ownership, or one of the thieves [does so] even if he does not prove [it]... [1] His saying: 'or two fingers' means other than the thumb. If just one finger besides the thumb is cut or paralyzed, the hand is cut because the loss of one finger does not visibly impair grasping, unlike the loss of two fingers, which are comparable to the thumb in reducing grasping ability. This is from al-Hidayah. [2] His saying: 'or paralyzed' – with a fatha on the shin and shadda on the lam – means dry such that it does not move. [3] His saying: 'or returned' means he stole something then returned it to its owner before he (the thief) litigates with him before the judge, whether the return is actual or legal (hukman), such as returning it to his ascendants even if not in his household: like his father, grandfather, mother, grandmother, because they have a semblance of ownership, so the semblance of return is established. Legal return also includes returning to his descendant, every blood relative within prohibited degrees, his mukatab, his slave, his wife, and his employee hired by month or year. This is in al-Bahr and al-Fath. [4] His saying: 'or he owns it' means he stole something then the thief owned it by the owner's gift or sale to him before cutting, even after the judge's ruling. [5] His saying: 'so he claims ownership' means he stole something and claims it is his, or one of the thieves makes that claim while the others remain silent, whether that is established by testimony or not, for his mere claim creates doubt. [6] His saying: 'or one' means if two men confess to theft or it is established by evidence, then one of them says...
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement enumerating conditions that prevent the hadd of amputation from being applied or that alter the specific limb to be cut. The text lists several scenarios: if the thief's left hand (the one normally cut for the second theft), its thumb, two fingers, or right foot are already missing or paralyzed; if the stolen property is returned to the owner before litigation; if the thief becomes owner of the item through gift or sale; if the value falls below the nisab before cutting; or if the thief claims ownership of the stolen item (even without proof). The commentary then explains each condition in detail. For 'two fingers': loss of two fingers impairs grasping as much as loss of the thumb, so the hadd is dropped; loss of one finger does not. For 'paralyzed' (shalla'), it means a rigid, non-functional limb. 'Return' includes both actual return and legal (hukmi) return, such as returning to ascendants or descendants who have a proprietary interest. 'Ownership' by gift or sale after theft but before cutting also nullifies the hadd. 'Claim of ownership' creates a doubt (shubha) which prevents the hadd, even if unproven. The passage is from a sharh (Al-Imdad or similar) explaining the matn of Al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
الشلاء (al-shalla'): A limb that is paralyzed, dry, and has no movement.
الشبهة (al-shubhah): Doubt; a situation that creates uncertainty about the application of the hadd, leading to its non-application.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this lesson, which of the following conditions drops the hadd of amputation on the left hand?
A) The left hand is missing one finger (not thumb).
B) The left hand has two fingers (not thumb) cut off.
C) The left hand is fully intact but the thief is left-handed.
D) The left hand has a minor scar.

Key Takeaway
A missing or paralyzed limb creates doubt (shubha) that prevents the hadd of amputation on that specific limb, with the exception that a single finger (other than thumb) is insufficient to create doubt.

Other Shubhat Preventing Amputation: Return, Ownership, Claim, and Value Drop
Listing non-physical shubhat that prevent amputation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the Hanafi conditions that prevent the hadd of amputation due to return of stolen property, change in ownership, claim of ownership, or decrease in value below nisab.

Core Idea
Post-theft events that create ownership-related doubts (shubhat) nullify the hadd of amputation, even without proof.

This part of the passage from Al-Hidayah's commentary discusses additional situations where the hadd of amputation is dropped due to shubha (doubt). These include returning the stolen item to the owner before litigation, the thief acquiring ownership through gift or sale, a decrease in value below the nisab threshold before cutting, and a claim of ownership by the thief (or one of multiple thieves) even if unproven. The commentary clarifies that these events create legal doubt, and the Hanafi principle of avoiding hudud with doubts applies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the post-theft event
Determine which specific event occurred: return to owner, change in ownership (gift/sale), value drop below nisab, or claim of ownership.
These events occur after the theft but before the hadd is carried out.
2
Apply the concept of shubha
Each event creates a legal doubt (shubha) regarding the thief's culpability or the integrity of the property. The hadd is dropped to avoid potential injustice.
The principle is 'avoid hudud with doubts' (ادرؤوا الحدود بالشبهات).
3
Distinguish between actual and legal return
Return can be actual (to owner) or legal (hukmi, e.g., to ascendants/descendants who have a proprietary interest). Both are sufficient to drop the hadd.
Legal return includes returning to father, mother, grandparents, children, spouse, and certain employees.
4
Analyze claim of ownership
If the thief claims the stolen item is his (or one of multiple thieves claims), that claim creates doubt even if unproven. The hadd is dropped.
The doubt exists because the claim raises uncertainty about the establishment of theft.
5
Check value drop condition
If the value of the stolen property falls below the nisab before the amputation is carried out, the hadd is dropped because the threshold for the punishment is no longer met.
The nisab is the minimum value for hadd; once it drops, the crime's gravity reduces.

Conditions Negating Hadd Amputation for Theft
Conditions that prevent the hadd amputation for theft.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions that negate the hadd amputation for theft and apply the rational proof (dalil aqli) to explain why cutting is not applied in each case.

Core Idea
Hadd is dropped when cutting would destroy total limb function or when a shubha (doubt) exists, even a mere claim of ownership.

The hadd punishment for theft is not applied in every case where the nisab is met. In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, we learn specific conditions—like a non-functional limb, return of property, or claim of ownership—that prevent amputation. Understanding these conditions is key to applying hudud justly and avoiding destruction that contradicts the purpose of punishment.

Guided Path
1
Enumerate the conditions that negate hadd
Read the opening lines of the passage and list all conditions mentioned: left hand cut/paralyzed, thumb missing, two fingers missing, right foot cut/paralyzed, return of property, ownership acquired, value drop, claim of ownership, owner non-demand.
The conditions are cumulative; any single one suffices to drop the hadd.
2
Understand the concept of shubha
Identify which conditions create a legal doubt (shubha). The claim of ownership, even without proof, creates shubha. Similarly, return to relatives with semblance of ownership creates shubhat al-ridd.
Shubha prevents the hadd even if weak.
3
Analyze the rational proof for non-functional limbs
Examine the reasoning: if the left hand is already non-functional, cutting the right hand destroys all grasping ability, which is destruction (ihlak), not punishment. The Shari'ah intends cutting as a penalty, not annihilation.
This rational proof shows the underlying maqsad.
4
Apply the rule to the co-thief scenario
When one of two thieves claims ownership, the hadd is dropped from both. Understand that the claim creates doubt for the other, as the theft is established by their joint act.
The objection of 'doubt of doubt' is rejected because the claimer's drop is by retraction, not doubt.

If his left hand, or its thumb, or its two fingers, or his right foot is cut off, or paralyzed, or he returns it to its owner before litigation, or he owns it by gift or sale, or its value decreases below the nisab before cutting, or he steals and claims ownership, or one of the two thieves claims ownership even if he does not prove [1] He said: or its two fingers; i.e. other than the thumb, for if a single finger other than the thumb is cut off or paralyzed, he is cut; because the loss of one does not cause an apparent defect in grasping, unlike the loss of two fingers; because they take the place of the thumb in decreasing grasping. Thus in al-Hidayah. [2] He said: or paralyzed; with fatha on the shin and tashdid on the lam: i.e. dry such that it does not move. [3] He said: or he returns it; meaning he stole something, then returned it to its owner before he (the thief) litigates with him (the owner) before the judge, whether the return is actual or legal, such as returning it to his roots, even if not among his dependents: like his father, grandfather, mother, grandmother; because for these there is a semblance of ownership, so the semblance of return is established. And from the legal return is the return to his branch, and every mahram relative, and to his mukatib, slave, wife, and hireling by month or year, as in al-Bahr and al-Fath. [4] He said: or he owns it; i.e. he stole something, then the thief owns it by the owner's gift or sale before the cutting, even after the judge's ruling. [0] He said: and claims ownership; i.e. he stole something and claimed it is his, or one of the two thieves claimed that, and the others remained silent, whether that is established by testimony or not, for his mere claim creates a doubt. [6] He said: or one; meaning if two men confess to theft or it is established by evidence, then one of them says: it is my property, the cutting is negated from both; because the retraction is effective regarding the retractor, and his mere claim of ownership drops the hadd from him and creates doubt regarding the other; because the theft was established by their partnership, not by his individuality. Or the owner does not demand it even if he (the thief) confesses to it, so there is no cutting; because if the right hand is cut and the grasping strength is missing in the left, it necessitates losing the entire benefit, which is in reality destruction. Similarly if the right foot is cut off or paralyzed; because if a person has no hand and foot on one side, he cannot walk at all. As for from two sides, he places the stick under his armpit, so it takes the place of the missing foot. If you say: His saying: it is my property creates doubt regarding the retractor, so regarding the other it is doubt of doubt, which is not considered. I say: The dropping of the cutting from the retractor is by his retraction, not by way of doubt, so the doubt is considered regarding the other, as in al-Kifaya. [1] He said: or he does not demand; meaning he did not litigate with him, the owner of the theft, and did not demand it even if he confessed. [2] He said: so no cutting; is the result of his saying: if his left hand... etc.; meaning he is not cut in all these cases. [3] He said: missing; because it is paralyzed or missing two fingers, or the thumb. For the loss [4] He said: he cannot... etc.; its summary: The Shari'ah only made the punishment of the thief cutting, not destruction, and losing the entire benefit of a limb's benefits is destruction legally. So if his right hand and right foot are cut from which he cannot benefit in walking, it necessitates losing the benefit of walking absolutely; the hand and foot from one side (right or left) necessitates the nullification of walking, unlike if one is cut from one side and the other from the other side. For then he can walk, even with difficulty, so it does not prevent cutting the left foot in the second theft after his right hand was cut. Similarly, if his left hand is such that he cannot benefit in grasping, if his right is cut it necessitates losing the benefit of grasping.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a critical refinement (hashiya) on the Hidayah, clarifying the conditions under which the hadd amputation for theft is not applied. The ruling statement begins: 'If his left hand, or its thumb, or its two fingers, or his right foot is cut off or paralyzed, or he returns it to its owner before litigation, or he owns it by gift or sale, or its value decreases below the nisab before cutting, or he steals and claims ownership, or one of the two thieves claims ownership even if he does not prove... then there is no cutting.' The commentary then explains each condition in detail. For the loss of two fingers (other than the thumb), it says that loss of one finger does not significantly impair grasping, but loss of two fingers does, so the cut is not applied. 'Shalla'' (paralyzed) is defined as immobile. The return of stolen property to the owner before litigation includes actual return and legal return (e.g., to relatives who have a semblance of ownership). The thief acquiring ownership by gift or sale after the theft also prevents the cut. A mere claim of ownership by the thief creates a doubt (shubha) that precludes the hadd, and if one of two thieves claims ownership, the cut is dropped from both because the theft was established by their joint act. Additionally, if the owner does not demand the property, even if the thief confesses, there is no cut. The rational proof (dalil aqli) is that cutting the right hand when the left is already impaired would destroy the entire benefit of gripping, which is effectively destruction, not punishment. Similarly, cutting the right foot when the left is missing prevents walking entirely, whereas cutting from both sides allows walking with assistance. The passage also addresses an objection: the claim of ownership creates doubt for the claimer, but for the other thief it is a 'doubt of doubt,' which is normally not considered. The response is that the cut is dropped from the claimer by his retraction, not by doubt, so the remaining doubt is valid for the other. All these conditions are from the zahir al-riwayah and are relied upon in the madhhab.

Key Terms
شلاء (shallā'): A paralyzed or dry limb that cannot move.
شبهة (shubhah): A doubt or semblance that prevents the application of a hadd punishment.
إصبعاها (iṣbaʿāhā): Her two fingers (dual of إصبع), specifically meaning two fingers other than the thumb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the ruling if a thief's left hand is already paralyzed and he steals with his right hand?
A) His right hand is cut because the left is already non-functional.
B) The cutting is not applied because it would lead to total loss of grasping benefit.
C) He is given the discretionary punishment (ta'zir) instead.
D) Both hands are cut.

Key Takeaway
The hadd amputation for theft is not a mechanical punishment; it is suspended by conditions that cause destruction or create doubt, preserving the maqasid of justice.

Return of Stolen Property Before Litigation and Hadd
Ruling on returning stolen property before legal proceedings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on returning stolen property before litigation, explain the condition of litigation (khusumah) for theft, and evaluate the opposing positions of Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Litigation is a condition for the appearance of theft; return before claim prevents hadd.

This lesson examines a critical condition for applying the hadd of theft: the requirement of litigation (khusumah) between the owner and thief. The Hanafi school holds that if the stolen property is returned before any legal proceedings, the theft is not legally established, and thus the hand is not cut. This position, however, was disputed within the Hanafi school by Abu Yusuf and externally by al-Shafi'i and Zufar. Understanding the underlying reasoning clarifies the Hanafi emphasis on procedural conditions in hudud.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling from the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah): the hand is not cut when the stolen property is returned to the owner before any litigation (khusumah).
The text explicitly says 'لا يمكنُ الدَّعوى ، فلا يظهرُ السِّرق' – no claim possible, so theft does not appear.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab disputes
Note that Abu Yusuf within the Hanafi school, and al-Shafi'i and Zufar outside, hold that the hand is cut. Abu Yusuf's view is a weaker narration (nawadir), while the opposing schools rely on the completeness of the theft act.
Intra-Hanafi: Abu Yusuf argues the theft was already complete and apparent.
3
Follow the opposing proof
Understand the reasoning: Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, and Zufar argue that the theft act itself is sufficient; subsequent events like return or gift do not eliminate the hadd because the crime is already established.
They see no shubha in the thief's ownership through gift or sale.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi apparent school responds that litigation (murafa'ah) is a condition for the appearance of theft. The evidence (bayyinah) is to cut off dispute; if the property is returned before dispute, the condition is lost. Also, execution of hadd requires continuation of litigation at the time of execution.
The judge's responsibility in hudud is not fulfilled by merely pronouncing the verdict; execution requires ongoing litigation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi law, if the thief returns the property and the owner does not file a claim, the judge does not cut the hand. Under Shafi'i law, the judge would cut regardless.
This difference stems from the procedural condition of litigation versus the substantive completion of the act.

[٢] قوله : ليعلم.... الخ ؛ وذلك لأن من المعلوم أنّ الملك لا يحصل في الهبة بدون القبض. (۳) قوله : يقطع ؛ هو رواية عن أبي يوسف ؛ لأن السرقة قد تمت انعقاداً وظهوراً، وبهذا العارض : أي ثبوت الملك للسارق بسبب الهبة أو البيع لا يتبين قيام الملك فلا شبهة تُسقط الحد. ووجه الظاهر: إنّ الإمضاء في باب الحدود من القضاء، فإنّ القاضي لا يخرجُ عن عهدة القضاء في باب الحدود بدون الاستيفاء، لا بمجرَّد قوله : حكمت بكذا ، فلا بُدَّ من بقاء الخصومة وقت الاستيفاء، كما يشترط حين ابتداء القضاء، وقد انتفت بالبيع أو الهبة، فإنّه لَمَّا ملكه بهبة أو بيع ترك الخصومة معه، وترك الخصومة يمنعُ القضاء بالقطع، فكذا يمنع الاستيفاء، ولـو بعـد الحكم اللساني ، كذا في «العناية»(۳) ، و«البناية» (٤)
And if the stolen property is returned to its owner before litigation, no claim is possible, so theft does not appear. And according to Abu Yusuf, the hand is cut. He only said "ownership by gift" to make known that what is meant is the gift with possession. And according to Zufar and al-Shafi'i, the hand is cut. The basis of this chapter is the refusal of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) and other Companions to cut the right hand and left hand for the third and fourth theft, as has passed. [1] His statement: "the hand is cut" is in consideration of the case when he returns it after referral to the judge, for then the hand is cut by agreement, whether the return is before or after the judge's ruling on cutting. The apparent school's view is that litigation is a condition for the appearance of theft because the evidence is made a proof to cut off dispute, and it has been cut off by returning to the owner before dispute, so the condition for appearance of theft is lost, so its appearance is cut off, and there is no cutting without its appearance, unlike after referral to the judge because the dispute is ended. Thus in al-'Inaya. [2] His statement: "to make known..." that because it is known that ownership in a gift does not obtain without possession. (3) His statement: "the hand is cut" is a narration from Abu Yusuf because the theft has been completed in occurrence and appearance, and by this occurrence—meaning the establishment of ownership for the thief due to gift or sale—the existence of ownership does not become clear, so no doubt (shubha) that drops the hadd. The apparent school's view is that execution in the chapter of hudud is part of judgement; the judge does not exit from the responsibility of judgement in hudud without execution, not merely by his saying "I have judged thus". So the continuation of litigation is necessary at the time of execution, just as it is required at the start of judgement. And it has been removed by sale or gift, for when he owned it by gift or sale, he left litigation with him, and leaving litigation prevents judgement with cutting, so similarly it prevents execution, even after oral judgement. Thus in al-'Inaya and al-Binaya.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling regarding the hadd punishment for theft when the stolen property is returned to its owner before any legal proceedings (khusumah). The base ruling, which is the position of the Hanafi school according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), is that the hand is not cut because the theft is not established. The reason is that litigation (murafa'ah) is a condition for the appearance (zuhur) of theft—without a claim, the theft cannot be proven in court. If the property is returned before litigation, no claim can be made, so the theft does not appear, and thus no hadd. This is contrasted with the case where the property is returned after referral to the judge: in that case, cutting is agreed upon by all. The passage then notes two contrary positions: (1) Abu Yusuf holds that the hand is cut, because the theft was already complete and apparent at the time of the act; the subsequent ownership by the thief does not create a sufficient doubt (shubha) to drop the hadd. (2) Zufar and al-Shafi'i also hold that the hand is cut. The commentary defends the Hanafi position by emphasizing that in hudud, execution is part of judgement and requires the continuation of litigation; once the thief acquires ownership, he drops the complaint, so execution cannot proceed. The passage also clarifies that when the matn says "ownership by gift" (milkahu bihibah), it implies gift with possession (qabd), as ownership is not complete without it. The commentary additionally references a principle: in repeated thefts, only the alternate hand is cut (e.g., left hand for third theft), following the practice of ʿAli.

Key Terms
الخصومة (al-khusumah): Litigation or legal dispute between the claimant (owner) and the defendant (thief) before the judge.
السرقة (al-sariqa): Theft: the act of taking another's property secretly and without right, subject to hadd when conditions are met.
شبهة (shubha): Doubt or ambiguity that can prevent the application of hadd punishments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the apparent Hanafi position (zahir al-riwayah), why is the hand not cut if the stolen property is returned to the owner before any legal proceedings?
A) Because the thief has repented and returned the property.
B) Because litigation is a condition for the appearance of theft, and without litigation the theft is not established.
C) Because the owner has forgiven the thief.
D) Because the minimum nisab was not met.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires litigation (khusumah) for theft to be established; returning property before any claim removes the condition for hadd.

Decrease in Value of Stolen Property: Hadd or Not?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on nisab continuity at execution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the effect of a decrease in market value of stolen property on the hadd punishment and explain the qiyas-based reasoning for Abu Hanifa's view.

Core Idea
A mere drop in market price after theft does not trigger the guarantee, so if the nisab condition fails at execution, the hadd is not applied.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about the persistence of the nisab (minimum value) requirement for theft. If a stolen item initially meets the nisab but its market value drops below that threshold before the hadd is carried out—even after a judicial ruling—should the thief still lose his hand? The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say yes, considering the physical item's existence. Abu Hanifa says no, drawing a clever distinction between physical destruction and price fluctuation.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the timing
Identify the scenario: the stolen item met the nisab (10 dirhams) at the time of theft, but its market value falls below that before execution. The hadd is not yet carried out even after a judicial ruling.
The nisab condition is tied to the time of appearance of theft (zuhur al-sariqa), which includes judgement and execution.
2
Identify the two positions
Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the hand is cut. Abu Hanifa holds that the hand is not cut. The text says "cut according to the two" and "not cut according to us (Abu Hanifa)".
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute; the mufta bihi position is Abu Hanifa's.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn consider the decrease in the physical item ('ayn), not its market price. Since the physical item still exists and is guaranteed (mamnun) against the thief, the nisab is considered intact.
They analogize to a case where the item is partially consumed—the hand is still cut because the thief is liable for the value.
4
Analyse Abu Hanifa's qiyas distinction
Abu Hanifa distinguishes between decrease in the physical item (istihlak) and decrease in price (nuqsan al-si'r). A decrease in the physical item is guaranteed—the thief owes its value, so nisab is complete in substance and debt. A decrease in price is not guaranteed—the thief does not owe the difference; therefore, if price drops below nisab, the condition fails at execution.
This is a qiyas khafi: the illa is that the condition of nisab requires continuity at execution, and price drop is not covered by the guarantee.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text says "it is not cut according to us" (wa innama la yuqta'u 'indana), indicating the authoritative school position is Abu Hanifa's.
This is consistent with the Hanafi emphasis on nisab as a condition at the time of execution.

Source Text

وكذا في نقصان القيمة" يقطع " عندهما (۱). ۳۱۷ وإنما لا يقطع عندنا؛ لأنَّ النصاب لما كان شرطاً عند ظهور السرقة، وهو حال القضاء". [٤] [١]قوله : نقصان القيمة ؛ أي نقصان قيمة المسروق بأن تكون قيمته عند السرقة عشرة دراهم فما فوقها، ثمَّ تنقص عنه قبل استيفاء الحد ولو بعد القضاء. [٢]قوله : يقطع عندهما ؛ وهو رواية عن محمد له اعتباراً بالنقصان في العين. ولنا : إن كمال النصاب لَمَّا كان شرطاً يشترط قيامه عند الإمضاء بخلاف النقصان في العين ؛ لأنه مضمون عليه، فكمل النصاب عيناً وديناً، كما إذا استهلك كلّه، أمّا نقصان السعر غير مضمون فافترقا، وكذا في «الهداية»(٢). [٣]قوله : وهو حال القضاء ؛ وقد مرَّ أن الإمضاء أيضاً من تتممة القضاء، ففواتُ الشرط بنقصان السعر عند القضاء أو عند الإمضاء يمنعُ الإمضاء.
Similarly, in decrease of value, the hand is cut according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And it is not cut according to us (Abu Hanifa) because the nisab was a condition at the time of appearance of theft, which is at the time of judgement. [4] [1] His statement: decrease in value means the value of the stolen property was ten dirhams or more at the time of theft, then it decreases below that before execution of the hadd, even after judgement. [2] His statement: cut according to the two; this is a narration from Muhammad, considering the decrease in the physical item. Our proof: the completion of nisab is a condition that requires its continuation at the time of execution, unlike the decrease in the physical item because it is guaranteed (mamnun) against him, so nisab remains complete in substance and debt (in value), as if he had consumed it all. As for decrease in price, it is not guaranteed, so they differ. Thus in al-Hidaya. [3] His statement: which is at the time of judgement; it has passed that execution is also part of the completion of judgement, so the loss of the condition due to decrease in price at judgement or at execution prevents execution.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage discusses a secondary issue regarding the hadd punishment when the value of the stolen property decreases after the nisab (minimum threshold) was initially met. The base text states: "Similarly, in decrease of value, the hand is cut according to the two (cindahuma)"—meaning Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. The commentary explains that this refers to a situation where the stolen item was worth ten dirhams (the nisab for theft) at the time of the theft, but its value drops below that before the hadd can be executed, even after a judicial ruling. According to Abu Hanifa, the hand is not cut because the nisab must exist at the time of appearance of theft, which is at the time of judgement (qada). Since the value decreased, the condition fails. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the hand is cut, considering that the decrease is not in the physical item but in its market price; the physical item, which was already guaranteed (mamnun) against the thief, remains intact. The commentary distinguishes between decrease in the physical item ('ayn) and decrease in price (si'r). A decrease in the physical item (e.g., part of it is consumed) does not prevent cutting because the thief is liable for the value, and nisab is considered complete in both substance and debt. But a decrease in market price is not guaranteed, so it affects the nisab and prevents cutting. This is the reasoning for Abu Hanifa's position. The passage further notes that execution (imda) is part of judgement; if the condition fails at the time of execution, the hadd cannot be enforced.

Key Terms
نصاب (nisab): The minimum value of stolen property required for hadd (ten dirhams or one dinar).
مضمون (mamnun): Guaranteed; the thief is liable for the value of the physical item he steals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The hand is cut because the physical item still exists.
B) The hand is not cut because the nisab condition fails at execution.
C) The hand is cut if the decrease occurred after the judicial ruling.
D) The hand is not cut only if the thief returns the item.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a decrease in the market value of stolen property below nisab before execution of hadd prevents the punishment, because the nisab condition must persist at execution and price fluctuation is not guaranteed against the thief.

Thief's Claim of Ownership and Hadd Punishment
Dispute on thief's claim of ownership and hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the thief's claim of ownership and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the analogy with retraction of confession.

Core Idea
A thief's claim of ownership creates a shubha that drops the hadd, by analogy with retraction of confession.

This lesson examines a classic dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: does a thief's mere claim that the stolen property is his own (without evidence) prevent the application of the hadd punishment for theft? Al-Shafi'i argues that accepting such claims would render the hadd practically inapplicable, as every thief would make the claim. The Hanafi school responds by drawing an analogy with the retraction of a confession, which creates a shubha (doubt) and drops the hadd by consensus. This lesson walks through the reasoning and practical consequences of this disagreement.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school holds regarding the thief's claim of ownership and its effect on the hadd punishment.
The claim, even without proof, creates a shubha that prevents cutting the hand.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Al-Shafi'i argues that accepting such a claim would block the door of hadd because every thief could make the claim.
The objection is based on practical enforcement.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand al-Shafi'i's reasoning: if mere claim suffices, no thief will ever be punished because all will claim ownership.
This would render the hadd ineffective.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds by drawing an analogy with the retraction of confession (ruju' an al-iqrar). Just as retraction creates a shubha and drops the hadd by consensus, the claim of ownership similarly creates a shubha.
The same 'blocking' argument could apply to retraction, yet it is accepted.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: a Hanafi judge would not apply the hadd if the thief claims ownership (even without proof), while a Shafi'i judge would proceed with the punishment.
This highlights the Hanafi inclination to avoid hudud in cases of doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ذكر في كتبنا أنه لا يندفع القطع عند الشافعي ه بمجرد دعـوى السارق أنَّ المسروق ملكه ؛ لأنه لا يعجز سارقٌ عن ذلك، فيؤدي إلى سد باب الحد [٤] [١]قوله : بمجرد دعوى ... الخ ؛ حاصله : إن مجرّد الدعوى من السارق بأنه ملكه بدون حجة لا يدفع القطع ؛ لأنه لا يعجز سارق عن مثل هذا، فيؤدي إلى سد باب الحد، فإن كل أحد يدعي مثل ذلك. وأجاب عنه في «الهداية (۳) و«النهاية»: بأن المقرّ بالسرقة إذا رجع عن إقراره يندفع القطع اتفاقاً ؛ لوجود الشبهة، ومن المعلوم أنه لا يعجز سارق عن رجوعه، فلما كان ذلك معتبراً في باب الشبهة كان ادعاؤه الملك أيضاً وإن لم يثبت ذلك بحجة شبهةً يندفع بها الحد.
And it has been mentioned in our books that cutting is not repelled according to al-Shafi'i merely by the thief's claim that the stolen property is his, because no thief is incapable of that, and it would lead to blocking the door of the hadd. [4] [1] His statement: "Merely by claim..." Its summary: The mere claim of the thief that it is his without proof does not repel cutting because no thief is incapable of such, and it would lead to blocking the door of the hadd, for everyone would claim such. And the answer to this is given in al-Hidaya and al-Nihaya: The one who confesses to theft, if he retracts his confession, the cutting is repelled by agreement due to the presence of shubha, and it is known that no thief is incapable of retraction. Since that is considered in the chapter of shubha, so too his claim of ownership, even if not proven by evidence, is a shubha by which the hadd is repelled.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection from al-Shafi'i: He argues that if the thief's mere claim of ownership were accepted to drop the hadd, then no thief would be punished because all would make such a claim. The Hanafi response, drawn from al-Hidaya and al-Nihaya, uses an analogy (qiyas) with retraction of confession. If a thief confesses to theft and then retracts his confession, the hadd is dropped by consensus due to the creation of doubt (shubha). Even though every thief could retract, this does not block the hadd because the system relies on evidence and proof. Similarly, the thief's claim of ownership, even without proof, creates a shubha that prevents the hadd. The rationale is that in hudud, doubts are given weight to avoid punishments. This shows the Hanafi method of istihsan: allowing shubha to override the general principle of preventing evasion.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): Ambiguity or doubt that prevents the application of hadd punishments.
إقرار (iqrar): Confession by the thief that he committed theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They argue that the claim must be proven with evidence.
B) They draw an analogy with retraction of confession, which also creates shubha and drops hadd.
C) They reject the claim entirely.
D) They require the owner to confirm the claim.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses analogy with retraction of confession to justify that a thief's claim of ownership, even if unproven, creates a shubha that drops the hadd punishment.

Effect of Value Drop on Hadd for Theft: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on effect of value drop before hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether a decrease in the stolen item's value before execution prevents the hadd punishment, and distinguish the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad from that of Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
A drop in value after theft blocks hadd; a physical defect does not, because price is unguaranteed.

This passage addresses a nuanced intra-Hanafi disagreement about the condition of nisab in theft. The nisab (minimum value of 10 dirhams) must be present for the hadd of cutting to be applied. But what if the stolen item's market value drops after the theft but before the judge executes the punishment? The two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that cutting still applies, while Imam Abu Hanifa rules that it does not. The key distinction is whether the decrease is in value (price) or in the physical item itself. Let's walk through the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the condition
Theft hadd requires nisab at the time of cutting. The dispute centers on whether a drop in value removes the nisab condition.
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa: no cutting if value drops below nisab before execution. Abu Yusuf & Muhammad: cutting applies.
3
Understand Abu Hanifa's proof
The nisab must be complete at execution (imda'). A price drop is not guaranteed (mamun) by the thief, so it removes the condition. Physical damage is guaranteed, so it doesn't remove the condition.
4
Understand the two shaykhs' proof
They analogize value drop to physical damage: since the thief guarantees the value, the nisab is considered complete in both item and debt, like if the item was consumed.

Book of Theft / Section: How the Cutting (of the hand) is Carried Out and Its Proof. Similarly, in the case of a deficiency in value, it is cut according to both of them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And the reason we do not cut (in our school) is because the nisab was a condition at the time of the emergence of the theft, which is the time of judgment. Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of a decrease in the value of the stolen item after the theft but before the hadd penalty is carried out. The matn states that according to the two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), the thief's hand is cut even if the value drops below the nisab before execution. However, according to our school (Imam Abu Hanifa and the relied-upon position), it is not cut. The reason is that the completeness of the nisab is a condition for the cutting, and this condition must exist at the time of execution (imda'), not merely at the time of theft. A decrease in the value (as opposed to a decrease in the physical item itself, e.g., if the item is damaged) is not something that is guaranteed by the thief – he is not liable for market fluctuations. Therefore, if the value drops, the nisab is no longer complete at the time of cutting, so the hadd is not applied. The two shaykhs analogize it to a decrease in the physical item (which is guaranteed and thus does not prevent cutting because the thief is liable for the value), but Imam Abu Hanifa distinguishes between the two because a price decrease is not guaranteed. The relied-upon fatwa is according to Imam Abu Hanifa, as stated in al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
نقصان القيمة (nuqsān al-qīmah): A decrease in the market value of the stolen item, as opposed to physical damage to the item itself.
نصاب (niṣāb): The minimum threshold value (10 dirhams) that triggers the hadd punishment for theft.
حال القضاء (ḥāl al-qaḍā’): The time when the judge passes the sentence; the condition of nisab must exist at this time or at execution.

Key Takeaway

Thief's Claim of Ownership: Does It Prevent Hadd?
Inter-madhhab dispute on shubha in theft hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on whether a thief's bare claim of ownership repels hadd, and explain the Hanafi qiyas argument from retraction of confession.

Core Idea
A thief's bare claim of ownership is a shubha that repels hadd, analogized from retraction of confession.

This lesson examines a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the effect of a thief's claim of ownership on the hadd punishment for theft. The Hanafi school, relying on the principle of shubha, argues that even a bare claim without proof creates sufficient doubt to avert the severe punishment. The Shafi'i school, concerned about the potential abuse of such claims, maintains that the hadd should be applied unless the thief provides evidence. We will explore the reasoning behind each position, with special attention to the Hanafi qiyas on retraction of confession.

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i position and its concern
First, understand the Shafi'i argument: that accepting a thief's bare claim would allow every thief to avoid punishment, effectively closing the door of hadd.
2
State the Hanafi position
Then, learn the Hanafi position: that the mere claim creates a shubha (doubt) that repels the hadd, even without proof.
3
Identify the Hanafi dalil (qiyas)
Trace the Hanafi proof: it is a qiyas on the case of a thief who retracts his confession, which repels hadd by agreement due to shubha. Since retraction is possible for any thief, it does not close the door; similarly, a claim of ownership is a valid shubha.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection
Understand how the Hanafis rebut the Shafi'i: the same objection (that every thief could do it) applies to retraction, yet retraction is accepted. Consistency demands accepting the claim as well.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Recognize the real-world difference: in a Hanafi court, hadd is not applied if the thief claims ownership without proof; in a Shafi'i court, hadd is applied unless proof is provided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ذكر في كتبنا أنه لا يندفع القطع عند الشافعي بمجرد دعوى السارق أن المسروق ملكه؛ لأنه لا يعجز سارق عن ذلك، فيؤدي إلى سد باب الحد [٤] قوله : بمجرد دعوى ... الخ؛ حاصله: إن مجرد الدعوى من السارق بأنه ملكه بدون حجة لا يدفع القطع؛ لأنه لا يعجز سارق عن مثل هذا، فيؤدي إلى سد باب الحد، فإن كل أحد يدعي مثل ذلك. وأجاب عنه في «الهداية» و«النهاية»: بأن المقرّ بالسرقة إذا رجع عن إقراره يندفع القطع اتفاقاً؛ لوجود الشبهة، ومن المعلوم أنه لا يعجز سارق عن رجوعه، فلما كان ذلك معتبراً في باب الشبهة كان ادعاؤه الملك أيضاً وإن لم يثبت ذلك بحجة شبهةً يندفع بها الحد.
And it has been mentioned in our books that the cutting is not repelled according to al-Shafi'i simply by the thief's claim that the stolen item is his property, because no thief would be incapable of that, leading to closing the door of hadd. [4] Statement: 'simply by the claim ...' – the summary: that the mere claim of the thief that it is his property without proof does not repel the cutting, because no thief would be incapable of such, leading to closing the door of hadd, for everyone would claim such. Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding whether a thief's mere claim that the stolen item is his property can prevent the hadd punishment. The Shafi'i school argues that such a bare claim should not prevent the cutting because every thief could make such a claim, which would render the hadd punishment obsolete. However, the Hanafi response, as found in al-Hidayah and al-Nihayah, points out that if a thief confesses and then retracts his confession, even the Shafi'is agree that the hadd is repelled due to the existence of shubha. Since even a retraction is possible for any thief, the same logic applies to a claim of ownership. Therefore, the claim itself, even without proof, creates a shubha (doubt) that prevents the application of the hadd. This is based on the Prophetic principle of averting hadd punishments through doubts (idra'u al-hudud bi al-shubuhat).

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): A doubt or ambiguity that prevents the application of a hadd punishment.
إقرار (iqrār): Confession; in theft, a confession of the theft is a basis for establishing the hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Two Thieves: One Absent, Testimony on Both
Inter-madhhab dispute on cutting the present thief when the absentee is not present for testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on cutting the present thief when one of two thieves is absent, explain the opposing Shafi'i argument, and evaluate the practical consequence of the dispute.

Core Idea
If two thieves steal and one is absent, the present thief is cut – absence does not block the hadd.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in hudud law: two thieves commit theft together, but when witnesses testify, one is absent. The Hanafi school holds that the present thief is still liable to have his hand cut, while the Shafi'i school, as reported in al-Wajiz, argues otherwise. Understanding this dispute clarifies how the Hanafi school prioritises established testimony over procedural delays or unproven claims.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify exactly what the Hanafi school rules in the scenario: two thieves steal, one is absent at testimony, the present thief is cut.
This is the view of Imam Abu Hanifa which he later reverted to, indicating it is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the opposing school and its view
Recognise that the Shafi'i school, through Imam al-Ghazali's al-Wajiz, holds the opposite: the present thief should not be cut.
The opposing view is based on a specific argument about the thief becoming a litigant if he claims ownership.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the reasoning: if the absent thief claims the stolen item is his property, he becomes a litigant; the present thief's cutting cannot then be based on an oath of another (the claimant).
This argument rests on procedural fairness and the requirement that the thief's guilt be established directly.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school maintains that the testimony stands against both thieves; absence or an unproven claim does not create a definitive shubha that repels the hadd.
The response is grounded in the principle that a mere claim does not override established proof.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: in a Hanafi court, the present thief loses his hand; in a Shafi'i court following this view, he does not.
This shows how the same facts lead to opposite outcomes due to methodological differences in handling claims and evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن سرقا وغاب أحدهما، فشهدا على سرقتهما قطع الآخر لكن في الوجيز ذكر خلاف هذا [١] قوله : لكن في الوجيز؛ هو كتاب في الفقه ملخص من البسيط والوسيط، والثلاثة من تأليفات حجة الإسلام محمد بن محمد الغزالي الشافعي، مؤلف إحياء العلوم، وكيمياء السعادة، المتوفى سنة (٥٠٥) خمس وخمسمئة. [٢] قوله : صار خصماً؛ حاصله: إنه لما ادعى السارق بأن المسروق ملكه صار خصماً في المال، ووقعت الخصومة بينه وبين المدعي الأول، فيندفع القطع؛ لأنه إن أتى بالبينة فقد ثبت ملكه، وارتفت السرقة التي هي عبارة عن أخذ مال الغير، وإلا لم يقم البينة وطالب بحلف الخصم، فإن نكل فذاك وإن حلف بأنه ليس ملكه فحلفه لا يوجب القطع، فإن الرجل لا يقطع بحلف غيره. [0] قوله: فإن سرقا ... الخ؛ يعني إذا سرق اثنان وغاب أحدهما وشهد شاهدان بعد غيبوبته على سرقتهما قطع الحاضر، وهذا قول أبي حنيفة له الذي رجع إليه.
If two (persons) steal and one of them is absent, then (witnesses) testify against their theft, the other (present) is cut. But in al-Wajiz, the opposite of this is mentioned. [1] Statement: 'but in al-Wajiz' – it is a book on fiqh summarized from al-Basit and al-Wasit, and the three are works of Hujjat al-Islam Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-Ghazali al-Shafi'i, author of Ihya' al-Ulum and Kimiya' al-Sa'adah, who died in the year 505. [2] Statement: 'he became a litigant' – the summary: that when the thief claimed that the stolen item is his property, he became a litigant in the property, and a dispute occurred between him and the first claimant, so the cutting is repelled. Because if he brings evidence, his ownership is established, and the theft (which is taking the property of another) is removed. Otherwise, if he does not produce evidence, he demands the oath of the opponent; if he (the opponent) refrains, that is it; and if he swears that it is not his property, his oath does not necessitate cutting, for a man is not cut by the oath of another. [0] Statement: 'if two steal...' – meaning if two steal and one of them is absent, and two witnesses testify after his absence about their theft, the present one is cut. This is the saying of Abu Hanifa which he later reverted to.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where two people steal together, but one of them is absent when the witnesses testify. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the present thief is still cut because the testimony covers both, and the absence of one does not prevent punishment of the other. However, in the Shafi'i book al-Wajiz, a different view is mentioned: that the present thief should not be cut because the claim of the thief (if he claims ownership) makes him a litigant, and the cutting cannot be based on an oath of another person. The Hanafi response is that the ruling of cutting the present thief is the view of Abu Hanifa to which he returned (presumably after earlier considering a different view). The commentary explains the Shafi'i reasoning: if the absent thief had claimed the stolen item as his own, he becomes a party to the property dispute, and his guilt is not established by the oath of the other thief. But the Hanafi position is that the testimony stands, and the present thief is cut. The reference to the footnotes clarifies that the Hanafi position is the one relied upon.

Key Terms
الوجيز (al-Wajiz): A Shafi'i fiqh book by al-Ghazali, summarizing al-Basit and al-Wasit.
خصم (khasm): Litigant; a party to a legal dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He is not cut because the absent thief's claim of ownership creates a shubha.
B) He is cut based on the testimony against both.
C) He is cut only if the owner demands cutting.
D) He is not cut until the absent thief is present.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a thief who is present at trial is cut based on testimony even if his accomplice is absent, rejecting the Shafi'i argument that a claim of ownership by the absent thief creates a litigation bar.

The Condition of Owner's Demand for Hadd
Ruling on the condition of owner's demand

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for applying the hadd for theft and identify the rhetorical device (istikhdam) used in the matn.

Core Idea
The owner's demand is a condition for the hadd — even a thief's confession alone does not trigger it.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies a procedural requirement for the hadd punishment for theft. While the thief's confession might seem sufficient, the Hanafi school conditions the application of the penalty on the owner's formal demand. The commentary also highlights a subtle linguistic point in the matn involving the rhetorical figure of istikhdam, where a single word is used in two different senses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal ruling
Read the passage and state the condition under which the hadd (cutting of the hand) is NOT applied.
The ruling is stated explicitly: 'if the owner does not demand, even if he confesses, then no cutting.'
2
Recognise the exception
Note that the thief's confession alone does not suffice; the owner's demand is an additional requirement.
This highlights the interplay between the rights of Allah (hadd) and the rights of the individual (owner).
3
Parse the pronoun references
Identify what the pronouns 'its' (in 'its owner') and 'it' (in 'confesses to it') refer to.
The commentary explains that 'its owner' refers to the stolen item, while 'confesses to it' refers to the act of theft.
4
Understand istikhdam
Recognise that the matn employs the rhetorical device istikhdam, where a word is used in two different meanings in the same context.
Here, 'al-sariqa' is used once to mean the stolen property and once to mean the theft itself.
5
Apply the ruling
Test your understanding by applying the ruling to a scenario: a thief confesses but the owner does not demand. What is the result?
The hadd is not applied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : أو لم يطالب مالكها وإن أقر هو بها فلا قطع؛ أي لم يطالب مالك السرقة، وإن أقر السارق بالسرقة. [٤]قوله : أي المسروق؛ أشار به إلى أن ضمير مالكها راجع إلى السرقة بمعنى المسروق، وأن ضمير: «بها»؛ راجع إلى السرقة بالمعنى المصدري، ففي «المتن» صنعة الاستخدام.
And his statement: 'or if the owner does not demand (the stolen property), even if he (the thief) confesses to it, then there is no cutting.' Meaning: if the owner of the theft (i.e., the stolen item) does not demand, even if the thief confesses to the theft, there is no cutting. [4] Statement: 'i.e., the stolen item' – he indicated by this that the pronoun 'its owner' refers to the theft in the sense of the stolen item, and the pronoun 'it' (in 'confesses to it') refers to the theft in the infinitive sense, so in the matn there is the figure of speech called istikhdam.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage states a straightforward procedural condition for applying the hadd punishment for theft. The ruling is that the cutting of the hand is not carried out unless the owner of the stolen property demands it. Even if the thief confesses to the theft, if the owner does not come forward and demand the punishment, the hadd is not applied. This is because the right involved includes both the right of Allah (the hadd) and the right of the individual (the stolen property). The owner's demand is necessary to initiate the legal process. The commentary also clarifies a subtle grammatical point in the matn regarding the pronoun referents: the word 'its owner' (malikuha) refers to the theft (al-sariqa) in the sense of the stolen item, while the pronoun in 'confesses to it' (aqarra biha) refers to the act of theft itself. This is a rhetorical device called istikhdam.

Key Terms
مطالبة المالك (muṭālabat al-mālik): The demand of the owner of the stolen property for the application of the hadd punishment.
استخدام (istikhdām): A rhetorical device where a single word used in two senses (here, one word referring to the stolen item and another to the act of theft).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The thief must confess.
B) The stolen property must be found.
C) The owner must demand the punishment.
D) The nisab must be met at the time of theft.

Key Takeaway
The owner's demand is a necessary condition for the hadd punishment; without it, even a confession does not result in cutting.

Ruling on Cutting the Present Thief When One of Two Thieves is Absent
Commentary on cutting the present thief when one of two thieves is absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cutting the present thief when one of two thieves is absent and explain why the absence does not create a legal doubt (shubha).

Core Idea
The absence of a co-thief does not create a legal doubt; the present thief is cut based on testimony.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya addresses a nuanced scenario in the law of theft (hadd al-sariqa): two thieves steal together, one is absent, and witnesses testify against both. The key question is whether the present thief can be punished by amputation despite the absent thief's potential claim of ownership. The final Hanafi position establishes that the present thief is cut, rejecting the argument that uncertainty prevents punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling
State the final Hanafi position: the present thief is cut when two thieves steal together and one is absent, provided witnesses testify and the owner makes a claim.
This is the mufta bihi position, agreed upon by Abu Hanifa (final view) and the Sahibayn.
2
Recognize the opposing view
Note that Abu Hanifa initially held the present thief is not cut, and this view is also found in some Shafi'i works like al-Wajiz. The opposing argument is that the absent thief's claim makes him a litigant, creating a shubha that prevents cutting.
This opposing view is based on the principle that cutting cannot be based on another's oath or potential claim.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response is that the absent thief's claim is unproven; the present thief's theft is established by testimony. A mere potential claim does not create an actual shubha sufficient to block the hadd.
The absence of the other thief does not introduce a real uncertainty because no evidence supports the absent thief's claim.
4
Determine the practical consequence
In practice, the Hanafi school cuts the present thief, while some Shafi'is would not. Also, Abu Hanifa's change of view shows the evolution within the school.
The thamarah al-khilaf is that the hadd is applied in a scenario where other schools might withhold it.

If two (thieves) stole and one of them is absent, and they testified to their theft, the other (present) one is cut. But in al-Wajiz, the contrary is mentioned, and it is reasoned that he became a litigant in the property, so how can he be cut by the oath of another? And his saying: Or if its owner did not claim, and he confessed to it, then no cutting; meaning if the owner of the stolen property does not claim, then no cutting, even if the thief confesses to the theft, because since a claim is a condition, the plaintiff's claim is necessary. [2] His saying: 'he became a litigant'... summary: When the thief claimed that the stolen property is his, he became a litigant in the property, and a dispute occurred between him and the first plaintiff, so cutting is repelled because if he brings evidence, his ownership is proven and the theft which is taking another's property is removed; otherwise he does not bring evidence and demands the opponent's oath; if he refuses, then that; but if he swears that it is not his property, his oath does not necessitate cutting, because a man is not cut by the oath of another. [3] His saying: 'did not claim its owner'... meaning the owner of the theft did not claim, even if the thief confessed.
