[0] His saying: 'If two stole...' means if two stole and one is absent, and two witnesses testify after his absence to their theft, the present one is cut, and this is the saying of Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him, to which he returned, and it is also explicitly stated in al-Minhaj... and its commentary Mughni al-Muhtaj... and the glosses of Qalyubi and Umayra... etc. But in Mughni al-Muhtaj: there is an opinion or view that he is cut, lest people take that as a pretext to repel the prescribed punishment. So from this it is known that what is in the Hanafi books is an opinion or view mentioned in the Shafi'i books, even if the relied-upon is otherwise.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya discusses the case of two thieves, one of whom is absent. The matn states that if the two testify to each other's theft, the present thief is cut. The commentator first notes an opposing view in al-Wajiz (a Shafi'i work by al-Ghazali), which argues that the present thief should not be cut because he has become a litigant (by claiming ownership) and cutting based on another's oath is not permissible. Then the commentary explains the matn's condition: if the owner does not claim the stolen property, there is no cutting even if the thief confesses, because a claim is necessary. The commentary then clarifies that the present thief is cut because the absence of the other does not create a valid legal doubt (shubha). The footnote [0] summarizes: Abu Hanifa's final position is that the present thief is cut, contrary to his initial view. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) also hold this position. The commentary also references Shafi'i works to show that the Hanafi position is not unique, but the relied-upon view in the Shafi'i school may differ. Thus, this is an intra-Hanafi dispute where the final fatwa is on cutting the present thief.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): A legal doubt that prevents the execution of the hadd punishment; here it refers to the possibility that the absent thief might have a valid claim to the stolen property.
خصومة (khusuma): The legal claim or litigation brought by the plaintiff against the thief; necessary to establish the theft for hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the present thief is cut even when the co-thief is absent, because unproven claims do not constitute legal doubt that blocks the hadd.

The Claimant in Theft: Depository, Usurper, and Others
Expansion on who can bring the theft claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which persons can bring a claim for the hadd of theft and state the Hanafi condition that the claimant must have a preservative hand.

Core Idea
The cutting of the thief requires a claim from a holder with a preservative hand.

In Hanafi criminal law, the punishment for theft (hadd) is not automatically applied upon the act; it requires a legal claim (khusuma) from a person who has a duty to preserve the stolen property. This lesson examines the classic text from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, which expands on who qualifies as a claimant, drawing a distinction between the owner and any trustee, borrower, or even a party to a usurious contract. Understanding this condition is essential for correctly applying the hadd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for cutting
Locate in the text where it states that cutting is established only by the claim of a holder with a preservative hand. This is the core condition being discussed.
The phrase 'yad hafiza' is key—it means a hand that preserves, i.e., a person legally obligated to safeguard the property.
2
List the examples given
The text provides four explicit examples: depositary (mustawda'), usurper (ghasib), party to usury (sahib riba), and borrower (musta'ir). Write them down and note the common element: each is an amin (trustee) who must preserve the property.
The examples are illustrative; the principle applies to anyone with a preservative hand.
3
Understand the rationale for each example
For each example, the commentary explains why the claim suffices: the trustee must preserve the deposit, the usurper must preserve the usurped item, the party to usury holds the usurious increase as if usurped, and the borrower must preserve the borrowed item.
The basis is the obligation to preserve, which gives legal standing to sue.
4
Distinguish from the owner's claim
Note that the text explicitly says the owner's claim is not necessary; the claim of a preservative holder suffices. This is the practical import of the ruling.
This distinguishes Hanafi law from other schools that may require the owner's direct claim.
5
Consider the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The text mentions that Abu Hanifa initially held a different view (not cutting the thief if the owner is absent), but the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is the one of the Sahibayn, which is cited here.
The ikhtilaf does not change the ruling on the claimant; it concerns a separate scenario of the absent thief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة / فصل : كيفية القطع ، وإثباته
وقطع بخصومة ذي يد حافظة كمودوع، وغاصب، وصاحب ربا، ومستعير" وهو قولهما، وكان يقول أوّلاً لا يقطعُ الحاضر ؛ لاحتمال أن يحضر الغائب فيدعى الشبهة الدارئة للحد عنهما كقصاص مشترك غابَ أحد المستحقين، فإنّه ليس للحاضر أن يستوفيه.
وجه قوله الآخر : إنّ الغيبة تمنع ثبوت السرقة على الغائب ؛ لأن القضاء على الغائب لا يجوز، فيبقى معدوماً، والمعدوم لا يورث شبهةً ولا معتبر بتوهم حدوث الشبهة. كذا في «الهداية»).
[١]قوله : وقطع بخصومة ذي يد حافظة ؛ أشار به إلى أمرين :
أحدهما : أنه لا بد للقطع من خصومة.
وثانيهما : إن خصومة مالك المال ليست بلازمة، بل تكفي خصومة من يقومُ مقامه، وهو من له على المال يد صحيحة حافظة، بأن يكون ممن يجب عليه حفظه ، فا ، فلو سُرِقَ المال من أمين تكفي خصومته ؛ لأنه يجب عليه حفظه، فله الخصومة عند السرقة، وكذا الغاصب يجب عليه حفظ المغصوب ، فلو سرق المغصوب من عنده كفت خصومته، وقس عليه غيره.
[٢] قوله : وصاحب ربا ؛ قال في «النهاية»: قال في «المحيط»: يحتمل أنه أراد به رجل باعَ عشرة دراهم بعشرين وقبض العشرين، فجاء السارق فسرق العشرين منه يقطعُ السارق بخصومته عندنا ؛ لأن هذا المال في يده بمنزلة المغصوب، والمشتري شراء فاسداً، ثمّ أنه ذكر في الكتاب ربِّ الوديعة والمغصوب منه ، ولم يذكر العاقد الآخر من عاقدي الربا ؛ لما أنه بالتسليم لم يبق له ملك ، ولا يد له.
[٣] قوله : ومستعير؛ فإنه إذا استعار إنسان شيئاً من إنسان، فسرق ذلك الشيء من عند المستعير، فله الخصومة.
Book of Theft / Section: How Cutting is Established
And cutting (is established) by the claim of a holder with a preservative hand, such as a depositary, usurper, party to usury, and borrower. And it is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). He (Abu Hanifa) initially used to say that the present (thief) is not cut, because of the possibility that the absent one will come and claim the doubt that wards off the hadd from both, like a joint qisas where one of the entitled is absent: the present one cannot exact it. The argument for the other view: absence prevents establishing theft against the absent one because judgment against the absent is not permissible, so he remains non-existent, and the non-existent does not cause a doubt, nor is the potential occurrence of doubt considered. Thus in al-Hidayah. [1] His saying: 'And cutting by the claim of a holder with a preservative hand' – he indicated two matters: one: that cutting requires a claim; two: that the claim of the owner of the property is not necessary, rather the claim of one who stands in his place suffices, i.e., one who has a valid preservative hand over the property, such as one who is obliged to preserve it. So if the property is stolen from a trustee, his claim suffices because he must preserve it, so he has the claim regarding the theft. Similarly, a usurper must preserve the usurped property, so if the usurped property is stolen from his possession, his claim suffices. And analogize the others. [2] His saying: 'and party to usury' – al-Nihaya quotes al-Muhit: it is possible he meant a man who sold ten dirhams for twenty and took possession of the twenty, then a thief stole the twenty from him; the thief is cut by his claim in our view because this property in his hand is like usurped property, and the buyer (has) a void purchase. Also, he mentioned in the book the owner of the deposit and the one from whom it was usurped, but did not mention the other party of the usurious transaction because after delivery he has neither ownership nor hand. [3] His saying: 'and borrower' – if a person borrows something from someone, then that thing is stolen from the borrower, he has the claim.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage introduces the second ruling: the hadd of theft is established only if there is a legal claim (khusuma) from someone with a preservative hand over the property. The matn lists examples: a depositary (mustawda'), a usurper (ghasib), a party to a usurious transaction (sahib riba), and a borrower (musta'ir). The commentary explains two key points: first, a claim is necessary for cutting; second, the claimant need not be the owner—any person who has a preservative hand (yad hafiza), i.e., one who is obliged to safeguard the property, can bring the claim. For each example: a trustee (amin) must preserve the deposit; a usurper must preserve the usurped item; a party to usury who took possession of the usurious increase (like buying 10 for 20 and receiving 20) holds the property as if usurped; the borrower has a duty to preserve the borrowed item. The passage also includes a repetition of the earlier dispute about the absent thief, indicating that the ruling on the claimant is based on the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), and that Abu Hanifa initially held a different view but later agreed. The relied-upon position is that the claim of such a holder suffices for the hadd.

Key Terms
يد حافظة (yad hafiza): A 'preservative hand' refers to a person who legally possesses the property and is obligated to preserve it, such as a trustee, borrower, etc.
خصومة (khusuma): A legal claim or lawsuit brought by someone who has a right over the stolen property.
أمين (amin): A trustee who is entrusted with property, such as a depositary; he has a duty to preserve it and thus can bring a claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following persons does NOT have standing to bring a claim for the hadd of theft?
A) A depositary from whose custody the property is stolen.
B) A borrower who had the item stolen while in his possession.
C) A person who merely finds the stolen property after the theft.
D) A party to a usurious contract who took the money.

Key Takeaway
The hadd of theft requires a claim from a holder with a preservative hand, not necessarily the owner.

Requirement of Khusuma for Hadd of Theft and the Co-Owner Absence Dispute
Ruling on who may bring a lawsuit for cutting the thief's hand

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state who may bring a lawsuit for the hadd of theft and explain why the absence of a co-owner does not create a shubha in the final Hanafi position.

Core Idea
A thief's hand is cut only after a lawsuit by any holder of a preserving hand — even a co-owner acting alone.

This passage from Al-Hidayah establishes a critical procedural rule: the hadd of cutting for theft is not automatically applied; it requires a formal lawsuit (khusuma) by someone who has a legal duty to preserve the stolen property. But who qualifies? The text explains that not only the owner, but also depositaries, usurpers, borrowers, and even parties to a void riba contract can bring the suit. Then it addresses a dispute within the Hanafi school: what if property is jointly owned and one co-owner is absent when theft occurs? The initial view of Abu Hanifa refused cutting based on analogy to qisas, but the final position, adopted also by the Sahibayn, allows the present co-owner's lawsuit, rejecting the mere possibility of future doubt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
The hadd of cutting requires a lawsuit (khusuma) by a person who has a 'preserving hand' (yadd hafiza) over the stolen property. This is a procedural condition.
The lawsuit establishes the theft and avoids error.
2
List the examples of custodians
The passage lists: depositary (mudi'), usurper (ghasib), party to riba (sahib riba), and borrower (musta'ir). In each case, the custodian is duty-bound to preserve the property.
These examples show that ownership is not required.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute
When property is jointly owned and one co-owner is absent, Abu Hanifa initially held that the present co-owner cannot have the thief's hand cut because of the possibility that the absent co-owner might later claim a shubha (doubt) that would avert the hadd. He later adopted the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad that the present co-owner's lawsuit suffices.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf resolved by the later view.
4
Analyze the analogy to qisas
The earlier analogy to joint qisas (where an absent heir prevents execution) is rejected. In theft, the hadd is a right of Allah, and the absent co-owner is legally non-existent, so no shubha arises from mere possibility. The principle 'al-madum la yurith shubha' applies.
The qiyas to qisas is considered invalid because the nature of the right differs.

Source Text

كتاب السرقة / فصل : كيفية القطع ، وإثباته وقَطْعٌ بِخُصومَةِ ذي يَدٍ حافِظَة كَمودوعٍ، وغاصِبٍ، وصاحِبِ رِباً، ومُستَعيرٍ وهو قولهما، وكان يقول أوّلاً لا يُقْطَعُ الحاضر لاحتمال أن يحضر الغائب فيدعي الشبهة الدارئة للحد عنهما كقصاص مشترك غاب أحد المستحقين، فإنه ليس للحاضر أن يستوفيه. وجه قوله الآخر : إن الغيبة تمنع ثبوت السرقة على الغائب لأن القضاء على الغائب لا يجوز، فيبقى معدوماً، والمعدوم لا يورث شبهة ولا معتبر بتوهم حدوث الشبهة. كذا في «الهداية». [١] قوله : وقطع بخصومة ذي يد حافظة أشار به إلى أمرين : أحدهما : أنه لا بد للقطع من خصومة. وثانيهما : إن خصومة مالك المال ليست بلازمة، بل تكفي خصومة من يقوم مقامه، وهو من له على المال يد صحيحة حافظة، بأن يكون ممن يجب عليه حفظه، فإذا سرق المال من أمين تكفي خصومته لأنه يجب عليه حفظه، فله الخصومة عند السرقة، وكذا الغاصب يجب عليه حفظ المغصوب، فلو سرق المغصوب من عنده كفت خصومته، وقس عليه غيره. [٣] قوله : ومستعير فإنه إذا استعار إنسان شيئاً من إنسان، فسرق ذلك الشيء من عند المستعير، فله الخصومة.
Book of Theft / Chapter: How the Cutting is Done and Its Proof. And cutting (the hand) is carried out upon a lawsuit by a holder of a preserving hand, such as a depositary, a usurper, a party to riba, and a borrower – and it is their (the Two Companions') view. And he (Abu Hanifa) used to say initially: The present (owner) is not cut because of the possibility that the absent (co-owner) may appear and claim a doubt that would avert the hadd for both, like in joint qisas where one of the entitled parties is absent; indeed the present one may not execute it. The proof of his other view: Absence prevents the establishment of theft against the absent because judgment against an absent person is not permissible, so he remains non-existent, and that which is non-existent does not cause a doubt, nor is consideration given to imagining the occurrence of a doubt. Thus in al-Hidayah. [1] His statement: 'And cutting by a lawsuit of a holder of a preserving hand' – he indicated two matters: One: that a lawsuit is necessary for cutting. Second: that the lawsuit of the owner of the property is not necessary; rather, the lawsuit of one who stands in his place suffices, and he is one who has a valid, preserving hand over the property, such as one who is obliged to preserve it. So if property is stolen from a trustee, his lawsuit suffices because he is obliged to preserve it, so he has the right to sue upon the theft. Likewise, the usurper is obliged to preserve the usurped property, so if the usurped property is stolen from him, his lawsuit suffices, and analogize to others. [2] His statement: 'and a party to riba' – He said in al-Nihaya: He said in al-Muhit: It is likely that he meant a man who sold ten dirhams for twenty and took possession of the twenty, then a thief came and stole the twenty from him; the thief's hand is cut by his lawsuit according to us because this property in his hand is like usurped property, and the buyer (made) a void purchase. Then he mentioned in the book the owner of the deposit and the one from whom property was usurped, and did not mention the other party to the riba transaction because upon delivery he no longer has ownership nor a hand. [3] His statement: 'and a borrower' – if a person borrows something from a person, and that thing is stolen from the borrower, he has the right to sue.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage begins the section on establishing the hadd of cutting for theft. It states a key procedural requirement: the thief's hand can only be cut after a proper lawsuit (khusuma) is brought. The lawsuit does not have to be by the owner; rather, any person who has a 'preserving hand' over the stolen property – meaning someone who is legally responsible for its preservation – can bring the suit. The text lists examples: a depositary (mudi'), a usurper (ghasib), a party to an invalid riba transaction (sahib riba), and a borrower (musta'ir). The commentary explains that these individuals have a duty to preserve the property, hence they have standing. The passage then delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a scenario where two people jointly own property, one is present and the other absent, and the property is stolen from the present owner. Abu Hanifa initially held that the present owner cannot have the thief's hand cut because the absent co-owner might later appear and claim a shubha (doubt) that would avert the hadd, similar to the rule in joint qisas if one of the heirs is absent. However, Abu Hanifa later adopted the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) that the absence does not create a valid shubha because judgment cannot be passed against an absent person, so the possibility of a future claim is irrelevant. This later view is the mufta bihi (relied upon) position. The passage thus establishes both the fundamental requirement of khusuma and the intra-madhhab resolution regarding co-ownership disputes.

Key Terms
يد حافظة (yadd hāfiẓa): A hand (possession) that carries the legal duty of preservation; the person holding it must safeguard the property and is liable for it.
خصومة (khuṣūma): A lawsuit or litigation filed to establish the theft and demand the hadd; a procedural requirement before cutting.
شبهة (shubha): A doubt or ambiguity that can prevent the hadd punishment, such as a claim of ownership or right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The thief's hand is not cut because of the absent co-owner.
B) The thief's hand is cut based on the lawsuit of the present co-owner.
C) The case is postponed until the absent co-owner appears.
D) Only the absent co-owner can bring the lawsuit.

Key Takeaway
The hadd of theft requires a lawsuit by anyone with a duty to preserve the property, and the mere absence of a co-owner does not prevent the cutting.

Custodians' Right to Sue for Theft: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Extension of hadd lawsuit rights to custodians and refutation of Shafi'i/Zufar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi school grants custodians the right to sue for the hadd of cutting and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A preserving hand implies the right to sue; without it, preservation is impossible.

In this passage, the Hanafi school extends the right to bring a lawsuit for theft (to establish the hadd of cutting) to various custodians: the lessee, the mudarib, the bailee on approval, and the pawnbroker. The text also affirms that the owner may sue when theft occurs from any of these custodians. This is contested by Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Zufar, who argue that these custodians only have authority to seek recovery (istirdad) for preservation, not to initiate a hadd lawsuit. The Hanafi response, based on necessity, demonstrates that preservation requires the ability to sue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the custodians
The passage lists four specific custodians: lessee (musta'jir), mudarib, bailee on approval (qabid 'ala som al-shira), and pawnbroker (murtahin). Note the nature of their possession: each holds the property with a preserving hand (yad hafiza) but without full ownership.
These are all cases where the custodian has an interest in safeguarding the property.
2
Understand the Hanafi rationale
The Hanafi school argues that the authority to preserve (wilayat al-hifz) cannot be achieved except by restoring the hand, which in the context of theft requires a lawsuit. Thus, the right to sue is inherent in the preserving hand.
This is a rule of necessity: the end (preservation) justifies the means (lawsuit for hadd).
3
State the Shafi'i/Zufar objection
Al-Shafi'i and Zufar concede that custodians have authority to seek recovery (istirdad) but argue this is solely for preservation, not for establishing the hadd. They see the hadd as a right of Allah requiring a direct plaintiff with full ownership or specific authority.
Their view elevates the spiritual/divine nature of the hadd over the practical need.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response ("Qulna") is that preservation itself—the Quranic command to protect property—entails the lawsuit. Once theft is proven through the suit, the text (Qur'an) mandates cutting, irrespective of whether the plaintiff is the owner or custodian.
The rebuttal is a logical inference: the lawsuit is a necessary means to fulfill the preservation duty.
5
Note the special case of the pledger
The pledger (rahn) can only sue after the debt is paid, because while the pledge exists, his right to the property is suspended. This exception underscores the principle that the right to sue depends on the right to recover the property.
This aligns with the owner's right: he can sue for theft from the custodian, but the pledger's right is deferred.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[٢] قوله : ومضارب - بكسر الراء - من المضاربة : وهو عقد شركة بأن يكون المال لأحدهما، والعمل من الآخر، والربح مشترك بينهما على حسب ما قرراه، فصاحب المال يقال له : رب المال، والعامل هو المضارب، فإذا سرق من المال شيء من عنده تكفي خصومته. [٣] قوله : وقابض أي من قبض المال على إرادة الشراء ولم يشتره. [٤] قوله : ومرتهن أي من أخذ شيئاً رهناً، فسرق منه، قال الهروي في «شرحه»: وكذا متولي الوقف والأب والوصي والوكيل، وفي الكـل خلاف الشافعي وزفر إذ لا خصومة لهؤلاء في الاسترداد عنده وعند زفره، وإن كانت لهم ولاية الاسترداد، لكنه للحفظ لا للقطع. قلنا : ولاية الحفظ لا تتأتى إلا بإعادة اليد وذا لا يتأتى إلا بالخصومة، وحينئذ تظهر السرقة بالتبعية، وهي موجبة للقطع في نفسها بالنص فيستوفي القطع. [٦] قوله : من سرق منهم قيل : هو مفعول ما لم يسم فاعله لقوله : «قطع»، فعلى هذا ضمير منهم يرجع إلى جميع المذكورين، والأصح أنه مفعول لخصومة المالك، وضمير «منهم» راجع إلى ما سوى المالك، والمعنى أنه يقطع في السرقة من المودع وغيره ممن له يد حافظة بدون ملك بخصومة مالك المال أيضاً كما يقطع بخصومة هؤلاء.
And a lessee, a mudarib, one who takes possession with the intention of purchase, and a pawnbroker, and by the lawsuit of the owner (the thief) of those from whom he stole. [1] His statement: 'and a lessee' – that he hires an animal or the like from a person, and it is stolen from him; he has the right to sue because he is a holder of a preserving hand. [2] His statement: 'and a mudarib' – with kasra on the ra' – from mudaraba: it is a partnership contract where the capital belongs to one and the work to the other, and the profit is shared between them as agreed; the capital owner is called 'rabb al-mal' and the worker is the mudarib; if something of the capital is stolen from him, his lawsuit suffices. [4] His statement: 'and a pawnbroker' – meaning he who takes something as a pledge, and it is stolen from him. Al-Harawi said in his Sharh: 'Likewise the trustee of a waqf, the father, the executor, and the agent; and concerning all of these there is disagreement with al-Shafi'i and Zufar, for they hold that these have no right to sue for recovery, even though they have the authority to seek recovery, but it is for preservation, not for cutting (the hand).' We (Hanafis) say: The authority of preservation cannot be achieved except by restoring the hand, and that cannot be achieved except through the lawsuit; then the theft appears incidentally, and it necessitates cutting in itself by the text, so the cutting is executed. [5] His statement: 'the owner' – and he is the depositor (with kasra on the dal), the lender, the lessor, the pledger, the capital owner, the seller, and the one from whom property was usurped. He said in al-Hidayah: 'Except that the pledger's hand is only cut by his lawsuit while the pledge exists after the debt is paid, because he has no right to claim the property without it.' [6] His statement: 'of those from whom he stole' – it is said it is a passive participle (maf'ul ma lam yusamma fa'iluhu) because of his statement 'cut'; thus the pronoun 'minhum' refers to all those mentioned. The sounder view is that it is the object of 'the owner's lawsuit', and the pronoun 'minhum' refers to other than the owner, meaning that the thief is cut in the case of theft from a depositary and others who have a preserving hand but no ownership, by the lawsuit of the owner as well, just as he is cut by the lawsuit of these custodians.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage continues the list of individuals whose lawsuit is sufficient for the hadd of cutting. It adds the lessee (musta'jir), the mudarib (investment partner), the bailee on approval (qabid 'ala som al-shira'), and the pawnbroker (murtahin). It also states that the original owner can bring the lawsuit when property is stolen from any of these custodians. The commentary explains each: the lessee has a preserving hand because he must take care of the hired item; the mudarib is responsible for the capital; the bailee on approval is in possession with intent to purchase; the pawnbroker holds the pledge for security. The text then addresses a cross-madhhab dispute: al-Shafi'i and Zufar argue that custodians like these cannot bring a lawsuit for the hadd of cutting; they can only seek recovery (istirdad) for the purpose of preservation, not for the hadd. The Hanafi response (Qulna) is that the authority of preservation necessarily requires restoring the hand, which can only be done through a lawsuit. The lawsuit then incidentally reveals the theft, and since the text (Qur'an) makes theft a cause for cutting, the hadd is executed. The passage also clarifies the grammatical point of the phrase 'min saraqa minhum' – the sounder view is that it means the owner's lawsuit suffices when theft occurs from any of the custodians. A special note from al-Hidayah: the pledger (rahn) can only bring a lawsuit after the debt is paid, because during the pledge he has no right to demand the property.

Key Terms
مضارب (muḍārib): The working partner in a mudaraba (silent partnership) who manages the capital; he has a preserving hand over the capital.
مرتهن (murtahin): The pledgee who holds a pledge as security for a debt; he has a preserving hand over the pledged item.
ولاية الاسترداد (wilāyat al-istirdād): The authority to seek recovery of stolen property; in this context, Shafi'i and Zufar limit custodians to this only, not to suing for the hadd.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, any person with a preserving hand over stolen property can initiate the lawsuit for the hadd of cutting, ensuring the Shari'ah's deterrent effect is not thwarted.

The Condition of a Claim for Theft Hadd
Explains why the victim's claim is necessary for theft hadd and distinguishes from zina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires the victim's claim for the hadd of theft and differentiate it from the case of zina.

Core Idea
The victim's claim removes doubt; without it, the hadd is not imposed.

The hadd of theft is a serious punishment, but it is not applied mechanically. In this passage, the commentator explains that even though cutting is a right of Allah, it is conditional upon the victim's formal claim. The reason is rooted in the principle of avoiding punishment where there is doubt. The victim knows best whether the property might actually belong to the thief or a relative, and without a claim, that doubt remains. The author also contrasts this with zina, where the woman's absence does not block the hadd, because she is presumed satisfied with the act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Condition
The passage states that the victim's claim (da'wa) is a condition (shart) for establishing theft and imposing the hand-cutting punishment. Even though the hadd is a right of Allah, it is not activated without this condition.
Note that 'shart' here means a condition for implementation, not for the sin itself.
2
Understand the Rationale (The Proof)
The proof given is rational (istihsan): the victim has superior knowledge of the true ownership. The property might belong to the thief through inheritance or to a close relative unknown to the thief. Thus, without a victim's claim, there is a real possibility that the punishment would be unjustly applied.
This is a doubt (shubha) that triggers the principle 'al-hadd yudra'u bil-shubahat' (hadd punishments are repelled by doubts).
3
Compare with the Case of Zina
The author addresses an imagined objection: why does the absence of the woman in zina not also prevent the hadd? The answer is that the woman in zina is considered satisfied (radiyah) with the act, so any later claim she might make to drop the hadd is considered suspicious (mutahhamah). Thus, her absence does not create a valid doubt.
This contrast highlights that the doubt in theft is objective (possible ownership), while in zina the doubt is subjective (her claim is not credible).
4
Derive the Principle
The overall principle: For hadd punishments, any credible doubt about the fulfilment of a necessary condition prevents the punishment. The victim's claim is necessary to remove the doubt of ownership, which is a real possibility. In zina, no such credible doubt exists from the woman's absence because she is assumed to be a willing participant.
This principle ensures that the severe hadd punishments are only applied when certainty is high.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن الدعوى شرط لظهور السرقة وقطع اليد وإن كان من حقوق الله تعالى؛ لأنه لا شك أن المسروق منه أعرف بحقيقة الحال من الشهود وكذا من السارق المقر به إذ يمكن أن يكون ملكاً للسارق بطريق الإرث أو ملكاً لذي رحم محرم وهو غير عالم به ففي ترك المسروق منه الدعوى وكذا في غيبته عدم وجوب القطع مظنة له. أما غيبة المزنية وإن كان فيها توهم أنها لو كانت حاضرة ادعت أمراً يسقط الحد فلا اعتبار به لأن المزنية راضية بالزنى. فهذا هو الفرق الذي وعدته في باب شهادة الزنا.
قوله: وإن كان; الواو وصلية: أي القطع وإن كان من حقوق الله; لكنه لا يترتب ما لم تظهر السرقة ولا تظهر ما لم توجد الخصومة. قوله: إن المسروق منه; أي الذي سرق من عنده مالكاً كان أو غيره. قوله: من الشهود; أي الذين يؤدون الشهادة بالسرقة. قوله: وهو; أي والحال أن السارق لا يعلم بكونه موروثاً له أو مملوكاً لذي رحم محرم فيقر بالسرقة. قوله: مظنة...الخ; يعني لما لم يخاصم المسروق منه وهو مالك المال أو صاحب يد حافظة مع السارق ولم يدع عليه السرقة أو غاب احتمل عدم وجوب القطع عليه لاحتمال أن يكون المال مملوكاً للسارق أو لذي رحم محرم منه فلا يجب القطع ما لم يخاصم المسروق منه. قوله: أما غيبة المزنية.. الخ; دفع دخل مقدر تقرير الدخل: إن ترك مخاصمة المسروق منه وغيبوبته لما لم يوجب القطع في السرقة ينبغي أن يكون الأمر كله في الزنا مع أنه ليس كذلك فإنهم لو شهدوا بالزنا وهي غائبة وجب على الزاني الحد مع احتمال أن تدعي المزنية أمراً يسقط الحد عنهما بعد حضورها وحاصل الدفع: إن المزنية راضية بالزنا فتكون في دعواها ما يسقط الحد متهمة فلا يعتبر هذا الاحتمال. قوله: راضية بالزنى; لأن الزنى وإن كان ابتداؤه بالإكراه لكن لابد فيه من الرضى انتهاء عند وجود اللذة. قوله: الذي وعدته; حيث قال في شرح قول المصنف: فإن شهدوا بزنى غائبة حد وبسرقة من غائب لا لشرطية الدعوى في السرقة دون الزنى على ما يأتي الفرق في كتاب السرقة.
Know that the claim is a condition for the occurrence of theft and the cutting of the hand, even though it is from the rights of Allah; because there is no doubt that the victim knows the reality of the situation better than the witnesses, and likewise better than the thief who confesses, since it is possible that the property belongs to the thief through inheritance, or belongs to a close relative, and he does not know that. So if the victim abandons the claim, and likewise if he is absent, there is no obligation to cut; it is a likely situation [for non-requirement]. As for the absence of the woman in zina, even if it is imagined that if she were present she would claim something that drops the hadd, it is not considered, because the woman in zina is satisfied with the fornication. So this is the difference that I promised in the chapter on the testimony of zina. [Footnotes: He said: wa in kana; the waw is connective: that is, the cutting, even though it is from the rights of Allah, but it is not implemented unless the theft is manifested, and it is not manifested unless there is litigation. He said: inna al-masruq minhu; i.e., the one from whom it is stolen, whether owner or other. He said: min al-shuhud; i.e., those who bear witness to the theft. He said: wa huwa; i.e., and the thief does not know that it is inherited by him or owned by a close relative, so he confesses to theft. He said: mazinnah... etc.; i.e., since the victim, who is the owner of the property or the guardian, did not litigate with the thief and did not claim theft, or he was absent, it is possible that there is no obligation of cutting on him; because of the possibility that the property is owned by the thief or his close relative, so the cutting is not obligatory unless the victim litigates. He said: ama ghibat al-mazniyyah... etc.; a rebuttal of an imagined objection. The objection's presentation: Since the abandonment of litigation and absence of the victim did not necessitate the cut in theft, the matter should be the same in zina, but it is not, for if they bear witness to zina while the woman is absent, the hadd is obligatory on the man, despite the possibility that after her presence she might claim something that drops the hadd from both. The summary of the rebuttal: The woman in zina is satisfied, so in her claim of something that drops the hadd, she is accused, so this possibility is not considered. He said: radiyat bil-zina; because zina, even if its beginning is by force, must include satisfaction at its end when pleasure occurs. He said: alladhi wa'adtuh; where he said in the commentary on the author's statement: 'For if they bear witness to zina of an absent woman, she is punished, but for theft of an absent person, not,' due to the necessity of a claim in theft but not in zina, as the difference will come in the book of theft.]
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on a section on theft. It states a fundamental rule: the victim's claim (da'wa) is a necessary condition for establishing theft and imposing the hadd of hand-cutting, even though cutting is a right of Allah. The reason is that the victim is most knowledgeable about the true state of affairs—better than witnesses or the thief's confession. The property might actually belong to the thief through inheritance, or it might belong to a close relative of the thief without the thief knowing. Therefore, if the victim does not bring a claim or is absent, the cut is not imposed. This is based on the principle of avoiding punishment where there is a doubt (shubha). The author then addresses a possible objection regarding the case of zina: Why doesn't the absence of the woman prevent the hadd for zina, as it does for theft? The answer is that the woman in zina is satisfied (radiyah) with the act, so if she later claims something to drop the hadd, she is accused (mutahhamah). Thus, her absence does not create a valid doubt. This is the difference promised in the section on the testimony of zina.

Key Terms
دعوى (da'wa): The formal claim (lawsuit) by the victim against the thief regarding the stolen property.
مظنة (mazinnah): A likely situation (of non-requirement of cutting) arising from the absence of the victim's claim.
مسروق منه (masruq minhu): The person from whom the property was stolen, i.e., the victim, who may be the owner or a custodian.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school require the victim's claim (da'wa) for the hadd of theft to be imposed?
A) Because the hadd of theft is a pure right of Allah and cannot be waived.
B) Because the victim has the best knowledge of the property's true ownership, and there may be a doubt that prevents the hadd.
C) Because the thief must confess first.
D) Because the hadd of theft requires the same procedure as zina.

Key Takeaway
The victim's claim is a necessary condition for the hadd of theft to eliminate the doubt of ownership, unlike zina where the woman's satisfaction nullifies similar doubt.

Theft from a Punished Thief: When the Hadd is Not Applied
Ruling on theft from a thief after amputation, with two proofs from Hanafi jurisprudence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on stealing from a thief after his hand has been cut and explain the two reasons why the hadd is not applied.

Core Idea
After the thief's hand is cut, the stolen property loses its inviolability (isma), and no hadd is applied to the second thief.

This lesson examines a nuanced case within the law of theft (sariqa). While theft generally triggers the hadd amputation, the Hanafi school delineates exceptions based on the protected status of property. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies that stealing from a thief who has already been punished alters the legal landscape because the property no longer enjoys full inviolability (isma). This ruling demonstrates how the Hanafi school prioritizes the condition of the property's protection over the mere act of taking.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the general rule: 'No cut is imposed on one who steals from a thief who has been cut.' Understand this as an exception to the standard hadd for theft.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
The ruling depends on timing: if the second theft occurs before the first thief's hand is cut, the second thief is cut; if after, he is not. Recognize that the first thief is treated like a usurper (ghasib) before the cut.
3
Understand isma (protection)
Define isma as the inviolability of property that makes theft eligible for hadd. Note that after the first thief's hand is cut, the property loses its isma in relation to both the original owner and the first thief.
4
Analyze the two reasons
The text gives two reasons why the hadd is not applied: (1) the first thief's hand after cutting is no longer a protected hand (like that of an owner, guarantor, or trustee), so his claim is not valid for cutting; (2) theft only incurs hadd when it targets protected property (ma'sum), and after the cut the property is no longer ma'sum.
5
Connect to the principle
The hadd requires both a protected hand (for the claim) and protected property. The absence of either after the first cut removes the basis for the second hadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عطف على الضمير المستكن في قوله وقطع قوله لا من سرق من سارق قطع أي لا يقطع بطلب المالك والسارق لو سرق من سارق بعد القطع لما سيأتي من سقوط عصمته.
قوله: على الضمير المستكن; بتشديد النون على صيغة اسم الفاعل أي المستتر وهو ضمير الفاعل الراجع إلى السارق وهذا يرشدك إلى بطلان قول من قال في شرح ذلك الكلام إن قوله من سرق منهم فاعل لقوله قطع.
قوله: لا من سرق... الخ; قال في النهاية حاصله إن المال إذا سرق من السارق فلا يخلو إما أن تكون السرقة الثانية قبل قطع السارق الأول أو بعده فإن كانت قبله فحينئذ يقطع الثاني بخصومة الأول لأن السارق الأول بمنزلة الغاصب وإن كان الأول قد قطعت يده فالسرقة لم تنعقد موجبة للقطع بوجهين إحداهما إن يد السارق لم تبق من الأيدي التي ذكرنا من ملك أو ضمان أو وديعة فخصومة من هذه صفته لا تعتبر في القطع وثانيهما إن السرقة إنما تنعقد موجبة للقطع إذا صادفت مالاً معصوماً ولم يبق المال معصوماً بعد القطع في حق المالك ولا في حق السارق الأول إلى هذا أشار في المبسوط والأسرار والمحيط.
قوله: لما سيأتي من سقوط عصمة; أي عصمة المال بخلاف ما إذا سرق من السارق الأول قبل قطع يد الأول أو بعدها ورد عنه الحد بشبهة لأن سقوط التقوم ضرورة القطع ولم يوجد فصار الأول كالغاصب كذا في الهداية.
Then he turned to the implicit pronoun in his saying 'wa qat'' and said: 'La man saraqa min sariqin quti'a' meaning that he is not cut at the request of the owner and the thief if he steals from a thief after the cut, due to what will come regarding the fall of his protection. [Footnotes: His saying: 'ala al-damir al-mustakinn; with shadda on the nun, meaning the hidden pronoun, which is the subject pronoun referring to the thief. This indicates the invalidity of the saying of one who said in the commentary of that statement that 'man saraqa minhum' is the subject of 'quti'a'. His saying: 'la man saraqa...' etc.; al-Nihaya said: its summary is that if property is stolen from the thief, either the second theft is before the cut of the first thief or after it. If before, then the second thief is cut at the claim of the first, because the first thief is like a usurper. But if the first has had his hand cut, the theft does not become obligatory for cutting in two ways: first, the hand of the thief is not among the hands we mentioned, whether ownership, guarantee, or trust, so the claim of one with such status is not considered for cutting. Second, theft only becomes obligatory for cutting when it befalls protected property, and the property is no longer protected after the cut with respect to the owner nor with respect to the first thief. This is indicated in al-Mabsut, al-Asrar, and al-Muhit. His saying: 'lima say'ati min suqut ismatihi'; i.e., the isma (protection) of the property, unlike if he steals from the first thief before or after the cut of his hand, and the hadd is repelled from him by a doubt, because the fall of value is a necessity of cutting, and it did not occur, so the first becomes like a usurper. Thus in al-Hidayah.]
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage discusses a secondary ruling: 'No cut is imposed on one who steals from a thief who has been cut.' The author explains that if the second theft occurs after the first thief's hand has been cut, the second thief is not subject to amputation because the property of the first thief after the cut is no longer protected (isma). The thief's hand after cutting is not considered among the protected hands (like that of an owner, guarantor, or trustee). Moreover, theft is only punishable by cut when it targets protected property; after the cut, the property is no longer ma'sum in relation to the original owner or the first thief. However, if the second theft occurs before the first thief's hand is cut, the second thief is cut at the claim of the first thief because the first thief is treated like a usurper (ghasib), and the property is still considered protected. This ruling is found in al-Mabsut, al-Asrar, and al-Muhit, and is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
عصمة (isma): Inviolability or protection of property; the quality that makes theft punishable by hadd.
غاصب (ghasib): Usurper; one who unlawfully takes possession of property with full awareness.
قطع (qat'): The amputation of the hand as a punishment for theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person steals from a thief who has already had his hand cut, what is the ruling for the second thief in the Hanafi school?
A) He is also cut because theft from a thief is still theft.
B) He is not cut because the stolen property is no longer protected (ma'sum) after the first cut.
C) He is cut only if the first thief reclaims the property.
D) He is cut if the theft occurred before the first thief's cut.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the hadd of theft is not applied if the stolen property has lost its inviolability (isma) due to a prior hadd punishment on the thief.

Cutting the Hand of a Confessing Slave Thief: Hanafi Internal Differences
Ruling on slave thief's confession with Hanafi internal differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on cutting a confessing slave thief and identify the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
A slave's confession to theft triggers cutting only if permitted or property consumed — not according to Abu Hanifa.

The Hanafi school has a well-known internal dispute regarding the cutting of a slave's hand based on his confession to theft. The disagreement hinges on the validity of a slave's confession in matters of hudud and the impact on the master's property. This lesson unpacks the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Zufar, and clarifies the operative ruling for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the general subject
Identify the core issue: whether a slave's confession to theft (with the stolen property returned to the owner) necessitates the hadd punishment of cutting the hand.
The passage explicitly addresses the case where the stolen property is returned to its owner.
2
Present Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that the slave is cut without any differentiation — regardless of whether the slave is permitted to trade (ma'dhun) or restricted (mahjur), and regardless of whether the stolen property is still extant or consumed.
This is stated in the matn: 'this, according to Abu Hanifa, is without differentiation.'
3
Present the Sahibayn's (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) position
They differentiate: if the slave is permitted (ma'dhun), he is cut and must return the property (or guarantee it if consumed). If the slave is restricted (mahjur), he is cut only if the stolen property is consumed; if extant, no cutting and the property goes to the master.
The passage explains that for the restricted slave, confession to a consumed property is valid because only cutting is involved.
4
Present Zufar's position
Zufar generally rejects cutting based on confession; he requires testimony (shahada) for cutting. He allows returning the property if extant, but no cutting.
Zufar's view is mentioned in multiple places: 'and Zufar said: The property is returned and he is not cut.'
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that of the Sahibayn: cutting is applied to a permitted slave or a restricted slave if the property is consumed. This is preferred because it balances the rights of the master and the victim.
The thamarah al-khilaf: a confessing permitted slave is cut, while a restricted slave with extant property is not cut (property goes to master).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة / فصل : كيفية القطع ، وإثباته
وقطع عبد أقر بسرقة وردت إلى مالكها : هذا عند أبي حنيفة من غير تفصيل وعند زفر لا يقطع من غير تفصيل
قوله : عبد ؛ أي مكلّف، فلو كان صغيراً، وأقر بالسرقة لم يقطع ، ويرد المال لو قائماً وكان مأذوناً ، وإن هالكاً يضمن، وإن كان محجوراً وصَدَّقه المولى يَرُدُّ المالَ إلى المسروق منه لو قائماً ، ولو هالكاً فلا ضمان.
قوله : أقرّ بسرقة ؛ الجملة صفة لعبد ولو قامت عليه بينة يقطع أيضاً بالطريق الأولى.
قوله : وردت ؛ أي السرقة بمعنى المسروق، وهذا إذا كان المسروق قائماً بعينه عند العبد السارق.
قوله : من غير تفصيل ؛ أي بين العبد والمحجور وبين المأذون ، قال في «النهاية»: قال في «المبسوط» : إذا أقرَّ العبد بسرقة فلا يخلو إما أن يكون مأذوناً له أو محجوراً، وكل وجه على وجهين، إما أن يكون المال مستهلكاً أو قائماً بعينه في يده.
فإن كان مأذوناً أقرَّ بمستهلك فعليه القطع عند علمائنا الثلاثة
وإن كان المال قائماً بعينه يقطع ويرد المال على المسروق عندنا، وقال زفر : يُرَدُّ المالُ ولا يقطع. وإن كان محجوراً عليه.
فإن أقر بسرقة مستهلك قطعت يده إلا على قول زفر .
وإن أقرّ بسرقة مال قائم فعلى قول أبي حنيفة يقطع ويرد المال، وعلى قول أبي يوسف لا يقطع والمال للمولى، وعند محمد وزفر لا يقطع والمال للمولى.
قوله : لا يقطع ؛ فعنده لا قطع على العبد إلا بالشهادة، وأما إقراره بالسرقة فلا يوجب القطع محجوراً كان أو مأذونا ، قال في «الهداية»: «لأن الأصل عنده أن إقرار العبد على نفسه بالحدود والقصاص لا يصح؛ لأنه يَرِدُ على نفسه وطَرَفِهِ، وكلُّ ذلك مال المولى، والإقرار على الغير غير مقبول»
لأن إقرار العبد بالحدودِ والقصاص لا يصح عنده وإن كان مأذوناً، فإن الإذن لم يتناولهما، أما في رد المال، فإن كان مأذوناً يصح، فيرد المال، وإن كان محجوراً لا.
وأما عندهما فإن كان مأذوناً يقطع ويرد المال. وإن كان محجوراً، فالمسروق إن كان هالكاً يصح إقراره ؛ لأنَّ الواجب ليس إلا القطع، وإقراره به صحيح.
وإن كان قائماً ، فعند أبي حنيفة يقطع ويرد المسروق.
قوله : يصح ؛ وذلك لأن إقرار المأذون بالمال صحيح لكونه مسلّطاً عليه من جهة المالك المولى فيؤاخذ بالمال المقرَّبه للمسروق منه إن كان قائماً بعينه، وبضمانه إن كان مستهلكاً.
بخلاف العبد المحجور، فإنّ إقراره بالمال غير صحيح، فإن ماله كله لمولى، ولم يسلّطه على التجارة وتوابعها فلا يجب على العبد المحجور المقر بالسرقة شيء لا القطع ولا الضمان، وهذا إذا كذبه المولى، فإن أقر بسرقة شيء بعينه قائم وصدقه المولى بأن المال مال المسروق منه يرد المال إليه.
قوله : يقطع ؛ لأن إقرار العبدِ بالحدودِ والقصاص صحيح عندهما، وعند أبي حنيفة له أيضاً، فيقطعُ بناءً على صحة إقراره، وإقرار المأذون بالمال أيضاً صحيح، فيؤخذ به ويرد المال كما قال به زفر.
قوله : لأن الواجب... الخ ؛ حاصله : إنّ المسروق إذا كان هالكاً فإقراره بالسرقة إقرار بما يوجب القطع ، وإقرار العبد به صحيح مطلقاً، ولا يعتبر إقرار المحجور بالمال، ولا يجتمع القطع مع الضمان على ما يأتي.
قوله : فعند أبي حنيفة ... الخ ؛ اعترض بعضُهم هاهنا بأنه لا يخلو عن تكرار ، فإنه قد ذكر مذهبه سابقا بقوله : هذا عند أبي حنيفة له من غير تفصيل. وأجيب عنه : بأنه إنّما أعاده هاهنا دفعاً لِمَا عسى أن يتوهم أنه لا فرق عنده بين القائم وبين الهالك، كما لا فرق عنده بين المأذون والمحجور.
The Book of Theft / Chapter: The Manner of Cutting and Its Establishment
(A slave who confessed to theft and it was returned to its owner: this, according to Abu Hanifa, is without differentiation, and according to Zufar, he is not cut without differentiation.)
His statement: 'slave'; meaning legally responsible, so if he were a minor and confessed to theft, he is not cut, and the property is returned if it is still extant and he is permitted (to trade), and if it is consumed he guarantees it, and if he is restricted and the master confirms him, the property is returned to the one from whom it was stolen if it is extant, and if it is consumed there is no guarantee.
His statement: 'confessed to theft'; the phrase is an adjective for 'slave', and if evidence is established against him, he is also cut, a fortiori.
His statement: 'and it was returned'; meaning the theft, i.e., the stolen property, and this is when the stolen property is still extant in the possession of the thieving slave.
His statement: 'without differentiation'; i.e., between the slave and the restricted and between the permitted. He said in al-Nihaya: He said in al-Mabsut: When the slave confesses to theft, it is either that he is permitted or restricted, and each aspect has two faces: either the property is consumed or is still extant in his hand.
If he is permitted and confessed to a consumed (property), then cutting is upon him according to our three scholars.
And if the property is still extant, he is cut and the property is returned to the one from whom it was stolen according to us, and Zufar said: The property is returned and he is not cut. And if he is restricted.
If he confessed to a consumed theft, his hand is cut except according to the statement of Zufar.
And if he confessed to a theft of extant property, then according to Abu Hanifa's statement, he is cut and the property is returned, and according to Abu Yusuf's statement, he is not cut and the property belongs to the master, and according to Muhammad and Zufar, he is not cut and the property belongs to the master.
His statement: 'he is not cut'; according to him, there is no cutting of the slave except by testimony, and as for his confession to theft, it does not necessitate cutting, whether he is restricted or permitted. He said in al-Hidayah: 'Because the principle according to him is that the slave's confession against himself regarding hudud and qisas is not valid; because it pertains to himself and his limb, and all of that is the property of the master, and confession against others is not accepted.'
Because the slave's confession regarding hudud and qisas is not valid according to him, even if he is permitted, for permission does not encompass them. As for the return of property, if he is permitted, it is valid, so he returns the property, and if he is restricted, not.
And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), if he is permitted, he is cut and returns the property. And if he is restricted, then the stolen property, if it is consumed, his confession is valid; because the obligation is only cutting, and his confession to it is valid.
And if it is extant, then according to Abu Hanifa, he is cut and the stolen (property) is returned.
His statement: 'is valid'; and that is because the confession of the permitted slave regarding property is valid because he is authorized over it by the master owner, so he is held liable for the property confessed to the one from whom it was stolen if it is extant, and for its guarantee if it is consumed.
Contrary to the restricted slave, for his confession regarding property is not valid; for all his property belongs to the master, and he has not been authorized for trade and its subsidiary matters. So nothing is obligatory upon the restricted slave who confessed to theft: neither cutting nor guarantee. This is if the master denies him. But if he confessed to a specific extant stolen thing and the master confirms him that the property is the property of the one from whom it was stolen, the property is returned to him.
His statement: 'he is cut'; because the slave's confession regarding hudud and qisas is valid according to them, and according to Abu Hanifa also, so he is cut based on the validity of his confession. And the confession of the permitted slave regarding property is also valid, so it is taken into account and the property is returned, as Zufar said.
His statement: 'because the obligation...' etc.; its summary: When the stolen property is consumed, his confession to theft is a confession to what necessitates cutting, and the slave's confession to it is absolutely valid. And the confession of the restricted slave regarding property is not considered, and cutting and guarantee do not combine, as will come.
His statement: 'so according to Abu Hanifa...' etc.; some objected here that it is not free from repetition, for he had mentioned his madhhab earlier with his statement: 'this, according to Abu Hanifa, is without differentiation.' And it was answered: He only repeated it here to repel any possible misunderstanding that he makes no distinction between the extant and the consumed, just as he makes no distinction between the permitted and the restricted.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling on cutting the hand of a slave who confesses to theft. The matn states that according to Abu Hanifa, the slave is cut without differentiation (between being permitted or restricted), while Zufar says he is not cut without differentiation. The commentary then clarifies the conditions: the slave must be legally responsible; a minor is not cut. If the slave is permitted (madhun) and the stolen property is still extant, he is cut and must return it; if consumed, he is cut and must guarantee it. If the slave is restricted (mahjur) and the master confirms his confession, the property is returned if extant; otherwise no liability. The key issue is that Abu Hanifa considers the slave's confession regarding hudud and qisas invalid because it affects the master's property, so the slave is not cut based on confession alone but can be cut by evidence. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that a permitted slave's confession is valid for cutting, while a restricted slave's confession is valid only if the stolen property is consumed (since only cutting is involved). Zufar generally rejects cutting based on confession. The commentary further explains that Abu Hanifa's repetition of his position is to clarify that he distinguishes between extant and consumed property, contrary to a possible misunderstanding.

Key Terms
عبد ('abd): A male slave, legally capable of criminal liability but under the ownership of a master.
إقرار (iqrar): Confession; a juridical admission of a right or obligation.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade and legal transactions.
محجور (mahjur): A slave restricted from trading, whose affairs are managed by the master.
قطع (qat'): Cutting (the hand) as a hadd punishment for theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He is always cut regardless of circumstances.
B) He is cut only if he is permitted to trade.
C) He is not cut based on confession; only evidence can lead to cutting.
D) He is cut only if the stolen property is still extant.

Key Takeaway
A slave's confession to theft triggers cutting only in specific cases according to the Sahibayn; Abu Hanifa rejects cutting on confession entirely.

Slave's Confession to Theft: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Amputation and Restitution
Intra-Hanafi dispute on slave's confession to theft and its consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on a slave's confession to theft and apply the root/subsidiary analysis to determine the rulings on amputation and restitution.

Core Idea
A slave's confession to theft splits Hanafi masters on whether amputation and property restitution are separable or inseparable.

When a slave confesses to theft, the Hanafi school divides on the consequences. This passage presents the intra-Hanafi dispute among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, focusing on whether the slave is authorized or restricted, whether the stolen item is extant or destroyed, and whether the master confirms the confession. The root of the disagreement lies in whether amputation (qat') and property restitution (radd al-mal) are independent rulings or one is subsidiary to the other.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify Abu Hanifa's ruling: for an authorized slave (ma'dhun), he is amputated and returns property. For a restricted slave (mahjur), if the item is extant, he is amputated and returns it; if destroyed, only amputated. If the master denies, the restricted slave suffers neither amputation nor restitution.
Abu Hanifa sees amputation as the root (asl) and property as subsidiary (taba').
2
Identify the opponents and their views
Abu Yusuf holds that a restricted slave is amputated but does not return property (each ruling independent). Muhammad holds that neither amputation nor restitution occurs (property is root, so invalid confession voids both).
Zufar's concern about slave self-harm is rejected as rare.
3
Follow the opponents' proof
Abu Yusuf separates the two confessions: amputation valid, property invalid (against master). Muhammad argues that if property confession fails, amputation cannot stand. Zufar warns of potential abuse, but the majority dismiss this as rare.
The free man example (confession of theft from third party) is used to show separability.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response (Abu Hanifa's rebuttal)
Abu Hanifa insists that amputation and restitution can be separated, citing the free man example where amputation occurs without restitution. He argues that amputation is not dropped even if the confessor desires only property, proving its primacy.
The rarity of slave self-harm is not a basis for law.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
For a restricted slave with an extant stolen item and master denial: Abu Hanifa orders both amputation and restitution; Abu Yusuf orders amputation only; Muhammad orders neither. If the master confirms, all agree on amputation.
The mufta bihi is not specified, but the commentary defends Abu Hanifa's view.

And according to Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, he is not amputated and does not return it. [2] So we say to Zufar: His confession to that which necessitates the loss of his life or limbs, even if it harms the master, is not prohibited because his harm outweighs the master's harm. And if it occurs to your mind that the evil nature of some slaves reaches a point where they prefer to destroy themselves to harm their masters, that is a rare matter upon which rulings should not be built. [1] His saying: 'and according to Abu Yusuf etc.'; the rationale of his saying: The restricted slave confessed to two things: to amputation and to property. The first: his confession to it is valid; the second: it is a confession against the master, so it is not valid with respect to him. Now if you ask: How is amputation obligatory without the obligation to return the extant property? I respond: This is possible, for one of the two rulings can be separated from the other. Do you not see that if a free man says: 'The garment in Zayd's hand, I stole it from Amr,' and Zayd says: 'It is my garment,' then the confessor's hand is amputated and the garment is not taken from Zayd? So amputation occurs without restitution. And according to Abu Hanifa: The confession to amputation is valid from him, so the property is also valid based upon it. And al-Hamdulillah: His confession to property is invalid; for this reason, his confession to usurpation is not valid, so the property remains for the master, and there is no amputation on the slave for stealing the master's property. And all this disagreement is when the master does not confirm him; if he confirms him, he is amputated in all the chapters. Thus in al-Hidayah and others. And in al-Nihayah: Mentioned in al-Fawa'id al-Zuhayriyyah: The essence of the disagreement reduces to one point: whether amputation is the root and property is subsidiary, or each is a root. Abu Hanifa said: Amputation is the root and property is subsidiary, evidenced by the fact that if he says: 'I desire the property and I do not desire amputation,' amputation is not dropped, and also because it is invalidated by prescription. Abu Yusuf said: Each of them is a root. As for property being a root, it is according to what Muhammad said; as for amputation being a root, it is according to what they said about the free man if he confesses to taking this property from Zayd while it is in the hand of Amr, and Amr denies him: his confession is valid regarding amputation but not property. And Muhammad said: Property is the root and amputation is subsidiary. Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the effect of a slave's confession to theft. The commentary opens by explaining that according to Abu Hanifa, a slave's confession to hudud (corporal punishments) and qisas (retaliation) is invalid even if he is authorized, because authorization (idhn) does not cover these matters. However, his confession regarding property is valid if he is authorized, so he must return the stolen property; if he is restricted, it is invalid. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), if the slave is authorized, he is amputated and returns the property. If he is restricted, the ruling depends on whether the stolen item is destroyed or extant. If destroyed, his confession is valid for amputation only, as amputation is the sole obligation. If extant, Abu Hanifa holds that he is both amputated and returns the item. The commentary then elaborates on the reasoning: an authorized slave has control over property from the master, so his confession regarding property is valid; a restricted slave's confession regarding property is invalid because his property belongs to the master. For a restricted slave who confesses to an extant stolen item while the master denies, neither amputation nor restitution occurs; but if the master confirms, the property is returned. The dispute among the imams is then detailed: Abu Yusuf holds that a restricted slave is amputated but does not return the property, because amputation is valid while property is against the master. Muhammad holds that neither amputation nor restitution occurs. The passage also addresses an objection by Zufar, arguing that a slave might confess to harm himself and his master, but this is rare and not basis for law. Finally, the root (asl) vs. subsidiary (taba') analysis is presented: Abu Hanifa sees amputation as root, property as subsidiary; Abu Yusuf sees each as independent; Muhammad sees property as root and amputation as subsidiary. The practical consequence is that when the master confirms the confession, all agree on amputation.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave authorized by his master to engage in trade; his confession regarding property is valid.
محجور (mahjur): A slave restricted from trade; his confession regarding property is invalid as his property belongs to the master.
إقرار (iqrar): Confession or admission, in this context a slave's statement acknowledging theft.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's internal dispute on a slave's confession to theft hinges on whether amputation and property restitution are considered independent or one subsidiary to the other, with practical consequences for the slave and master.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Is Cutting Subsidiary to Returning Stolen Goods?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on subsidiary relationship between cutting and returning stolen goods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on the relationship between cutting and returning stolen goods, and evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf in the slave's confession case.

Core Idea
Cutting is an independent hadd punishment, not a mere consequence of returning goods.

This passage examines a subtle intra-Hanafi disagreement: does amputation (cutting) depend on the return of stolen property? Imam Muhammad argues it is subsidiary; Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue it is independent, though they differ on the precise relationship. The dispute has practical consequences, especially in the validity of a restricted slave's confession leading to amputation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
State the views of Muhammad (cutting subsidiary to return), Abu Yusuf (both independent), and Abu Hanifa (cutting original, return subsidiary).
All three agree cutting is not subsidiary; only Muhammad held it is.
2
Locate key evidence for each
Examine the arguments: property can be established without cutting; compensation vs punishment; slave confession case.
Pay attention to the nature of cutting as 'jaza' (punishment) not 'daman' (compensation).
3
Analyze the slave confession subsidiary issue
Understand why Muhammad's view leads to invalidating the confession for cutting, while the majority view validates it because cutting harms the slave more than the master.
The slave is not suspected of harming the master by confessing to a hadd crime.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What practical difference does this dispute make? The validity of a slave's confession for cutting and whether cutting requires return of goods.
The mufta bihi position allows cutting based on slave confession even if goods are not returned.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Why is the relied-upon position that cutting and return are separate? Evidence from the non-combination of guarantee and cutting in Hanafi law and the fall of property inviolability by cutting.
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah prefers Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's view.

[٢]قوله : الشرطية الدعوى ; فإنّه قد مرَّ أنّ خصومة المسروق منه شرط لظهور السرقة والقطع , وهذا آية كون المال أصلاً والقطع تبعاً. [0]قوله : لأن رد...الخ ; حاصله : إنّ ردّ المالِ ضمان محل السرقة, أي الذي تقعُ (۱) عطف على شرطية يعني قد يثبت المال بدون القطع كما اذا شهد رجل وامرأتان بالسرقة أو أقر بها, ثم رجع فإنه يضمن المال ولا يقطع كذا في «العناية»(٥ : ٤١١). (۲) (العناية ( ٥ : ٤١١).  كتاب السرقة / فصل : كيفية القطع , وأثباته وما قطع به إن بقى رد ۳۲۷ فأبو يوسف له لم يجعل أحدهما تبعاً للآخر، فيعتبر إقراره في حق نفسه، وهو القطع لا في حق المولى، وهو رد المال. وأبو حنيفة الله جعل الفعل أصلاً ; لأن المحال كالشروط. (وما قطع به " إن بقى ردّ فيه، والقطع جزاء لنفس الفعل، فكل منهما حكم على حدة، فلا يكون القطع تبعاً لرد العين. [ ۱ ] قوله : فأبو يوسف له ; أي بعد ما اتفق أبو حنيفة وأبو يوسف له على أنّ القطع ليس تبعاً لرد العين، كما قال به محمد , اختلفا فذهب أبو يوسف إلى أنّ كلا منهما مستقل بنفسه، ليس أحدهما تبعاً للآخر , وذهب أبو حنيفة له إلى أن الفعل أصل والرد تبع ; لأنّ محلّ الفعل كالشرط , والشرط يكون تابعاً للمشروط. [٢) قوله : أصلاً ; بدليل أنه تسقط عصمة المال باعتبار القطع لما مر، ويأتي من أنّ الضمان والقطع لا يجتمعان عندنا فسقوط العصمة والتقومُ في حق السارق يدلّ على أن المال تبع ; فإنه لو كان أصلاً لما تغير حاله من حال الابتداء الذي هو التقوم إلى غير التقوم، وبدليل أنه يستوفى بالقطع بعد استهلاك المال. كذا في «النهاية».
After that, the principle according to Muhammad is that the return of the stolen goods is the original, and cutting is subsidiary to it, as indicated by the condition that the claim precedes the proof of property without cutting, without the reverse. And the confession of a legally restricted slave regarding property is not valid. So its subsidiary, which is cutting, is not established. We said: Cutting is not subsidiary to returning the goods because returning the goods is compensation for the place (of theft), while cutting is a punishment that necessitates a hadd penalty and retaliation. The slave is not suspected in this confession of intending to harm the master, as he is suspected in confession regarding property, because the slave's harm in this confession is greater than the master's harm. It is far from a rational person to seek harm to others by bringing stronger harm upon himself. No heed is paid to what some foolish people do, overpowered by wickedness, because legal rulings are not attached to rare matters. So he is not suspected here, and his confession is considered. If you say: Even if he is not suspected therein, it is a confession against another, since the slave is entirely the master's property, so it should not be considered. He said: It is valid from the aspect that he is a human being, then it extends to financial matters, so it is valid from where [...] [Footnote 1]: His statement: 'then...' etc.: beginning of directing the disagreement among our three imams after finishing the answer to Zufr's statement. [Footnote 2]: His statement: 'the condition of the claim' because it has passed that the adversary of the victim is a condition for the manifestation of theft and cutting, and this indicates that the property is the original and cutting is subsidiary. [Footnote 3]: His statement: 'and the establishment' is a conjunction to 'conditionality', meaning that property may be established without cutting, as when a man and two women testify to theft or he confesses and then retracts, he guarantees the property and is not cut. Likewise in al-'Inayah. [Footnote 4]: His statement: 'is not valid' is different from the authorized slave, because his confession regarding property is valid, so it is valid regarding cutting as a subsidiary. Then Abu Yusuf did not make one subsidiary to the other, so his confession is considered regarding himself, which is cutting, not regarding the master, which is returning the property. And Abu Hanifa made the act the original because the place is like conditions. 'And what is cut with, if it remains, is returned.' Cutting is a recompense for the act itself, so each is a separate ruling; thus cutting is not subsidiary to returning the property. [Commentary 1]: His statement: 'So Abu Yusuf...' i.e., after Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf agreed that cutting is not subsidiary to returning the property, as Muhammad said, they differed: Abu Yusuf held that each is independent, not subsidiary to the other, and Abu Hanifa held that the act is the original and return is subsidiary, because the place of the act is like a condition, and the condition is subsidiary to the conditioned. [Commentary 2]: His statement: 'original' is proven by the fact that the inviolability of property falls by virtue of cutting, as has passed, and it will come that guarantee and cutting do not combine in our school. So the fall of inviolability and valuation regarding the thief indicates that property is subsidiary; for if it were original, its state would not change from the initial state of valuation to non-valuation, and by the evidence that it is fulfilled by cutting after consumption of the property. Likewise in al-Nihayah.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute analysis. Imam Muhammad considered returning the stolen goods as the original legal consequence of theft, with cutting (amputation) as merely subsidiary. He argued that property can be established without cutting in certain cases (e.g., when witnesses retract), proving the independence of the financial liability. However, the commentator (the sharh) rejects this, asserting that cutting is not subsidiary to returning the goods. The reasoning is that returning goods is compensatory (damān al-mahal), while cutting is a penal sanction (jaza') belonging to the category of hadd and qisas. The text then discusses a subsidiary issue: a legally restricted slave's confession regarding property is invalid, but his confession regarding cutting is valid, because he is not suspected of harming his master by seeking a hadd penalty that would bring greater harm to himself. The slave's human dignity allows his confession to be considered for acts of worship/punishments, and it then extends to financial matters. The dispute among the three imams is then detailed: after agreeing with Muhammad that cutting is not subsidiary, Abu Yusuf held each consequence as independent, while Abu Hanifa made the act of cutting the original and the return subsidiary, analogizing the place of the act to a condition for the ruling. The commentator provides proofs: the fall of property inviolability by cutting and the non-combination of guarantee and cutting in the Hanafi school demonstrate that property is subsidiary.

Key Terms
رد العين (radd al-'ayn): The return of the stolen goods to the owner; the obligation to physically return the stolen property if it still exists.
قطع (qat'): Amputation of the hand as the hadd penalty for theft.
تبعة (tiba'ah): Subsidiary relationship; one ruling following another as a consequence.
ضمان المحل (damān al-mahal): Guarantee/compensation for the place (the stolen goods); financial liability for the value.
إقرار العبد المحجور (iqrār al-'abd al-mahjūr): Confession by a legally restricted slave (who is not permitted to trade).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, is the cutting of the hand subsidiary to the return of the stolen goods?
A) Yes, cutting is always subsidiary to returning the goods.
B) No, cutting is an independent punishment not contingent on return.
C) It depends on whether the thief is a slave.
D) Cutting is subsidiary only if the stolen goods are consumed.

Key Takeaway
Cutting for theft is an independent hadd punishment; its validity does not depend on the return of stolen goods according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Return of the Cut Hand or Instrument After Hadd
Ruling on returning the cut instrument/hand after hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the return of the cut hand or instrument after hadd, explain the condition for return, and distinguish between judicial and religious liability.

Core Idea
After cutting, the instrument or hand is returned if the stolen goods remain; cutting is not subsidiary to return.

This lesson unpacks a specific ruling from Hanafi hudud law: what happens to the instrument used for amputation (or the cut hand itself) after the hadd penalty is carried out? The base text says: 'And what is cut with, if it remains, it is returned.' We will explore the condition, the underlying reasoning about the relationship between cutting and property liability, and the important distinction between judicial and religious accountability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Subject of the Ruling
Determine what 'what is cut with' refers to: the instrument (e.g., sword) or the cut hand. The commentary indicates both possibilities, but the core ruling is that if it remains after cutting, it is returned to its rightful owner (the state for the instrument, the thief's family for the hand).
The text uses 'wa mā quti'a bihī' which can mean the tool or the limb.
2
Explain the Condition for Return
The phrase 'in baqiya' (if it remains) conditions the return on the stolen goods being intact after the cutting. If the stolen property is consumed or lost, the cut hand or instrument is not returned.
This condition links the return of the physical object to the existence of the stolen property.
3
Distinguish Judicial vs. Religious Liability
The commentary clarifies that judicially (qaḍā'an), the thief is not liable for the stolen goods after cutting because the hadd penalty is complete. However, religiously (diyānatan), he may still be accountable before Allah for the loss or damage inflicted on the owner.
This distinction is common in Hanafi fiqh: worldly penalties do not necessarily absolve religious responsibility.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Difference on Subordination
Within the Hanafi school, Abu Hanifa held that the cutting is the original act and the return is subsidiary, while Abu Yusuf held that both are independent. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) aligns with Abu Yusuf's independence view, as the base ruling conditions return on the survival of the stolen goods, showing that return is not automatically tied to cutting.
This ikhtilaf does not change the practical ruling but reflects different legal reasoning.

And what is cut with, if it remains, is returned. Cutting is a recompense for the act itself, so each is a separate ruling; thus cutting is not subsidiary to returning the property. [Commentary 1]: His statement: 'So Abu Yusuf...' i.e., after Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf agreed that cutting is not subsidiary to returning the property, as Muhammad said, they differed: Abu Yusuf held that each is independent, not subsidiary to the other, and Abu Hanifa held that the act is the original and return is subsidiary, because the place of the act is like a condition, and the condition is subsidiary to the conditioned.
