So the fall of inviolability and valuation regarding the thief indicates that property is subsidiary; for if it were original, its state would not change from the initial state of valuation to non-valuation, and by the evidence that it is fulfilled by cutting after consumption of the property. Likewise in al-Nihayah. [Commentary 3]: His statement: 'and what is cut with' it appears that it means what cutting is obligatory with. Indeed, the non-liability is not restricted to being after the occurrence of cutting; rather, there is no liability upon him, neither before cutting nor after it. Yes, if before cutting the owner says: 'I guarantee it,' he is not cut according to us. And if he says: 'I choose cutting,' he is cut and not liable. Because in the first case, it implies his return from a claim of theft to a claim of property. Likewise in al-Bahr. And in al-Fath, from al-Mabsut: Hisham narrated from Muhammad that the liability of the thief is only dropped judicially due to difficulty of ruling on commutative justice; but religiously, one gives a fatwa of liability due to loss and damage to the owner from the thief. From [Commentary 4]: His statement: 'if it remains' i.e., if the thief is cut and the stolen goods still exist in their original state, even if not...
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on what to cut with (the manner of amputation) and the disposition of the cut hand. The base ruling states: 'And what is cut with, if it remains, is returned.' The commentator explains that this refers to the instrument used for cutting (e.g., a sword) — if it remains after the cutting, it is returned to its owner (the state presumably). Alternatively, it may refer to the cut hand itself — if the stolen goods remain intact, the cut hand is given back to the thief? The commentary clarifies that the ruling is about the absence of financial liability: once cutting is established, there is no guarantee (damān) on the thief for the goods, whether before or after cutting. However, if the owner chooses to guarantee the goods rather than demand cutting, then the thief is not cut. The distinction is between judicial and religious liability: judicially, the thief's liability is dropped because the hadd penalty is complete, but religiously, he may still be liable in the Hereafter. The text then begins to explain the condition 'if it remains' (in baqiya) but is cut off.

Key Terms
وما قطع به (wa mā quti'a bihī): The instrument used for the amputation (e.g., a sword); also possibly the cut hand itself.
إن بقى رد (in baqiya rudda): Conditional phrase: 'if it remains, it is returned' — referring to the instrument or hand.
قضاءً وديانةً (qaḍā'an wa diyānatan): Judicially vs. religiously — a distinction in liability. Judicially, the thief is not liable after cutting; religiously, he may still be accountable before Allah.

Checkpoint

A) The cut hand is returned to the thief's family.

C) The cut hand is given to the victim as compensation.
D) The cut hand is kept by the authorities until the thief pays a fine.

Key Takeaway
The cut hand or instrument is returned to its rightful owner only if the stolen goods remain intact; cutting does not automatically extinguish the obligation to return property, but it does remove judicial liability for the thief.

The Hanafi Ruling on Compensation After Amputation for Theft
Explaining Hanafi ruling on property return after amputation and compensation issue

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on returning stolen property after amputation and explain why compensation is generally not required, distinguishing between the relied-upon view and rare narrations.

Core Idea
Amputation and compensation do not combine: no liability for destroyed stolen property.

Theft in Hanafi law triggers two separate consequences: the fixed punishment of amputation (hadd) and the civil obligation to return the stolen item. But what if the item is destroyed or consumed after the amputation? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position that no financial compensation is owed to the owner in the well-known view, and explores the internal and external debates on this issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the source passage to find the central Hanafi ruling on returning the stolen item after amputation and the absence of compensation for destruction. Note the distinction between the item remaining (return wajib) and being destroyed (no guarantee).
2
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Locate the differences between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding whether amputation is dependent on return, and note the rare narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad on compensation for consumption.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i counter-argument
Identify Imam al-Shafi'i's position that amputation and guarantee combine, and understand his dalil based on inviolability of property.
4
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal
Trace the Hanafi reasoning: amputation and guarantee differ in subject, entitled party, and cause, so one does not nullify the other. The inviolability of the property is lost upon amputation, justifying no compensation.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
Conclude that the well-known (mashhur) view is no compensation, based on Abu Yusuf's narration, and contrast it with the rare narration requiring compensation for consumption.

Abu Hanifa held that the act is primary and the return is dependent because the place of the act is like a condition, and the condition is dependent on the conditioned. Yes, if it was before amputation and the owner said: I guarantee him, he is not amputated according to us. And if he said: I choose amputation, he is amputated and is not liable; because in the first case it implies his retraction from the claim of theft to the claim of property. So in 'al-Bahr'. And in 'al-Fath' from 'al-Mabsut': Hisham narrated from Muhammad that the guarantee is only dropped from the thief in judicial rulings because it is impossible to judge for exact equivalence; but in religious terms (dinan) a fatwa is given for guarantee due to the loss and deficiency occurring to the owner from the thief's side. [2] His statement: That it is obligatory; according to this narration, he distinguished between destruction (halak) and consumption (istihlak); in halak there is no guarantee, in istihlak there is guarantee. [3] His statement: They combine; because amputation and guarantee are two different rights in terms of subject, entitled party, and cause. The subject of amputation is the hand, the subject of guarantee is the liability (dhimma); the entitled party for amputation is Allah and His deputy, the entitled party for guarantee is the owner of the stolen item; the cause of amputation is the transgression against Allah, and the cause of guarantee is the infliction of loss on the owner. When they differ, the obligation of one does not nullify the other. An analogy is the case of someone who consumes owned game in the sanctuary, where two obligations are due: its value to the owner and another value as penalty. [4] His statement: Based on the inviolability of property; for the property that was stolen is owned and inviolable; without inviolability amputation would not be obligatory, for it is not obligatory due to theft of permissible property not owned by anyone, nor due to theft of non-inviolable property. And when the property is inviolable, return and guarantee are necessarily obligatory. [0] His statement: And we say...etc.; our companions argued for the non-obligation of guarantee in several ways:
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This commentary passage from a work on Hanafi fiqh addresses the legal consequences after a thief's hand is amputated for theft. The core ruling is that if the stolen item is still in existence, it must be returned to the owner, but if it is destroyed or consumed, the thief is generally not liable for compensation. The text begins by noting a point of agreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf (both differing from Imam Muhammad): amputation is not a substitute for returning the item; rather, they are separate. However, they disagree on whether one is dependent on the other. Abu Yusuf sees them as entirely independent, while Abu Hanifa views the act of theft as primary and the return of the item as secondary, likening the place of the act (the hand) to a condition that is subsidiary to the conditioned. Evidence for this priority is that the inviolability of the stolen property is lost due to amputation, and guarantee (daman) and amputation do not combine in Hanafi law. The authority_weight of this principle is from Zahir al-Riwayah. The text then clarifies that the absence of guarantee applies both before and after amputation; if the owner chooses to guarantee before amputation, the thief is not cut. The well-known position (mashhur) is that no guarantee is due upon destruction, following the narration from Abu Yusuf. However, a rare narration (Nawadir) from Hasan ibn Ziyad reports that Imam Abu Hanifa held that if the thief consumes the item (istihlak), he must compensate, although this is not the relied-upon view. The passage then contrasts the Shafi'i position: Imam al-Shafi'i holds that amputation and guarantee combine because guarantee is based on the inviolability of property. The commentary refutes this by explaining that amputation and guarantee are rights with different subjects (hand vs. liability), different entitled parties (Allah vs. the owner), and different causes (transgression against Allah vs. harm to the owner), so they do not conflict. The Hanafi school's arguments for non-guarantee are introduced but not fully elaborated in this excerpt.

Key Terms
الضمان (al-daman): Financial liability or guarantee for loss or destruction of property; the obligation to compensate the owner.
القطع (al-qat'): Amputation of the thief's hand as a fixed punishment (hadd) for theft.
الرد (al-radd): Return of the stolen item to its owner if it is still extant.
الاستهلاك (al-istihlak): Consumption or destruction of the stolen property by the thief, such as eating it or using it up.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position, if a thief's hand is amputated and the stolen item has been destroyed (istihlak) by him, is he liable for compensation?
A) Yes, he must compensate the owner.
B) No, he is not liable for compensation.
C) Yes, but only if the destruction occurred before the amputation.
D) No, except in a rare narration (Nawadir) from Hasan ibn Ziyad.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, after amputation for theft, the stolen item must be returned if it exists, but no compensation is due for its destruction or consumption based on the principle that amputation and guarantee do not combine.

Thief's Liability After Hadd: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
States Hanafi ruling and proofs for no guarantee after hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the thief's liability after hadd and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
After the hadd, the thief owes nothing—the cut hand cancels the debt.

A thief's hand is amputated for theft. But must he also pay back the stolen property? The Hanafi school says no, while the Shafi'i school says yes. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling, its proofs, and the reasoning behind the difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: after the hadd of amputation, the thief is not liable for compensation, whether the property perished (halak) or was consumed (istihlak). Note the intra-Hanafi weak riwaya that only applies to consumption.
The mashhur (well-known) view is from Abu Yusuf.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Name the opposing school (Shafi'i) and their position: the thief must guarantee in both destruction and consumption.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What is the Shafi'i evidence? The property is inviolable (ma'sum), and hadd does not remove that inviolability. Compensation is built on ismat al-mal.
They also argue that hadd and guarantee are rights of different parties and can coexist.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafis rebut with: (1) a hadith (though weak) saying no compensation after hadd; (2) rational proof: guarantee would retroactively make the thief owner, creating doubt (shubha) that invalidates the hadd; (3) the inviolability of the property transfers to Allah at theft, reducing the owner's claim.
The qiyas on ghasb (usurper) is used: paying compensation gives ownership retroactively.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi, after amputation the thief walks away with no financial liability; under Shafi'i, he must pay the value in addition to the hadd.
This difference affects the victim's right to recover losses.

And in a narration of al-Daraqutni: 'No compensation on the thief after cutting his right hand.' And in a narration of al-Bazzar and al-Tabarani: 'The thief does not guarantee his theft after the establishment of the hadd.' This hadith would put an end to contention and settle the dispute if it were proven by an acceptable chain, but it is not proven, for all its chains contain weakness due to disconnection and ignorance, as elaborated by al-Zayla'i in Takhrij Ahadith al-Hidaya. And al-'Ayni mentioned in al-Binaya that Ibn Jarir al-Tabari narrated it in Tahdhib al-Athar with a connected acceptable chain. Then non-guarantee was narrated from Ibn Sirin, al-Nakha'i, al-Sha'bi, al-'Ata', al-Hasan, and Qatada. He said: This is correct because of His statement: 'As for the thief, male and female, cut off their hands in recompense for what they have earned'—He did not command fining; had it been obligatory upon them, He would have mentioned it. 2. Among them: The obligation of guarantee contradicts the obligation of cutting because the thief becomes owner of it, retroactively from the time of taking, after paying the guarantee as established in the chapter on guaranteeing the usurper and others. Thus the theft would have occurred on his own property, and if ownership is retroactive, cutting is negated due to doubt. It is known that cutting is definitively established, so guarantee is negated. And also: The inviolability (isma) falls as a right of the servant at the time of theft, and its explanation has passed. [1] His statement: 'by transfer of inviolability' because if it remained, it would be permissible in itself; for it is known by established principle that whatever is forbidden as a right of the servant is permissible in itself, so the property is forbidden to the thief from one aspect but not another.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Sariqa (The Book of Theft) in the commentary 'Umda al-Ri'ayah expands on the ruling from the matn of al-Wiqaya. The matn states: 'Otherwise he does not guarantee, even if he destroys it.' The commentator explains that the phrase 'even if he destroys it' is a qualification to exclude a rare transmission (riwaya) from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa, which held that the thief must guarantee (pay compensation) in case of consumption (istihlak), i.e., using or destroying the property in a way that prevents return. The Hanafi position, however, is the well-known (mashhur) one: after the hadd penalty (amputation of the hand) is carried out, the thief is not liable for compensation, whether the property perished (halak) or was consumed (istihlak). This is based on the narration of Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa. The Shafi'i school opposes this, holding that the thief must guarantee in both cases because compensation is based on the inviolability of property (ismat al-mal). The Hanafi response is that cutting (hadd) and guarantee are two different rights with different loci (hand vs. liability), different entitled parties (Allah vs. the victim), and different causes (transgression against Allah vs. inflicting loss on the owner). Therefore, the obligation of one does not nullify the other. To support the non-obligation of guarantee, Hanafi scholars cite several proofs: (1) a hadith narrated by al-Nasa'i, al-Daraqutni, al-Bazzar, and al-Tabarani that explicitly states no guarantee after hadd, though its chain is weak; (2) a rational proof: if guarantee were obligatory, the thief would become owner of the property retroactively after paying, creating a doubt (shubha) that would prevent the hadd—but since hadd is definitively established (qat'i al-thubut), guarantee must be negated; (3) the inviolability of the stolen property transfers to Allah at the time of theft, so the property's protection for the owner drops. The passage also explains that if a third party (buyer or donee) consumes the property, the victim can hold them liable, and the buyer can recover the price from the thief.

Key Terms
الضمان (aḍ-Ḍamān): Financial compensation or guarantee paid for loss or destruction of property.
الهلاك (al-Halāk): Destruction of the stolen property in the thief's hand without any act of consumption (e.g., it gets lost or damaged naturally).
الاستهلاك (al-Istihlāk): Consumption or use of the stolen property by the thief, such that the property cannot be returned in kind.
العصمة (al-ʿIṣma): Inviolability or protected status of property that demands compensation if violated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mashhur) Hanafi position, after the hadd of amputation is carried out, what is the thief's liability for the stolen property if it was consumed (istihlāk)?
A) He must pay the value to the owner.
B) He does not pay any compensation.
C) He pays only if the property was also destroyed.
D) He pays half the value.

Key Takeaway
After the hadd of amputation, the Hanafi school holds that the thief is financially absolved, regardless of whether the stolen property perished or was consumed.

Liability for Cutting the Wrong Hand in Theft Punishment
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for cutting wrong hand

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on liability for cutting the wrong hand in hudud and evaluate the reasoning of Abu Hanifa, the Two Companions, and Zufar.

Core Idea
Cutting the left hand saves the right; Abu Hanifa exempts liability, but Sahibayn and Zufar differ.

When a judge orders cutting a thief's right hand, but the executor cuts the left instead, should he be liable? The Hanafi school has three positions. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each view and identifies the operative ruling for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Identify the exact situation: judge orders cut of right hand for theft, executor cuts left hand. Note that the executor may act intentionally or by mistake.
The text distinguishes between error in ijtihad and error in distinguishing right from left.
2
Identify the three intra-Hanafi positions
List the rulings of Abu Hanifa, Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and Zufar. Focus on the conditions (intentional vs. mistaken).
Abu Hanifa: no liability at all. Sahibayn: no liability for mistake, arsh for intentional. Zufar: liability always.
3
Analyze the proofs for each position
Examine the dalil: Abu Hanifa says the right hand was already lawful to destroy, so cutting left is a replacement and not damage. Sahibayn view: intentional cutting of an inviolable limb without right incurs arsh. Zufar: any error in executing a hadd that harms a third party creates liability.
The key issue is whether the text (Quran 5:38) specifies the right hand or is general.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, executor pays nothing even if intentional; under Sahibayn, pays arsh if intentional; under Zufar, pays always.
This affects how courts handle erroneous executions and compensation.
5
Identify the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The matn explicitly states 'no liability' and the commentary defends Abu Hanifa's view as the correct interpretation. Thus, the relied-upon position for fatwa is that of Abu Hanifa.
Other views are preserved as valid ikhtilaf but are not acted upon.

Source Text

كتاب السرقة / فصل : كيفية القطع ، وإثباته ولا قاطع يسار من أمر بقطع يمينه بسرقة ولو عمداً والخطأ. [١] قوله : ولا قاطع ... الخ ؛ يعني إذا أمر الحاكم بقطع يمين السارق بعد ثبوتِ السرقة، فقطع المأمور يساره لا يضمن القاطع، ولو كان عمداً، وقالا : لا ضمان في الخطأ، ويضمن أرش اليسار في العمد، وقال زفره : يضمن مطلقاً : أي في العمد والمراد بالخطأ الخطأ في الاجتهاد من القاطع ؛ لأن قطع اليسار يجزى نظراً إلى إطلاق النص، أما الخطأ في معرفة اليمين من اليسار، فلا يجعل عفواً ؛ لأنه بعيد يتهم به مدعيه ، وقيل : يجعل عفوا. لزفر ه إنه قطع يداً معصومة، فإن الواجب كان قطع اليمين، والخطأ في حق العباد غير موضوع. وجوابه : إنه ليس في النصّ تعيين اليمين، فيكون الخطأ من القاطع موضوعاً. ولهما : إنه قطع طرفا معصوماً بغير حق ولا تأويل، بل تعتمد الظلم، فلا يعفى، وكان ينبغي أن يجب القصاص إلا أنه ارتفع للشبهة ، فوجب الأرش في العمد. وله : أنه أتلف وأخلف من جنسه ما هو خير منه، وهو اليد اليمنى، فلا يعد اتلافاً ، كذا في «النهر الفائق» و«الهداية». [٢] قوله : من أمر بقطع يمينه ؛ فلو لم يصرّح الحاكم باليمين ، بل قال : اقطع يده ، فقطع الجلاد يسراه عمداً او خطأ فلا ضمان عليه بالطريق الأولى، وكذا لو أخرج السارق يساره ، فقال : هذه يميني فقطعها لا ضمان عليه اتفاقاً. كذا في «البحر الرائق» وقال الزيلعي : نقلاً عن «شرح الطحاوي»: «ومن وجب عليه القطع في يمينه فقطع رجل يده اليسرى عمداً أو خطأ فلا ضمان عليه؛ لأن اليمين كان مستحقاً للإتلاف فقطع اليسرى فسلمت به اليمين».
Book of Theft / Section: Manner of Cutting and Its Establishment. And there is no liability for the cutter of the left hand of the one ordered to cut his right hand for theft, even if intentional or in error. [1] His statement: 'And there is no liability for the cutter...' means: when the judge orders cutting the right hand of the thief after the theft is established, and the executor cuts his left hand, the cutter is not liable, even if intentional. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: no liability in error, but he guarantees the compensation (arsh) of the left hand in intentional. Zufar said: he is liable absolutely: i.e., in intentional. And what is meant by error is error in ijtihad of the cutter, because cutting the left hand is permissible considering the absolute text (of the Qur'an). As for error in distinguishing right from left, it is not excused, because it is far-fetched and the claimant is suspected (of lying). And it is said: it is excused. For Zufar: he cut an inviolable hand, while the obligatory was cutting the right hand, and error in the rights of people is not excusable. Its response: there is no specification of the right hand in the text, so the error of the cutter is excused. For them (the two companions): he cut an inviolable limb without right or interpretation, rather based on injustice, so it is not excused, and retaliation (qisas) would be obligatory except that it is waived due to doubt, so compensation is due in intentional. For him (Abu Hanifa): he damaged and replaced from the same kind what is better than it, which is the right hand, so it is not considered damage. Thus in al-Nahr al-Fa'iq and al-Hidayah. [2] His statement: 'the one ordered to cut his right hand' — if the judge did not specify the right hand, but said 'cut his hand' and the executor cut his left hand intentionally or by mistake, there is no liability by primary way. Similarly, if the thief extended his left hand and said 'this is my right hand' and he cut it, there is no liability by agreement. Thus in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. And al-Zaylai said, quoting from Sharh al-Tahawi: 'One upon whom cutting of his right hand is obligatory, and a man cuts his left hand intentionally or by mistake, there is no liability; because the right hand was lawful to destroy, so by cutting the left hand, the right hand is saved.'
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Theft details a scenario where a judge orders the cutting of a thief's right hand, but the executor mistakenly or intentionally cuts the left hand instead. The matn states emphatically that the executor is not liable (la qati') even if the act was deliberate. The commentary expands on the internal Hanafi disagreement. Abu Hanifa (indicated by 'la hu' or 'lahu' in the text) holds that there is no liability at all, whether the executor acted intentionally or mistakenly, because the right hand was already lawfully subject to destruction (istihlāk). By cutting the left, he actually saved the right hand, which is better; thus it is not considered an unlawful destruction. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) distinguish between error (khatā') and intention: if the execution was a genuine mistake (e.g., due to ijtihad in interpreting the text), there is no liability, but if the executor knowingly cut the left hand, he must pay the full arsh (compensation) for the left hand, because it was an inviolable limb. Zufar goes further, holding that the executor is liable in both cases, since any cutting of a non-mandated limb is an aggression. The preferred position appears to be Abu Hanifa's, as it is given as the main ruling and is supported by the reasoning that the text (Quran 5:38) does not specify 'right hand,' and the left hand cutting is a valid fulfillment by ijtihad. The commentary also notes that if the judge merely said 'cut his hand' without specifying right or left, there is definitely no liability, as the executor had discretion. The passage guards against oversimplifying the rules of judicial execution: even a clear mistake does not automatically entail financial liability if it falls within the scope of ijtihad.

Key Terms
قاطع (qāti‘): The person who executes the cutting (the official cutter). Here, it refers to the one who physically severs the thief's hand.
أرش (arsh): Compensation paid for injury to a body part that is not a specified diyah amount. Here, it is the full value of the left hand.
يسار (yasār): Left hand. The opposite of the right hand (yamīn) which is the prescribed organ for cutting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (operative ruling) in the Hanafi school, what is the liability of an executor who, upon being ordered to cut the thief's right hand, intentionally cuts the left hand?
A) He must pay the full compensation (arsh) for the left hand.
B) He is not liable because the right hand was already lawful to destroy.
C) He is liable only if the thief demands compensation.
D) He must pay half the arsh because the mistake was unintentional.

Key Takeaway
An executor who cuts the left hand instead of the ordered right hand is not liable even if intentional, because the right hand was already subject to lawful destruction.

Theft of Gold/Silver and Transformation into Coins: Cut and Return
Intra-Hanafi ruling on theft of gold/silver turned into coins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the application of hadd and return of stolen goods when the thief transforms the stolen substance, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The thief is cut and the coins are returned: Abu Hanifa preserves the owner's right through the permanence of the stolen substance, while the Sahibayn see manufacture as creating new ownership.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute. The key question: when a thief steals gold or silver and then makes them into coins, does the hadd (amputation) still apply, and must the coins be returned to the owner? Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ, and their reasoning reveals deep principles about ownership, transformation, and compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
State exactly what the thief did: stole gold/silver, then manufactured them into dirhams or dinars. The legal issue is whether the transformation affects the original theft ruling.
The theft is assumed to have met all conditions for hadd before manufacture.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa
According to Abu Hanifa, the thief is subject to hadd (amputation) and the manufactured coins must be returned to the original owner.
The cut is for the original theft; the return is because the substance (ʿayn) of the gold/silver remains the owner's property.
3
State the position of the Sahibayn
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the thief is not cut (since he now owns the transformed item) and the coins are not returned; rather, the thief must compensate the original value.
They consider the manufacture valuable (mutqawwam), creating a new entity that extinguishes the owner's right.
4
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa: the substance is unchanged, the name and ruling of gold/silver persist, and return to original state is possible. Sahibayn: the change in name, ruling (e.g., riba rules), and purpose severs the owner's right, analogous to misappropriated brass made into a pot.
The dispute centers on whether manufacturing is a transformative act (istihlak) or a mere change of form.
5
Determine the tarjih and mufta bihi
The text (and later Hanafi authorities) adopt Abu Hanifa's view as the relied-upon position. The thief is cut and coins are returned.
The Sahibayn's view is acknowledged but not operative for fatwa in this case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن جعل ما سرق دراهم، أو دنانير قطع وردت) : هذا عند ابي حنيفة إخراجه بعد ملكه ولا قطع بسرقة ماله نفسه بل بسرقة مال غيره بخلاف ما إذا أخرج ثم شق، فإنه وجد هناك التصرف بعد تمام السرقة. والجواب عنه : إن الأخذَ وُضِعَ سبباً للضمان لا للملك، وإنما الملك يثبتُ للسارق لأداء الضمان، فإذا اختار المالك تضمين نقصان الثوب وأخذه لم يلزم اجتماعُ البدلين في ملك واحد. 11 ] قوله : لئلا يجتمع البدلان؛ هذا على سبيل التغليب، وإلا فالشيء مبدل منه، والضمان بدل، وحاصله : إنه لو لم يثبت الملك للسارق بعد أداء الضمان، يلزمُ اجتماع المبدل منه : أي الثوب والبدل : أي ضمانه في ملك شخص واحد، وهو المالك القديم، وهو غير معقول عقلاً ونقلاً. قوله : ومن جعل ...الخ؛ يعني إن سرق ذهباً أو فضة، ثم صنعه دراهم أو دنانير قطع فيه، وردّت تلك الدراهم والدنانير إلى المالك عنده، وأما نحو النحاس لو جعله أواني بعد السرقة، فإن كان ذلك المصنوع يباع وزناً فكذلك عنده خلافاً لهما. وإن كان يباع عدداً فهي للسارق اتَّفاقاً؛ لأنّ هذه الصنعة بدلت العين والاسم بدليل أنه تغيّر بها حكم الربا، حيث خرجت عن كونها موزونة، بخلاف الذهب والفضة لبقاء الاسم مع بقاء العين حكماً، كذا في «الفتح»، وغيره. وأما عندهما لا يجب ردُّهما، لأنَّ الصَّنعةَ متقومة عندهما فصارت شيئاً آخر. قوله : لا يجب ردّهما؛ وهل يجب القطع عندهما؟ فقيل: لا؛ لأنه ملكها قبله، وقيل: يجب؛ لأنه صار بالصنعة شيئاً آخر، فلم يملك عينه، وأما على قوله: فوجوب الحدّ ظاهر؛ لأن السارق لا يملكه، كذا في «الهداية». قوله : متقومة عندهما؛ لأنّ بهذه الصنعة يتبدل العين اسماً وحكماً ومقصوداً، وكلّ ما كان كذلك ينقطعُ به حق المالك، كما إذا كان المغصوب صفراً فضربه قمقمة. وله: إن عين المسروق باق والصنعة الحادثة والاسم الحادث ليسا ملازمين، فإنّ إعادتها إلى الحالة الأولى ممكنة، وأصل هذا الخلاف الخلاف في الغصب.
And he who makes what he stole into dirhams or dinars, he is cut and returned: This according to Abu Hanifa, after his ownership, and there is no cut for stealing his own property, but for stealing the property of others, contrary to if he brought it out and then cut, because there the disposal occurred after completion of the theft. The answer to it: Because taking was made a cause for guarantee, not for ownership, and ownership is established for the thief only to fulfill the guarantee. So if the owner chooses to hold him liable for the decrease of the garment and takes it, combining two compensations in one ownership does not occur. [11] His statement: 'so that two compensations are not combined'; this is by way of predominance, otherwise the thing is the replaced and the guarantee is the replacement. The summary is: if ownership were not established for the thief after fulfilling the guarantee, combining the replaced (i.e., the garment) and the replacement (i.e., its guarantee) in the ownership of one person would occur, namely the former owner, and this is inconceivable by reason and transmission. His statement: 'and he who makes...' meaning: if he steals gold or silver, then makes them into dirhams or dinars, he is cut for it, and those dirhams and dinars are returned to the owner according to him (Abu Hanifa). As for copper, if he makes it into vessels after theft, then if that manufactured item is sold by weight, similarly according to him, contrary to them (Sahibayn). And if it is sold by count, then it belongs to the thief by agreement, because this manufacture changes the substance and the name, as evidenced by the fact that the ruling of riba changes, as it exits from being weighed, unlike gold and silver because the name remains along with the substance in terms of ruling, as in al-Fath and others. And according to them (Sahibayn), it is not obligatory to return them, because the manufacture is valuable according to them, so it becomes something else. His statement: 'it is not obligatory to return them'; and is cutting obligatory according to them? It is said: no, because he owned it before, and it is said: yes, because it became something else by manufacture, so he did not own its substance. But according to his (Abu Hanifa's) statement, the obligation of the hadd is apparent, because the thief does not own it, as in al-Hidayah. His statement: 'valuable according to them'; because by this manufacture the substance changes in name, ruling, and purpose, and everything that is like that, the owner's right terminates, as when a brass item is misappropriated and he beats it into a pot. And for him (Abu Hanifa): the substance of the stolen item remains, and the new manufacture and the new name are not inseparable, because returning it to the original state is possible, and the basis of this disagreement is the disagreement in ghasb.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling when a thief steals gold or silver and then manufactures them into coins (dirhams or dīnārs). According to Abu Hanifa, the thief is still subject to amputation (cut) because the act of stealing is considered complete on the original gold/silver, and the subsequent manufacturing does not change the fact that he stole someone else's property. Moreover, the manufactured coins are returned to the original owner because the owner's right remains attached to the substance. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree: they hold that the manufacturing creates a valuable new item (mutqawwam), which becomes the property of the thief because the workmanship has transformed it into a different entity. Therefore, according to them, the thief is not cut (since he now owns it) and the coins are not returned; rather, the thief compensates the owner for the original value. The text explains the reasoning: for Abu Hanifa, the substance (substantial form) of the gold/silver remains unchanged in name and ruling, so the owner's right persists. For the Sahibayn, the change in name, ruling, and purpose (as evidenced by the change in riba rulings) severs the owner's right. The author also notes an analogy with misappropriation (ghaṣb). A subsidiary point is made about copper: if it is made into vessels sold by weight, the same dispute applies; if sold by count, it is agreed to be the thief's property.

Key Terms
صنعة متقومة (ṣanʿa mutaqawwima): Manufacturing that adds significant value, such that the item is considered a new entity in the eyes of the law.
عين (ʿayn): The physical substance of the stolen item, as distinct from its form or function.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what happens when a thief steals gold and makes it into dinar coins?
A) The thief is not cut and keeps the coins.
B) The thief is cut but the coins belong to the thief.
C) The thief is cut and the coins are returned to the owner.
D) The thief is not cut but must pay compensation.

Key Takeaway
When stolen gold or silver is transformed into coins, Abu Hanifa's position (cut plus return) is the operative ruling because the substance remains the owner's property.

Dyeing a Stolen Cloth: Red vs Black – Liability and Return
Ruling on dyeing a stolen cloth and its effect on return and liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a thief who dyes a stolen cloth red versus black, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
Red dye transforms the cloth so completely that the owner's right is lost; black dye does not.

A thief steals a cloth, dyes it red or black, and then cuts it. Does he have to return the cloth or compensate the owner? The Hanafi school distinguishes based on the degree of transformation. Red dye is seen as a major change that severs the owner's right, while black dye is minor and preserves it. However, Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees, arguing that the cloth remains the principal and the owner is entitled to its return regardless of the dye. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act (stealing plus dyeing and cutting)? What is the ruling for red dye? For black dye?
The matn states: no return/no guarantee for red; return for black.
2
Locate the dalil and the basis of distinction
Why does colour matter? The commentary explains that red dye changes the cloth in name, purpose, and value, terminating the owner's right. Black dye is less transformative.
The key is the concept of 'tabi' (subsidiary) vs 'asl' (principal).
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold no return for red; Muhammad holds that the owner takes the cloth and pays for the dye's increase.
Muhammad's view: the cloth is the principal, the dye is subsidiary, so the owner still owns the cloth.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response to Muhammad
Why do the Sahibayn reject Muhammad's view? Because the red dye alters the cloth so much that returning it is impractical; the value is lost by cutting.
The analogy from ghasb (misappropriation) is also relevant.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference? Under the Sahibayn, the thief keeps the red-dyed cloth and owes nothing. Under Muhammad, the owner gets the cloth back but pays for the increased value.
This affects real cases of stolen goods that are transformed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(فإن حمره فقطع فلا ردّ ولا ضمان، وإن سوده ردّ) : أي إن سرق ثوباً وصبغَهُ أحمر فقطعَ لا يجب ردُّ الثَّوب وإن هلك فلا ضمان. وعند محمد له يؤخذ الثوب، ويعطى ما زادَ الصّبغ. (1) قوله: لا يجب ردّهما؛ وهل يجب القطع عندهما؟ فقيل: لا؛ لأنه ملكها قبله، وقيل: يجب؛ لأنه صار بالصنعة شيئاً آخر، فلم يملك عينه، وأما على قوله: فوجوب الحدّ ظاهر؛ لأن السارق لا يملكه، كذا في «الهداية»(۲). قوله: متقومة عندهما؛ لأنّ بهذه الصنعة يتبدل العين اسماً وحكماً ومقصوداً، وكلّ ما كان كذلك ينقطعُ به حق المالك، كما إذا كان المغصوب صفراً فضربه قمقمة. وله: إن عين المسروق باق والصنعة الحادثة والاسم الحادث ليسا ملازمين، فإنّ إعادتها إلى الحالة الأولى ممكنة، وأصل هذا الخلاف الخلاف في الغصب. قوله: فإن حمره؛ ماض من التحمير، أي سرق ثوباً فصبغه أحمر فقطع، لا يجب عليه ردّ الثوب ولا الضمان، وكذا لو سرق حنطة فطحنها، أو سويقاً فَلَته. قوله: فقطع؛ أي بعد الصبغ؛ فإن قطع أولاً ثمّ صبغه فهل يردّه أم لا؟ الذي في «الاختيار»: إنّه يردّه، وذكر في «البحر» وغيره: إنه لا يرد. قوله: يؤخذ؛ أي يأخذه المالك ويردّ على السارق ما زاد في قيمته بسبب الصبغ؛ فإنّ الصبغ يزيد في قيمة الثوب، لا سيما الصبغ الأحمر، وهذا كما إذا غصب ثوباً فصبغه أحمر، فإنه يؤخذ الثوبُ من الغاصب ويعطى له ما زاد الصبغ فيه، والسرّ فيه: إن الثوبَ أصل والصبغ تابع، فلا يمتنع أخذ الأصل بسبب التبع. أولهما: إن الصبغ قائم صورة ومعنى، وحق المالك في الثوب قائم صورة لا معنى لزوال التقوم بالقطع. (1) لأن عين ماله قائم من كل وجه وهو أصل والصبغ تبع فكان اعتبار الأصل أولى، ولهما أن الصبغ قائم صورة ومعنى وحق صاحب الثوب قائم صورة لا معنى لزوال التقوم بالقطع. ينظر: درر الحكام(٢: ٨٤).
(And if he reddens it and cuts, then no return and no guarantee, and if he blackens it, he returns): meaning if he steals a cloth and dyes it red and cuts, it is not obligatory to return the cloth, and if it perishes, there is no guarantee. And according to Muhammad, the cloth is taken and he is given the increase of the dye. (1) His statement: 'it is not obligatory to return them'; and is cutting obligatory according to them? It is said: no, because he owned it before, and it is said: yes, because it became something else by manufacture, so he did not own its substance. But according to his statement, the obligation of the hadd is apparent, because the thief does not own it, as in al-Hidayah. His statement: 'valuable according to them'; because by this manufacture the substance changes in name, ruling, and purpose, and everything that is like that, the owner's right terminates, as when a brass item is misappropriated and he beats it into a pot. And for him: the substance of the stolen item remains, and the new manufacture and the new name are not inseparable, because returning it to the original state is possible, and the basis of this disagreement is the disagreement in ghasb. His statement: 'if he reddens it'; past tense of reddening, meaning he steals a cloth and dyes it red and cuts, it is not obligatory to return the cloth nor any guarantee, and likewise if he steals wheat and grinds it, or sawiq and flattens it. His statement: 'and cuts'; meaning after dyeing; if he cuts first then dyes it, should he return it or not? What is in al-Ikhtiyar: he returns it, and in al-Bahr and others: he does not return. His statement: 'is taken'; meaning the owner takes it and gives back to the thief what increased in value due to the dye, for dye increases the value of the cloth, especially red dye. This is like if someone misappropriates a cloth and dyes it red, then the cloth is taken from the misappropriator and he is given the increase due to the dye. The secret is: the cloth is the principal and the dye is subsidiary, so taking the principal due to the subsidiary is not prevented. According to them (Sahibayn): the dye is present in form and meaning, and the owner's right in the cloth is present in form but not in meaning because the value is lost by cutting.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling when a thief dyes a stolen cloth red or black. The matn states that if he dyes it red and then cuts (presumably meaning he severs it from the loom or such), there is no return of the cloth and no guarantee (liability). If he dyes it black, he must return the cloth. The commentator explains the reasoning: for red dye, the change is so significant that the owner's right is lost, whereas black dye does not transform it as much. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Muhammad (al-Shaybani) holds that the cloth is taken by the owner and the thief is compensated for the increase in value due to the dye. The basis of the dispute is whether the dye is considered a subsidiary (tabi') that follows the principal (asl) or an independent valuable. For Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, in case of red dye, since the value is lost by cutting (theft), the thief does not have to return or compensate. For black dye, the cloth is returned because the change is less drastic. The author also draws an analogy from ghasb (misappropriation). Additional notes discuss the sequence: if the thief cuts first then dyes, opinions differ. The key principle is that the owner's right is not destroyed if the substance remains and the manufacturing is reversible.

Key Terms
تحمير (taḥmīr): Dyeing red, which is considered a major transformation that separates the item from its original state.
تبع (tabiʿ): Subsidiary element that follows the principal in terms of ruling, such as dye being subsidiary to the cloth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for a thief who dyes a stolen cloth black and cuts it, according to the matn?
A) No return and no guarantee
B) Return is obligatory
C) Return is obligatory with compensation
D) The thief is punished by amputation

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between transformative (red) and non-transformative (black) alterations to stolen property, with distinct consequences for return and liability.

Dyeing Stolen Garments: Red vs Black and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on dye's effect on stolen property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on a stolen garment dyed red or black, and explain the basis of their disagreement.

Core Idea
Red dye terminates the owner's right; black dye does not—unless custom says otherwise.

The Hanafi school has a rich internal discussion on whether dyeing a stolen garment changes ownership and liability. The base text gives one ruling for red and another for black, but the two students of Abu Hanifa disagree. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and the role of custom in determining increase versus deficiency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
What is the act? A thief steals a garment and dyes it either red or black, then cuts it (i.e., the theft is complete). The dispute: does the original owner retain the right to reclaim the garment, and is the thief liable?
The act of dyeing is the transformative action in question.
2
State the base text ruling and Abu Hanifa's position
According to the base text (and Abu Hanifa), if dyed red: no return, no guarantee. If dyed black: must return it. Note that for red, the thief gains full ownership; for black, the owner's right persists because black is a deficiency.
Abu Hanifa distinguishes between increase (ziyada) and deficiency (nuqsan).
3
Contrast with the Sahibayn's positions
Muhammad al-Shaybani agrees on black but says for red the owner may reclaim by paying the dye's increase. Abu Yusuf says no return for either color because both increase value. Understand their reasoning: the manufactured item (al-sina'ah) is valuable and transforms the object in name, ruling, and purpose.
The Sahibayn analogize to a usurped brass object beaten into a kettle.
4
Analyze the root of the dispute
The difference traces back to the chapter on usurpation (ghasp). Abu Hanifa argues the original substance remains and the change is reversible. The Sahibayn emphasize the transformation in value and purpose. The dispute over black being increase or deficiency is attributed to changing custom ('urf): people did not wear black in Abu Hanifa's time but did later.
This is a difference of zaman (time) not burhan (proof), according to the commentary.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
For red: under Abu Hanifa and the base text, the owner cannot reclaim; under Muhammad, the owner may reclaim by compensating for the dye. For black: under Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the owner reclaims without compensation; under Abu Yusuf, the owner cannot reclaim.
The mufta bihi is not explicitly stated, but the text leans towards the Sahibayn's view for red.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن حمره فقطع فلا ردّ ولا ضمان، وإن سوده ردّ. وأما عندهما لا يجب ردُّهما، لأن الصَّنعة متقومة عندهما فصارت شيئاً آخر. (فإن حمره فقطع فلا ردّ ولا ضمان، وإن سوده رد) : أي إن سرق ثوباً وصبغه أحمر فقطع لا يجب رد الثوب وإن هلك فلا ضمان. وعند محمد له يؤخذ الثوب، ويعطى ما زاد الصبغ. قوله: لا يجب ردّهما؛ وهل يجب القطع عندهما؟ فقيل: لا؛ لأنه ملكها قبله، وقيل: يجب؛ لأنه صار بالصنعة شيئاً آخر، فلم يملك عينه، وأما على قوله فوجوب الحد ظاهر؛ لأن السارق لا يملكه، كذا في الهداية. قوله: متقومة عندهما؛ لأن بهذه الصنعة يتبدل العين اسماً وحكماً ومقصوداً، وكل ما كان كذلك ينقطع به حق المالك، كما إذا كان المغصوب صفراً فضربه قمقمة. وله: إن عين المسروق باق والصنعة الحادثة والاسم الحادث ليسا ملازمين، فإن إعادتها إلى الحالة الأولى ممكنة، وأصل هذا الخلاف الخلاف في الغصب. قوله: فإن حمره؛ ماض من التحمير، أي سرق ثوباً فصبغه أحمر فقطع، لا يجب عليه رد الثوب ولا الضمان، وكذا لو سرق حنطة فطحنها، أو سويقاً فَلَته. قوله: فقطع؛ أي بعد الصبغ؛ فإن قطع أولاً ثم صبغه فهل يرده أم لا؟ الذي في الاختيار: إنه يرده، وذكر في البحر وغيره: إنه لا يرد. قوله: يؤخذ؛ أي يأخذه المالك ويرد على السارق ما زاد في قيمته بسبب الصبغ؛ فإن الصبغ يزيد في قيمة الثوب، لا سيما الصبغ الأحمر، وهذا كما إذا غصب ثوباً فصبغه أحمر، فإنه يؤخذ الثوب من الغاصب ويعطى له ما زاد الصبغ فيه، والسر فيه: إن الثوب أصل والصبغ تابع، فلا يمتنع أخذ الأصل بسبب التبع. ولهما: إن الصبغ قائم صورة ومعنى، وحق المالك في الثوب قائم صورة لا معنى لزوال التقوم بالقطع. وإن سوده رد عند أبي حنيفة؛ لكون السواد نقصاناً فلا ينقطع حق المالك، وكذا محمد كما في الحمرة، فإن الصبغ لا يقطع حق المالك. وعند أبي يوسف لا يرد فإن السواد زيادة كالحمرة، فيقطع حق المالك. معنى، ومراعاة ما هو قائم من كل وجه أولى من مراعاة ما هو قائم من وجه، فلذلك رجحنا جانب السارق وقلنا بعدم الرد وعدم الضمان، كذا في الهداية والبناية. قوله: فلا ينقطع؛ فإن حقه إنما ينقطع بالزيادة لا بالنقصان. قوله: زيادة كالحمرة؛ وكذا عند محمد له، فإنه يزيد في قيمة الثوب، والاختلاف بينهما وبينه في كون السواد زيادة كالحمرة أو نقصاناً اختلاف زمان لا اختلاف برهان، فإن الناس كانوا لا يلبسون الأسود في زمانه، وشاع لبسه في زمانهما، كذا في الفتح.
If he dyes it red and cuts it, there is no return (of the garment) and no guarantee (if it perishes). If he dyes it black, he must return it. As for the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), they say: It is not obligatory to return them (i.e., the dyed garments), because the manufactured item is considered valuable (muttaqawwimah) in their view, so it has become something else. (The phrase: 'If he dyes it red and cuts it, there is no return and no guarantee, and if he dyes it black, he returns it') means: If he steals a garment and dyes it red and then cuts it, it is not obligatory to return the garment, and if it perishes there is no guarantee. According to Muhammad, the owner may take the garment and pay the increase of the dye. His statement: 'It is not obligatory to return them' – and is the cutting (of the hand) obligatory according to the two? It is said: No, because he (the thief) owned it before (the dye?); and it is said: Yes, because it became something else through the manufacture, so he does not own the same thing. As for the other view (Abu Hanifa's), the obligation of the cutting (hadd) is apparent because the thief does not own it. This is in al-Hidayah. His statement: 'Mutattaawwimah in their view' – because through this manufacture the object changes in name, ruling, and purpose, and everything that is like that, the right of the owner is terminated, like if the usurped object was brass and he beat it into a kettle. And his (Abu Hanifa's) view: The substance of the stolen object remains, and the newly created manufacture and the new name are not inseparable (lazim), because its return to the original state is possible, and the root of this dispute is the dispute in usurpation (ghasp). His statement: 'If he dyes it red' – it is the past tense of tahmir, meaning he stole a garment and dyed it red and cut it; it is not obligatory upon him to return the garment nor the guarantee, and similarly if he stole wheat and ground it, or saweeq and fried it. His statement: 'and cuts it' – i.e., after dyeing; if he cuts first and then dyes it, is he to return it or not? In al-Ikhtiyar it is said he returns it, and it is mentioned in al-Bahr and others that he does not. His statement: 'is taken' – i.e., the owner takes it and gives to the thief the increase in its value due to the dye; for the dye increases the value of the garment, especially red dye, and this just as if he usurped a garment and dyed it red, then the garment is taken from the usurper and he is given the increase of the dye. The secret is that the garment is the original and the dye is subsidiary, so taking the original due to the subsidiary is not prevented. And their (the two's view): The dye is present in form and meaning, and the right of the owner in the garment is present in form but not in meaning due to the loss of value (taqawwum) by the cutting. And if he dyes it black, he must return it according to Abu Hanifa, because black is a deficiency (nuqsan) so the right of the owner is not terminated; and similarly according to Muhammad as for red, for the dye does not terminate the owner's right. According to Abu Yusuf, he does not return it, for black is an increase like red, so it terminates the owner's right. Meaning, considering what exists in every respect is more appropriate than considering what exists in one respect. Therefore we gave preponderance to the thief's side and said no return and no guarantee, as in al-Hidayah and al-Binayah. His statement: 'Then it is not terminated' – for his right is only terminated by increase, not deficiency. His statement: 'An increase like red' – and similarly according to Muhammad, it increases the value of the garment, and the difference between them and him (i.e., Abu Hanifa) concerning whether black is an increase like red or a deficiency is a difference of time, not a difference of proof; for people did not wear black in his time (Abu Hanifa's), but wearing it became widespread in their time (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's). This is in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the effect of dyeing a stolen garment on the owner's right to reclaim it and the thief's liability. The base text states: if the stolen garment is dyed red and then cut, the thief is neither required to return it nor liable for compensation; if dyed black, he must return it. The commentary expands on this, explaining the reasoning of the three major Hanafi imams. The dispute centers on whether the dye (especially black) increases or decreases the value and whether it transforms the object into something else. Abu Hanifa holds that red dye increases value and thus terminates the owner's right, while black dye decreases value (being a deficiency) so the right remains. Muhammad al-Shaybani agrees with Abu Hanifa on black but differs on red: the owner may reclaim the red-dyed garment by paying the increase in value. Abu Yusuf considers black an increase like red, so he rules no return for either color. The commentary mentions that the difference in opinion on black is due to a change in societal custom—black was not worn in Abu Hanifa's time but became popular later—so it is a difference of time not proof. The passage also touches on the cutting of the thief's hand, with some saying it is obligatory because the thief never owns the object, while others say it is not because he owned it through transformation. The root of the dispute is analogized to usupation (ghasp) where similar differences exist. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated, but the text leans toward no return for red following the Sahibayn's view.

Key Terms
الصبغ (al-sabgh): The dye applied to a stolen garment, specifically red or black, which changes its appearance and value.
الصنعة (al-sina'ah): The act of manufacturing or processing an object, such as dyeing, which may transform it legally.
النقصان (al-nuqsan): Deficiency; in this context, black dye is considered a deficiency that does not increase the garment's value, so the owner's right persists.
الزيادة (al-ziyada): Increase; red dye (and black according to Abu Yusuf) is considered an increase in value, terminating the owner's right to reclaim the original garment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The thief must return it because black is a deficiency.
B) The thief must not return it, but pay compensation.
C) The thief must not return it and has no liability.
D) The thief must return it with compensation for the dye.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the effect of dyeing a stolen garment on the owner's right depends on whether the dye is considered an increase or deficiency, which can vary with custom.

Black Dye on Stolen Garments: Increase or Decrease?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on effect of dyeing stolen garment black

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on dyeing stolen garments black, explain why the ruling depends on custom (urf), and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Whether black dye is an increase or decrease depends on custom, not fixed proof.

This passage addresses a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: does the owner's right to reclaim a stolen garment end when the thief dyes it black? Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad say no (decrease), while Abu Yusuf says yes (increase). The difference turns on changing customs—what was a decrease in one era became an increase later. This lesson explores the reasoning and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions within the Hanafi school
Summarize the view of Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad (black dye is a decrease, garment returned) and that of Abu Yusuf (black dye is an increase, thief keeps it).
2
Determine the key point of contention
The core issue is whether black dye constitutes an increase (ziyada) or a decrease (nuqsan) in value, which determines if the owner's right is cut off.
3
Analyze the evidence each side uses
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rely on the custom of their time where black reduced value. Abu Yusuf argues black dye adds value like red dye (agreed to be an increase), and he notes that custom has changed to favor black.
The commentator states the difference is one of time, not of proof (ikhtilāf zamān lā ikhtilāf burhān).
4
Understand the role of custom (urf)
The ruling is not static; it adapts to the prevalent custom. In Abu Hanifa's era black was not commonly worn, so it decreased value. Later, black became fashionable, thus an increase.
5
Identify the mufta bihi and its basis
Later authorities (Al-Hidayah, Fath al-Qadir) prefer Abu Yusuf's view. The principle 'observing what is established from every aspect is better than from one aspect' is cited to favor the thief's side.
The mufta bihi is: no return, no guarantee.

[2] قوله : زيادة كالحمرة ؛ وكذا عند محمد رحمه الله، فإنه يزيد في قيمة الثوب والاختلاف بينهما وبينه في كون السواد زيادة كالحمرة، أو نقصاناً اختلاف زمان لا اختلاف برهان، فإن الناس كانوا لا يلبسون الأسود في زمانه، وشاع لبسه في زمانهما، كذا في الفتح.
The manner of cutting and its proof, Book of Theft, Section. If he blackened it, it is returned according to Abu Hanifa because blackness is a decrease, so the owner's right is not terminated; and likewise Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, as in the case of redness, for dye does not terminate the owner's right. According to Abu Yusuf, it is not returned because blackness is an increase like redness, so it terminates the owner's right. Meaning, observing what is established from every aspect is more appropriate than observing what is established from one aspect, therefore we favored the side of the thief and said no return and no guarantee, as in Al-Hidayah and Al-Binayah. [1] His statement: so it is not terminated; for his right is only terminated by increase not by decrease. [2] His statement: increase like redness; and likewise according to Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, it increases the value of the garment, and the difference between them (Abu Hanifa) and him (Abu Yusuf) regarding whether blackness is an increase like redness or a decrease is a difference of time not a difference of proof, for people did not wear black in his time, and wearing it became common in their time, as in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of dyeing a stolen garment. The central issue: does the owner's right to reclaim the garment terminate when the thief dyes it? The ruling depends on whether the dye is considered an increase (ziyada) or a decrease (nuqsan) in value. Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad hold that black dye is a decrease, thus the owner's right remains and the garment must be returned. Abu Yusuf argues that black dye is an increase like red dye, thus the owner's right terminates and the thief keeps the garment with no compensation. The commentator explains that the preferred (mufta bihi) view is that of Abu Yusuf, because it takes into account the complete picture: the thief's right to the increase and the fact that black dye adds value. The difference between the early and later scholars is attributed to changing customs: in Abu Hanifa's time black was not commonly worn, so it decreased value; later it became fashionable, increasing value. Thus, the ruling adapts to custom (urf).

Key Terms
سواد (sawād): Blackness; the black dye applied to a stolen garment by the thief.
زيادة (ziyāda): Increase; an addition in value that terminates the original owner's right to reclaim the garment.
نقصان (nuqsān): Decrease; a reduction in value that does not terminate the owner's right, so the garment must be returned.
قطع حق المالك (qaṭ‘ ḥaqq al-mālik): Termination of the owner's right to reclaim the stolen property; the thief becomes the owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Highway Robbery: Definition and Conditions for Hadd
Defines highway robbery and lists six conditions for hadd punishment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of highway robbery (qat' al-tariq) and list the six conditions required for the hadd punishment.

Core Idea
Highway robbery is a distinct crime with six strict conditions for the hadd.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi definition of highway robbery (qat' al-tariq), also known as 'the greater theft'. The passage from a commentary on al-Hidayah clarifies the linguistic meaning and then enumerates six precise conditions that must all be present for the hadd punishment to apply. Understanding these conditions is crucial for distinguishing highway robbery from ordinary theft and for correctly applying Islamic criminal law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Legal Category
First, note that highway robbery is a distinct crime from ordinary theft. It is called 'the greater theft' (al-sariqa al-kubra) because it involves overpowering victims on the road. In Hanafi law, it falls under the chapter of hudud (fixed punishments).
The commentary explains that ordinary theft was discussed first because it is more common.
2
List the Six Conditions for Hadd
The passage from al-Binaya lists six conditions that must all be met for the hadd to be obligatory. Memorize them in order: (1) power and strength of the robbers, (2) outside the city, (3) in Dar al-Islam, (4) stolen property reaches nisab, (5) all robbers are strangers to the victims (non-mahram), (6) they are captured before repenting.
Each condition has a rationale; e.g., condition 5 ensures no family relation that might reduce culpability.
3
Understand the Subject and Object
The hadd applies only to a mukallaf (responsible adult) who intentionally commits the robbery. It applies equally to Muslims and dhimmis, men and women. The victim must be ma'sum (protected) – i.e., a Muslim or dhimmi. A musta'min (temporary safe-passage holder) is not subject to hadd because he is not obligated by Islamic laws.
This differentiation shows the importance of 'isma' (inviolability) in determining liability.
4
Note Exceptions and General Rulings
If any condition is missing, the hadd is not applied. For example, if the robbery occurs inside the city, it is treated as ordinary theft. If the robbers repent before capture, the hadd is dropped. If a mahram is among the robbers, the hadd is not applied to any of them.
These exceptions highlight the strictness of hudud punishments – they are not applied if there is any doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب قطع الطريق (من قصده معصوماً)
قوله : باب قطع الطريق ؛ معنى هذا اللفظ قطعُ المارة والمسافرين عن الطريق ، فيكون من باب الحذف والإيصال ، وقيل : المراد بالطريق المارة من قبيل إطلاق المحل على الحال.
وقيل : الإضافة بمعنى: «في» ؛ أي قطع في الطريق ، وهذا يقال له السرقة الكبرى، ولما كانت السرقة الصغرى المذكورة في كتاب السرقة أكثر وقوعاً بالنسبة إلى قطع الطريق قدم بحثها على بحثه.
قال في «البناية»: اعلم لقطع الطريق شرائط :
الأول : أن يكون لهم شوكة وقوة بحيث لا يمكن للمارة المقاومة معهم.
الثاني: أن يكون خارج المصر بعيدا عنه.
الثالث : أن يكون في دار الإسلام. الرابع : أن يكون المأخوذ قدر النصاب.
الخامس : أن يكون القطاع كلّهم أجانب في حق أصحاب الأموال، حتى إذا كان فيهم ذو رحم محرم لا يجب القطع.
السادس : أن يؤخذوا قبل التوبة.
قوله : من قصده ؛ أي قطع الطريق، وفي الإطلاق إشارة إلى عموم الحكم فيها إذا كان قطع الطريق من جماعة ، وفيما إذا كان من واحد له شوكة، والمراد به المكلف، فإن القاطع الصبي أو المجنون لا حدّ عليه، وفي الإطلاق إشارة أيضاً إلى عموم الحكم للمسلم والذمي، وللرجل والمرأة.
قوله : معصوماً ؛ العصمة : الحفظ، والمراد كونه محفوظ الدم والمال بالإسلام أو عقد الذمة، فلو قطع الطريق مستأمن لا يحدّ ؛ لأنه غير مخاطب بالشرائع.
Chapter of Highway Robbery (from one who intends a protected person).
His statement: 'Chapter of Highway Robbery'; the meaning of this expression is cutting off travelers and wayfarers from the road, so it is of the category of deletion and delivery. And it is said: what is meant by 'the road' is the travelers, as a metonymy of the place for the occupant. And it is said: the genitive construction is in the sense of 'in', i.e., cutting off on the road. And this is called the greater theft. Since the lesser theft mentioned in the Book of Theft occurs more frequently compared to highway robbery, its discussion was placed before the discussion of this.
(1) He said in al-Binaya: Know that highway robbery has conditions:
First: That they have such power and strength that travelers cannot resist them.
Second: That it be outside the city, far from it.
Third: That it be in the Abode of Islam.
Fourth: That what is taken be of the amount of nisab.
Fifth: That the robbers all be strangers with respect to the owners of the property, so that if among them there is a mahram relative, cutting off (the hand) is not obligatory.
Sixth: That they be captured before repentance.
His statement: 'from one who intends' i.e., highway robbery. And in the absolute expression there is an indication of the generality of the ruling whether the highway robbery is by a group or by a single person who has power. And what is meant is the responsible person, for if the robber is a minor or insane, there is no hadd punishment upon him. And in the absolute expression there is also an indication of the generality of the ruling for the Muslim and the dhimmi, and for the man and the woman.
His statement: 'protected'; al-isma means preservation, and what is meant is that his blood and property are preserved by Islam or by the contract of dhimma. So if a musta'min (foreigner granted safe passage) commits highway robbery, he is not punished with hadd because he is not obligated by the laws.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defining the chapter on highway robbery (qat' al-tariq) and outlining the conditions for the hadd punishment to apply. The author begins by explaining the term linguistically: it literally means cutting off travellers from the road. Some say it is a metonymy where 'road' stands for the travellers, others interpret the genitive construction as meaning 'cutting off on the road'. He notes that this is called 'the greater theft' (al-sariqa al-kubra), distinct from ordinary theft which is covered in the previous book. The commentary then quotes al-Binaya listing six conditions: (1) the robbers must have power and strength to overpower travellers; (2) the robbery must occur outside the city; (3) within Dar al-Islam; (4) the stolen property reaches the nisab threshold for theft; (5) all robbers are strangers (non-mahram) to the victims – if a mahram relative participates, the hadd is not applied; (6) the robbers are captured before they repent. Next, the author explains the phrase 'from one who intends' indicating that the ruling applies whether the robbery is by a group or a single strong person, and only applies to a responsible (mukallaf) person – a minor or insane person incurs no hadd. It also applies equally to Muslims and dhimmis, men and women. Finally, 'protected' (ma'sum) means the victim's blood and property are inviolable due to Islam or dhimma. A musta'min (foreigner with safe passage) who commits highway robbery is not subject to hadd because he is not obligated by Islamic laws.

Key Terms
قطع الطريق (qat' al-tariq): Highway robbery: forcibly taking property from travellers on the road with power and strength.
عصمة (isma): Protection; the inviolability of a person's life and property due to Islam or a dhimma contract.
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim from a hostile territory who has been granted temporary safe passage (aman).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a condition for the hadd of highway robbery in the Hanafi school?
A) The robbers must be from the victim's own family
B) The robbery must occur outside the city
C) The stolen property must be less than the nisab
D) The robbers must repent before being captured

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the hadd for highway robbery requires six strict conditions to be met simultaneously; missing any one nullifies the hadd.

Definition and General Rulings of Highway Robbery
Defines the chapter and lists general applicability conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qat' al-tariq and state the general rulings regarding the perpetrator and victim.

Core Idea
Highway robbery (qat' al-tariq) is grand theft by force on a public road; its hadd applies only against a protected person (ma'sum) by a mukallaf.

This lesson opens the chapter on highway robbery. We first clarify the linguistic meaning of qat' al-tariq and distinguish it from ordinary theft. Then we examine the general applicability of the hadd: it applies to groups or individuals with power, to responsible adults (mukallaf), and equally to Muslims and dhimmis, men and women. We also learn who is considered a protected person (ma'sum) and the case of the musta'min who is not subject to hadd but only ta'zir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and its definition
Start with the term 'qat' al-tariq' – it means cutting off travelers from the road. Note the linguistic possibilities: the idafah may imply cutting 'on' the road. This is called grand theft (al-sariqah al-kubra) as opposed to ordinary theft.
The author explains why ordinary theft chapter is placed before this one: more frequent.
2
Determine the scope of the hadd regarding the perpetrator
The hadd applies whether the act is done by a group with shawkah or an individual with power. The perpetrator must be a mukallaf (adult, sane); minors and insane persons are exempt. The ruling is general for Muslim, dhimmi, male, and female.
The phrase 'man qasadahu' indicates generality.
3
Identify the protected status of the victim
The victim must be ma'sum – protected in blood and property by Islam or a dhimmi pact. A musta'min (temporary safe conduct holder) is not ma'sum for hadd purposes; if a musta'min commits highway robbery, he is not subject to hadd but only ta'zir because he is not addressed by the laws that impose the hadd.
Ta'zir is discretionary punishment.
4
Summarize the general rulings from the passage
The hadd of highway robbery applies when all conditions are met. The ruling is general for perpetrators (Muslim/dhimmi, male/female) but limited by the victim's protected status. The musta'min perpetrator receives ta'zir, not hadd.
The conditions for hadd are detailed in the next lesson.

[1] His statement: 'Chapter of Highway Robbery'; the meaning of this phrase is cutting off passers-by and travelers from the road. So it is from the category of deletion and connection. And it is said that the intended meaning by 'road' is the passers-by, as a metonymy of place for the object. And it is said that the idafah (construct) has the meaning of 'fi' (in), i.e., cutting on the road. This is called 'grand theft'. Commentary
This passage is a definitional ruling that opens the chapter on highway robbery (qat' al-tariq) in the text of al-Wiqayah. The commentator first clarifies the linguistic meaning of the term: it is the act of cutting off travelers from the road, and the grammatical construction (idafah) may imply cutting 'on' the road. This is termed 'grand theft' (al-sariqah al-kubra) as opposed to ordinary theft (al-sariqah al-sughra). The author explains why the chapter on ordinary theft is presented first: it is more common. He then lists six conditions for the hadd penalty from the work al-Binayah: (1) the robbers must have such strength that travelers cannot resist; (2) the act must occur outside the city, far from it; (3) in Dar al-Islam; (4) the property taken must reach the nisab threshold; (5) all robbers must be strangers to the victims (i.e., no mahram relative among them, as that would preclude the hadd); (6) the robbers must be apprehended before repenting (i.e., before they voluntarily repent, the hadd applies; after repentance, the penalty may be lifted according to some interpretations). The commentator then explains the phrase 'from one who intends upon a protected person' (man qasadahu ma'suman). The generality of the ruling applies whether the robber is a group or an individual with power. The person subject to hadd must be a mukallaf (adult, sane); minors and insane persons are exempt from hadd. The ruling applies equally to Muslims and dhimmis, men and women. The 'protected person' is one whose life and property are inviolable by virtue of Islam or a dhimmi pact. A musta'min (a non-Muslim with temporary safe conduct) is not subject to hadd if he commits highway robbery because he is not addressed by the laws (shara'i') that impose the hadd; however, he is subject to ta'zir (discretionary punishment).

Key Terms
قطع الطريق (qat' al-tariq): Highway robbery: forcefully taking property from travelers on the road with a show of strength.
عصمة (isma): Inviolability of blood and property, established by Islam or dhimmi pact.
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim who enters Dar al-Islam under a safe conduct (aman) for a limited period.

D) The robbers must be caught before repentance.

Key Takeaway
Highway robbery is defined by force and location; the hadd applies only to mukallaf perpetrators against ma'sum victims, with musta'min receiving ta'zir.

The Six Conditions for Hadd of Highway Robbery
Lists the six conditions for the hadd of highway robbery from al-Binayah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the six Hanafi conditions for the hadd of highway robbery as given in al-Binayah.

Core Idea
Six conditions must be met for the hadd of highway robbery: strength, location, jurisdiction, nisab, stranger status, and apprehension before repentance.

In this lesson, we examine the six specific conditions (shurut) that must be present for the hadd of highway robbery to apply. These conditions, cited from al-Binayah, narrow the scope of the penalty significantly. Each condition has a legal rationale that distinguishes highway robbery from ordinary theft or other crimes. Understanding these conditions is essential for correctly applying the hadd.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the condition of shawkah (strength)
The robbers must have such power that travelers cannot resist. This distinguishes highway robbery from petty theft or snatching, where the thief might be overpowered. If the victim could easily resist, it is not considered highway robbery.
This condition emphasizes the element of overpowering force.
2
Examine the location condition: outside the city
The act must occur outside the city, far from it. Inside the city, the crime is treated as ordinary theft or hirabah with different rules, because the victim could potentially seek help.
Some scholars discuss the exact distance; the general principle is isolation.
3
Consider the jurisdiction: Dar al-Islam
The hadd only applies within Islamic territory because the penal system of Islam has jurisdiction there. In Dar al-Harb, other rules apply (e.g., the spoils of war).
This ensures the sovereignty of Islamic law.
4
Evaluate the nisab requirement
The property taken must reach the nisab threshold for theft (as defined in the book of theft, e.g., 10 dirhams). This ensures the severity of the crime warrants the extreme penalty.
Nisab is the minimum value for which the hand amputation applies in theft; the same threshold is used for highway robbery.
5
Understand the stranger condition (ajanib)
All robbers must be strangers to the owners of the property, meaning no mahram relative among them. If any robber is a mahram, the hadd is dropped because the relationship creates a legal ambiguity (shubhah) regarding the prohibition.
This is based on the principle that doubts (shubuhat) prevent hadd punishments.
6
Note the condition of being caught before repentance
The robbers must be apprehended before they voluntarily repent. If they repent before capture, the hadd may be lifted (according to some interpretations). This encourages repentance and reflects the Quranic verse (5:33-34) which offers a path to forgiveness.
This condition is debated among scholars; the Hanafi position is that if they repent before being caught, their repentance is accepted and they are not subject to hadd.
